THE BED PAGE.
The University of Sydney.
A UNIVERSITY is not to be finally judged
by its infancy. But as in the human
species from the tendencies shown in
early childhood, from the readiness or
the failure of the young mind to respond to
the calls made upon it by its environment, some
forecast can be made of manhood; so from the state
of a university which is as yet only 50 years old,
there can be deduced some reasonable ideas of its
probable development in the future. With this
reservation —that as in the child a waywardness
of temper, perceived early, may be checked and
finally eradicated by kindly discipline, or a dull-
ness of understanding corrected by pi’oper train-
ing, so a university may be turned from a wrong
path, or may be forced out of a lazy stagnation, if
the faults are seen and the fitting remedies used
before wrong has become a habit and sloth has
turned to infirmity. The jubilee of Sydney Uni-
versity, just celebrated, when, crowing lustily,
the infant institution brought the deeds of its
little space of 50 years for public judgment, raises
directly the- question whether it is on the path
which leads to national usefulness.
“University” is a bold word. It is in itself
sufficient reply to those who look upon a univer-
sity merely as a body entitled to grind out
degrees, to make doctors or engineers, and law-
yers and bachelors of arts, to examine and to give
diplomas. Many so-called “ universities ” claim no
other function ; but misuse of a word does not
change its meaning. The true university attractsas
to a focus, the choicest spirits of a nation or of a
city, gives them bv study and by discussion the
opportunity of attaining to some knowledge of the
universal and to that state of mind which is intelli-
gent and tolerant. Necessarily a true university
must sum up and express the national spirit of the
people among whom it is placed. Whilst pure
knowledge knows no boundaries and no races, a
very essential condition of its increase and im-
provement is that there should, in its method of
pursuit, be a tinge of national feeling. There is a
very definite distinction between the European
intellect and the Asiatic intellect, and, subdivid-
ing further, between the French intellect—elegant,
critical, subtle, daring ; and the German intellect—
laboriously inquisitive, conscientious and scienti-
fic ; the English intellect—comprehensive, dogma-
tic, conservative ; and the Scottish intellect —stub-
born, fanatical, logical and minute. A university
should reflect the intellectual temperament of its
country. But it should be, nevertheless, abso-
lutely cosmopolitan, holding no barrier of race
against students or against ideas. The “academies”
of the ancient philosophers providing, as they did,
the germ of the modern university, suggested
also what should be its leading features—a ruling
bent of mind constituting the bond of govern-
ment, a brotherhood of students, and ah ab-
solute freedom of discussion. A university
must have an individuality, a “ spirit ’’ of its
own ; there should be certain broad principles
on which, as a body, it is agreed ; it should
have a fraternal organisation embracing students,
graduates and teachers; it should welcome
and discuss with the utmost possible freedom
all new ideas. With the more defined statement
of the first point that, as an Australian institution,
Sydney University should be Australian! in its
individuality, just as Oxford is English and Aber-
deen is Scottish, then all is stated of what Sydney
University should be. It is a mostly Scottish insti-
tution, with no individuality and no spirit except
what is embraced in a sordid commercialism, and
an almost savage intolerance of opinion.
The Scots are a great people, to whose kindness
in managing the bulk of their affairs the English
should be profoundly grateful; and Australia
has, on the whole, no cause to be dissatisfied
because Scotchmen control so many of her mines,
banks, Parliaments, newspapers, and other enter-
prises. But in matters of thought as distinguished
from matters of business, the Scottish intellect is
still so deeply tainted with the bigoted intolerance
of the 16th and 17th centuries, and with a clannish-
ness which, though admirable in some of its
manifestations, tends to strangle freedom of
thought and tolerance of speculation, that a
Scotch-run degree-shop is about the worst de-
parture possible from the ideal of an Australian
University—unless, perhaps, an Irish-run factory
for diplomas and blather.
J*
At its inception Sydney University was in-
tended, in the words of Wentworth, to be “an
institution for the poor,” “ to enlighten the mind,
refine the understanding, and to elevate their
fellow men.” It was to be “ a pregnant womb from
which would arise a long list of illustrious names of
statesmen, of patriots, of philosophers, of philan-
thropists who would shed a deathless halo, not
only on their country, but on that University
which called them into being.” This somewhat
turgid eloquence probably conveyed the'true hopes
of the founders of Sydney University. It cannot
be urged that either the State or private indivi-
duals showed a niggard spirit in supporting the
institution. A huge grant of land, a noble subsidy
to the cost of its buildings, frequent special gifts,
an annual grant (reaching this year to £16,200),
generous concessions in regard to travelling fees—
these represent the State’s support. By the
time the University of Sydney had produced its
first graduate, the late Mr. Justice Windeyer, it
was calculated that it had cost the State £lOO,OOO,
and in its Jubilee year the debit of the University
toN.S.W., including the value of its land endow-
ment, reaches well into the second million.
Private benefactions were not less liberal. The
Challis bequest was £200,000, the P. N. Russell
gift £50,000, and there has been a multitude of
smaller endowments. The object of the univer-
sity was from the first clearly expressed ; money
was from the first generously supplied to fulfil
that object. And the result? The “institution
for the poor” has become one of the great
strongholds of the snobbishness and the inso-
lence of the rich, a place from which the
poor man’s son is barred by almost insuper-
able obstacles, an institution many of whose
members seek- to treat as of inferior caste the
State school teachers for whose training in the Arts
course the Government pays. There isa conspicuous
lack of that enlightenment of the mind, refine-
ment of the understanding, and effort for the eleva-
tion of humanity which Mr. Wentworth in his
optimism predicted. But there is observable much
bigoted intolerance of opinion and narrowness of
view, much hateful religious sectarianism, much
degradation of the mind to snobbish and racial
prejudice. The worship of wealth, the eager
pursuit of contemptible titular distinctions, the
suppression of mental freedom —these are the
chief manifestations of the university spirit in
Sydney.
J*
In other lands, universities have been, as the
occasion demanded, conservative guardians of the
good which was threatened, or radical rebels
against the established evil which had become
intolerable. In Russia the university spirit is
revolutionary, in England it has been at times
radical, but more often conservative. Always
it has been of benefit. No sensible person would
suggest that a university should be the nursery
of a political cult, should be, for example, always
radical. But what is demanded of it, if it is to
fulfil its true functions, is that it should give
expression to the progressive spirit of new thought,
even when it hesitantly weighs in the balance
proposed innovation. It should be the intellectual
and in a sense the political Supreme Court of
the people, eagerly welcoming for discussion
every new thought and new opinion, but deliber-
ate in committing itself to any movement. Cer-
tainly it must have a “spirit,” an intellectual
view, a purpose, an influence on the thought
of the community. Sydney University has none
of these. It is a school, with some very good
and some very poor masters, some graduates of
distinction, some pupils of promise, but with
absolutely no influence on the thought of the
community, no power of light or leading, no appar-
ent purpose in life but to carry on miserable
squabbles and intrigues fitted only for suburban
back-yards ; to confer degrees ; to hunt for titles
and social distinctions ; and to make money.
The constitution of Sydney University is typi-
cal of its lack of spirit and character. The Senate
(the Upper House, so to speak, of its Parliament)—
a body dominated by a spirit of hard Scotch com-
mercialism—governs the institution without the
slightest reference to Convocation (in a sense the
Lower House of its Parliament). Convocation is
not called together regularly ; when it meets, on
rare occasions, some scores of strangers face one
another, drivel for a while, and separate ; they
do not form a corporate entity ; they have less
homogeneousness than a chance public meeting of
citizens. When the task falls on Convocation to
elect a senator, the vote is taken in. Sydney, and
all country members are practically debarred from
the franchise. Everything is done that can be done
to impress the fact that the university is not a
University, that, after giving its degrees, it wants
to hear and know as little as possible about its
graduates. That is necessary to the reign of the
clique. If the university of Sydney were to be
true to the spirit of university life, it would, in
time, reflect the spirit of New South Wales, not
of the Edinburgh medical sohool and the flunkeys’
parlor of Windsor Castle.
Jt
A conspicuous advertisement of the narrow in-
tolerance of the governing body of Sydney Uni-
versity was shown in this year of jubilee, when its
History professor, Mr, Wood, dared to fulfil one of
the true duties of his position by giving expression
to a conscientious but, at the time, unpopular
opinion on a great problem of national conscience.
He was treated by the dominant faction with
harsh, splenetic rudeness, and would, it is said,
have been summarily dismissed if the Government
of the State had not, at the instigation of the
Labor Party, conveyed a hint as to the unwisdom
of such a step. It was the representatives of the
“horny-handed” who taught Sydney University
to keep its bigoted intolerance of freedom of
thought within such bounds as to prevent the in-
stitution being made openly contemptible in the
eyes of the world. That event of the jubilee year
is not to be marked with a white stone.
J*
All this notwithstanding, Sydney University
need not be set down as hopeless. It is possible
of regeneration. The incident just referred to
showed a healthy under-current of feeling against
the tyrannical bigotry of the reigning Wack-
ford Squeerses among the students: their
championship of free speech was resolute
and generous. Among the teaching staff, too,
are men whose liberality of thought is as
unquestionable as their intellectual attainments.
The institution suffers from bad government, not
from inherent rottenness. With a change of the
system, a breaking-down of clique rule, and time
there is hope. The difficulty is in suggesting
means of reform. The Labor party —though its
interference has on one occasion been useful—is
hardly the fitting body to undertake the task.
And from what other power in politics can action
be expected ?
Adelaide has just now two art exhibitions.
One is the Photographic Society’s annual show
(N.S.W. and Queensland not represented this
year, Victoria being the only visitor). Tricks of
the camera seem to be barred —such as the
astonishing one which last year showed the Ophir
at anchor on the Torrens Lake, just below Parlia-
ment House ! Prizes mostly go to prints that
have been touched up and blurred and made to
look like sepia or other work. The other exhi-
bition is one got up locally by Unley, the subur-
ban district which returns to Parliament the
Premier and the head of the Opposition and the
leader of the Labor Party. It contains an “ auto-
type by Rembrandt ” (so the papers say), but is
lifted above mediocrity only by a wonderful
tapestry, designed by Burne-Jones and worked by
William Morris. This is lent by George Brook-
man, the Kalgoorlie semi-millionaire.
J*
Disraeli, the Oriental gentleman with a vel-
veteen-breeches idea of running an Empire, was,
though the fact is not generally known, a “ poet ”
as well as a novelist and an acute flatterer of
elderly ladies. “G. S., ”in London Daily Chronicle,
revives the memory of “The Revolutionary
Epick, The Work of Disraeli the Younger, author
of ‘ The Psychological Romance.’ ” The poetry is
very poor stuff, tawdry tinselled fustian, not quite
bad enough to be comical. But Disraeli’s preface
emphatically is humorous:
It was on the plains of Troy that I first conceived the
idea of this work. Wandering over that illustrious scene,
surrounded by the tombs of heroes and by the confluence
of poetic streams, my musing thoughts clustered round
the memory of that immortal song, to which all creeds
and countries alike respond, which has vanquished
chance, and defies Time. Deeming myself, perchance too
rashly, in that excited hour, a Poet, 1 cursed the destiny
that had played me in an age that boasted of being anti-
poetical. And while .my Fancy thus struggled with my
Reason, it Hashed across my mind, like the lightning
which was then playing over Ida, that in those great
poems which rise, the pyramids of poetic art, arhid
the falling and the fading' splendor of less crea-
tions, the Poet hath ever embodied the spirit
of his Time. Thus, the most heroic incident of
an heroic age produced in the Iliad an Heroic
Epick ; thus the consolidation of the most superb of
Empires, produced in the .®neid a Political Epick ; the
revival of Learning, and the birth of vernacular Genius,
presented us in the Divine Comedy' with a National
Epick ; dnd the Reformation and its consequences called
from the rapt lyre of Milton a Religious Epick.
And the spirit of my Time, shall it alone be uncele-
brated ?
Standing upon Asia, and gazing upon Europe,, with the
broad Hellespont alone between us, and the shadow of
Night descending on the mountains, these mighty conti-
nents appeared to me, as it were, the Rival Principles-of
Government, that at present contend for the mastery of
the world. “What!” I exclaimed, “is the Revolution
of France a less important event than the siege of Troy ?
Is Napoleon a less interesting character than Achilles ?
For me remains the Revolutionary Epick.”
Me and Homer and Virgil and Milton ! The
preface throws a fresh ray of light on the man
who was' the ancestor in politics of Chamberlain,
in literature of Marie Corelli.
Writers and their Ways.
WHAT a man writes is, of course, the
first thing. How he writes comes
second in the list. It is all very well
to say that we do not care whence the
jewel came as long as the jewel is a real jewel,
and is ours to have and hold. But we do care.
I remember well the time when I could not look
at a picture, or hear an air played, or read a poem
without making for myself a mental portrait of
the author—painter, musician, poet. But, of
oourse, these portraits were suggested by ideal
pictures I had seen in magazines, or elsewhere.
The figures—which, as I know now, had
nothing human in them —had large, deep,
dreamy eyes and long hair, and pale,
chiselled features. It would have been to me
sheer blasphemy in those days to imagine a Fat
Poet. I have seen poets enough since then, and,
God knows, not one of them was fat. I have met
musicians—one or two —who were stout, but I
cannot call to mind a Fat Painter. Australian
Art of any sort does not run to adiposity. I used
»to think that baldness was a sign of the end of
Romance. I know now that it is only the
beginning. What a number of bald headed, grey-
headed, streaky-grey-headed, ginger-and-silver-
headed young fellows I know ! Romeos every
man of them.
* * * *
I met Marcus Clarke once. It was in Ilenning-
ham’s Hotel, in Swanston-street, Melbourne,
opposite the Public Library. Somebody whose
name I have forgotten introduced me, and said
with pompous sarcasm that I was a young aspirant
to literature, and that Marcus had better look
after his laurels. I felt furiously ashamed and
distressed, but Clarke nodded kindly, shook my
hand, and told me that he would say something to
me about Literature later on. I inferred from the
tone of his voice that the information he had
to give me would not be pleasant. He never gave
it. George Walstab was there, and Garnet
Walch' and Grosvenor (“Jack”) Bunster, and,
I think, Bob Whitworth and others. The con-
versation flowed on. I was in Paradise—
a Paradise that smelt of whisky and cigar-smoke,
and echoed with light-hearted laughter. I had
previously read the Vie de Bohenie, and I said to
myself, “ This is Bohemia, indeed.” And it was.
All good Fellows. All good writers. Then, in a
pause of the conversation, while they were order-
ing drinks, or lighting their pipes, 6r something,
Marcus Clarke turned to me and asked me what I
was doing—meaning, I suppose, in that galley. I
replied that I was by trade a correspondence
clerk, but I was then writing for a suburban paper,
and never wanted to be a correspondence clerk
again. Some member of the company, who was
passing out of the room, tapped me on the
shoulder and said r “Don’t give away your silk
purse for a sow’s ear.” I didn’t catch his meaning
at the moment, but all the others laughed. Now
I know why they laughed.
• * V •
Kendall I met once. It was in a newspaper
office. He wanted some money and was offered a
guinea for a poem. It was a wet day and the rain
was streaming over the brim of his tall hat as he
set it upon the table. He was sick, and in trouble,
and his head was not his own. Yet he was ex-
pected to write a charming Australian lyric in his
own charming style about anything he pleased,
carte blanche. I was introduced to him. He
looked at me with deep-set, sorfowful eyes, shook
my hand silently, and bent down over his paper.
No use. The numbers would not flow. He
threw down his pen, and put his hat upon his
head again. The proprietor of the journal came
in, cast a glance upon the paper with a few lines
scrawled upon it, and slapped Kendall upon the
shoulder. “And you call yourself a poet,” he
said; “why, the Dublin Jew” (a rhymer of the
period who has now a flourishing bacon-shop near
the Haymarket) “could do better than that.
Nevermind! Better hick next time ! ” And he
slipped a sovereign and a shilling into Kendall’s
hand. And as lam now telling things that may
possibly be interesting because they are positively
true, I may say that the other party to the trans-
action was Harold Grey.
a * * *
Grey amused me greatly. Light heart, light
head, light conscience, and the lightest hand upon
a pen this country has ever seen. He had no
reverence for anything alive, and he had no more
sense of religion than the bloodhound I used to
keep at Mosmrm’s Bay. When he sat down to
write there would be lemons and chillies —hottest
and reddest of their kind —upon the table. And
he had a dainty way of dipping his pen carelessly
in the ink and writing his verses, or sketches, or
paragraphs, as if he were inscribing them upon
the wings of a butterfly.
There is at work a fairy pen,
Whose tip is wet with magic ink ;
And deep down in the pixies’ glen
A goblin writes on paper pink ;
And flow’rets bloom on every bush,
And fays on leaves pretend to slumber ;
They whisper one another —“ Hush !
He’s writing that new Christmas Number!”
Not good poetry, perhaps, but as Grey himself
used to say—“a d d good imitation of it.”
The Xmas number referred to is an old back
number of Brisbane Figaro, but, in my
memory at any rate, Grey will never be a back
number.
* » * *
To come near to the present. Some years ago I
called upon Edward Dyson. He had a charming
room over a grocery-shop. Carpeted nicely, but
as you walked over the floor you could feel the
hams and the sides of bacon swinging underneath.
Books of verse scattered casually around upon the
table in the centre of the room. On a side-table
near the fire—it was in winter time—his writing
apparatus carefully arranged, pens, ink, and
paper. Edward told lire that he could not com-
fortably write his best imaginative work unless he
were near the realities of life. I borrowed a side
of bacon from him. And when my pigs come to
market I shall not forget Edward Dyson.
* * * *
“Jacs” Bunster, however, was the man after
my own heart. You would ask him where a
certain article was—something of immediate im-
portance to yourself and to himself—and he would
reply vaguely, “In one of those bags ” Used to
keep his manusci’ipt wealth in sacks, like Ali
Baba. And then, if he were, in the humour and
the gout was not troubling him, he would talk of
old times and tell me over again the famous story
of “Anthony Brady, Cock o’ the North,”- He
told it once in print, in some obscure, 'forgotten
journal, but if he would resurrect it—and I am
glad to hear that he himself is still alive—it would
make the finest drama seen upon the stage since
the days of the “Colleen Bawn.” He used to
write anyhow—with any pen, in any ink, on any
paper that came in his way. And he would point
humorously to the sacks and say, with a glitter
of fun in his fine old blue eyes “ Lots of gold in
some of them some day.” I have had sacks of my
own since then, but have never found gold—not
even a gold cup—in any one of them.
* * * *
John Earrell I knew once, and twice, and al-
ways. Now in his later, wider-vested days he is
a distinguished writer upon the staff of the Daily
Telegraph. But when I first knew him—long
before his Pegasus was put into such sordid, use-
ful harness —he told me that he wrote his best
poems when he had one candle for light, and the
upper end of a beer-cask for desk. Eull cask, too.
But then he was a brewer. Never cared for so
much beer around, he said.
* * * *
Mrs. T sent for me one day. I was then
living in Abbotsford, a suburb of Melbourne. I
knew the lady by reading her stories in the Aus-
tralian Journal. Best writer of Australian De-
tective Stories I ever met with. She was sitting
up in bed, and asked me to take a chair. Handed
me a manuscript. I went on reading ; she went
on writing. Prettiest, clearest, most stylish
handwriting I ever saw. Forget what it was all
about now. “What do you think of it?” she
enquired, propping one arm upon her pillow.
“ Very much,” I replied. And meant what I
said. She rose half-mast high in her bed, clothed
in her night-dress. A handsome woman of sixty
or so who had evidently been a beauty in her day.
“ Go and get us a bottle of beer,’ she said, offer-
ing me the money. But I had money enough for
that much. And we drank together very
amicably. Last I saw of Mrs. T . God spare
her days if she is alive.
* * * *
I know little of the methods of composition of
current lady writers. One of them, however, in-
forms me that she writes best after taking her
morning bath. But this is a cruel and almost
impossible Ideal. Another tells me that she
thinks she could do her finest work if she were
floating like a halcyon on the bosom of the deep,
blue, fathomlesss sea. I took her out one day to
Little Coogee where men and women, if properly
attired, may bathe together in Continental
fashion. We were the only people there, with
the exception of a F f at Lady, who made a tidal
wave on the beach when she plumped into the
water. She (my friend) crept into the sea until
it came to her knees, and then fled back with a
four-hands-high wave fiercely pursuing her. Said
she was afraid to wet her hair because it would
take too long to dry. And I was darned near
drowned on that occasion, showing off, but she
never even looked at my performance. She was
writing, while her hair was drying—writing some-
thing about taking the Pacific by its foamy mane
and wringing its white beard.
* * • *
Lawson tells me that he likes best to write in
an atmosphere of quiet. Same with Roderio
Quinn. Paterson writes cateh-as-eatch-can. But
he has the catch—a sort of cavalier swagger and
swing and suggestion of the hard-riding hero who
killed three horses under him, and ate the third,
being short of provisions, and slew several men
upon the road, and then drew up at an inn, gave
his horse some whisky in his corn, wiped the
blood from his sword, kissed the maid of the inn,
ordered a flagon of canary, drank it, and then sat
down to write a rollicking lyric.
* * * •
I had accumulated much material for the pur-
pose of this article. And I have not used a line
of it. It will keep. Before I conclude, however,
let me say that I have never known an Australian
writer—and I have met every man of them worth
his salt —who wrote under the inspiration of
liquor. The Muse lives in many unexpected
places, but the Bottle is not one of them. Re-
spectable people will please take note.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Home.
I weary of the city, I sicken of the street,
My heart is filled with pity for the ones that there
I meet;
For have they in the morning ever heard the
bush-birds sing?
Or on the ridgy ranges heard the steel-shod horse-
hoofs ring ?
Have they ever, on the pine-tops, watched the
sun’s departing rays,
Or marked the rolling current of the bush-fire’s
lurid blaze ?
No! With dissipation’s measures drowning dissi-
pation’s strife —
Hunt ye down your fevered pleasures—shuffle fast
the cards of life !
Would I give my free existence for your petty
gain and loss ?
Would I rather God than devil ? Would I barter
crown for cross ?
Would Away the thought! My steps turn
where my heart’s flag is unfurled.
Better be the friend of Nature than be King of all
the World !
Some love the barren moorland and the bleak and
wintry sky,
And some love the craggy shoreland, and will love
it till they die.
There are some that love the farm-house, there are
some that ever pine
For old Ocean’s restless tossing—for the foaming,
seething brine;
It is ever what you’re bred to. And no matter
where I roam
My heart is in the bushland—for the bushland is
my home.
BRISBANE.
NADA.
The Tomboy.
She had a saucy word for you,
She had a dimpled smile;
She’d ride the thickest timber thro’
In quite bewitching style.
She had no thought of danger when
The chase was wild and hot—
The admiration of the men—
The careless tomboy, Tot.
She milked the cows and stooked the hay,
She worked among the trees;
She’d tackle h •, and lightly say:
>( ’Twas just like shelling peas ! ”
She danced divinely, Heaven knows;
Her cooking beat the lot;
She “scooped ” the prizes at the shows,
Did little fair-haired Tot.
There came a day—we say it low—
When Death was hovering near ;
Her life was past its evening glow,
The sky was grey and drear.
But in her eyes was mischief yet—
Brown eyes of fairy Tot—
She answered : “ Either way I’ll bet—
That Tomboy dies, or not! ”
VIC.
WILL JARRATT.
The Farting.
Fare ye well ! my laughing Southland, and your
rolling hills of green,
(0 the glory of your morning, wrapt in Summer’s
silver sheen,
And your gleaming dews of Winter, and your
biting breath that chills
When the morning mists are lifting and the go’d
is on the hills.)
Fare ye well! my laughing Southland (0 your
sleepy land of dreams !
0 your glens of fern and frondage, and your spark-
ling mountain-streams.)
And your river fondly droning (0 its bosom lit
with stars)
To the range and rolling ridges as it tumbles o’er
the bars.
Fare ye well! my laughing Southland, and those
dreamy dells of mine—
Ah ! in fairy-fashioned frondage still I see the
creepers twine,
Still I hear the songs of Nature when the drooping
leaves are stirred—
Now the music of the river, now the piping of a
bird.
Fare ye well! my laughing Southland, and old
lovers, false and true,
(0 those rosy sunlit faces, and those laughing eyes
of blue!
0 the rides we twain have taken in the dawning,
dusk, and day !
0 the pleasures of bush dances half a score of miles
away !)
Fare ye well! my laughing Southland, and my
t haunting “might have been,”
(0 my Mother and my Mistress! omy Lover
and my Queen !)
Fare ye well! my sun-kissed Southland, and my
people there who dwell,
0 my nver and my ranges—all—a wanderer’s
farewell!
N.B.W.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 18, 1902.
The Maoriland Political Aspect.
In the course of the last dozen years Maori-
land has got into a fixed habit of returning
its present Government to office by a large
majority, and force of habit will carry the
Government through the election that is now
pending. There is no Opposition worth
speaking of; no Opposition leader worth
mentioning; no Opposition policy worth
calling such. The men who used to be the
Opposition—sueh of them as still stay drearily
on the stage and totter in a senile fashion
before the footlights—are mostly very old.
They have been out of power for a dozen
years. They have hardly, in all that period,
carried even a snatch-division on a question
about a culvert on a back-country road.
They could hardly remember how to draft a
Bill now, and they have forgotten what
success looks like. Also, they have only
the dimmest possible recollection as to
what their own political principles are—
it being so long since they had any
use for their political principles. The very
backest kind of back-number party in the
politics of any other State is young and fresh
compared to the Maoriland Opposition. But
a new Opposition is growing up. It pro-
bably won't be very apparent at the present
election, but all the same there is a rift in
the lute, and the lute is by no means the
instrument it was.
* * * *
A dozen years ago there were practically
no small farmers in Maoriland. Before the
time of Premier Ballance the big arrogant
squatters, and the three banks, and the
large pastoral companies ruled the roost.
Ballance and Seddon created the great
mass of small independent agriculturists.
They threw open State lands on easy terms;
broke up big estates by the graduated
land-tax ; bought more big estates and cut
them up ; helped along the farmer by cheap
loans from the State Land bank; and created
an agricultural revolution. The politi-
cians who represented the once dominant
uarty of the big squatters became a scorned
and despised remnant in Parliament, and of
the old Continuous Government there was
hardly enough left to raise an argument. At
first the new agricultural element created by
BallAnce and Seddon was humble and
democratic. It hadn’t a copper of its own.
It owed the Government for its land. It
hadn’t got its fences up, and its hut was only
half-built. It had a weedy horse and two
cows, one of them dry ; and it toiled from
dawn to dark, and was glad to get Govern-
ment employment at railway construction in
slack times. It was quite willing to regard
the town artisan as a brother and an equal,
and in town and country the democratic party
was solid. These were the palmy days of
the Ballance-Seddon Government, before
Jeshurun waxed fat and kicked.
* * * *
But the miserable little cockie, whom the
Ballance and Seddon Governments took
from being the squatter’s serf and made an
independent farmer, became prosperous and
arrogant. As he .got his mortgage paid off
he began to feel just like the old, lofty
squatter, though on a rather smaller scale.
So far, only the earlier members of the new
class have fairly emerged from the prelimi-
nary stage of debt and humility; but more
and more of them are emerging every year.
This new class looks down on the town
artisan, its one-time brother and fellow-
democrat, from a great elevation. It wants
cheap labor, though it is barely 10 years
since it was a laborer itself. It has doubts
as to whether the “lower classes ” can be
trusted with the suffrage, and it is strongly
opposed to socialistic legislation—though, but
for the socialistic legislation which provided
it with cheap land and cheap money and all
manner of State assistance, it would still be
pursuing the cow of its old squatter boss for
15s. a week and inferior board and lodging.
It begins to have ideas, now, about
trade, and some of the members of the
Farmers’ Union say quite frankly that
Protection should be abolished, so as to
destroy a whole lot of the town industries,
and shut up the factories, and throw a large
number of people out of employment, and
then these people would get into a proper
state of humility, and be glad to come out
and work for the farmer at very little per
week, as they ought to do. In the course
of ten years, a very large number of the
liard-up laborers whom the Ballance-
Seddon policy turned into independent
farmer-proprietors have quite altered their
point of view, and begin to be seriously
shocked at the arrogance of the ‘ ‘ working
classes ” in wanting good wages.
* * * *
So political parties in Maoriland stand
thus : There is, first, the remnant of the old
Tory party—a few very old mental dodderers
of little account. Then there is the great
overwhelming Seddon party which is be-
ginning to decay internally. It consists, to
begin with, of the arrogant and affluent
section of the new farming class which,
having paid off its mortgage and ceased to
shovel manure personally, begins to regard
itself as a squirearchy. This is the body
which wants to abolish Protection so as to
kill city industries and supply cheap labor ;
it begins to talk of the rights of property
and to be shocked at the attitude of the
“ lower orders,” and to have doubts about
the wisdom of “socialistic legislation,”
but it still follows its old leaders—more
through force of habit than for any
other reason. Then there are the newer
agriculturists, whose mortgages to the
State are not paid off yet—who are still
more cockie than squire, and who haven’t
attained to the lordly arrogance of the real
Farmers’ Unionists, but who are progressing
that way. Next there is the main strength
and reliance of the Democratic Ministry—
the manufacturing population. Under its
splendid system of Protection and socialistic
legislation Maoriland has acquired a body of
manufacturing operatives which, in propor-
tion to its population, is nearly twice as
large as the manufacturing community in
backward New South Wales, and which
beats even Victoria badly ; and this section
of the people is the mainstay of the Seddon
Government—or at least it was till the
Seddon Government began to fall away from
its own ideals. And, finally, there are
signs of a new party growing up, a party
which regards Seddon as slow and anti-
■ quated, which loathes Seddon’s lieutenant,
Joseph Ward—he of the oily tongue and
the Colonial Bank ; which has ideas
about the nationalisation of all in-
dustries, and which objects to being found
dead in the same paddock with the
Farmers’ Union. It is a great party which
will follow Seddon and co. at the coming
election, but it is also a tumultuous party,
and a house that is beginning to be divided
against itself. If the necessary leaders pre-
sented themselves it might split into three
parties almost any day.
* * * *
The Government has changed just as the
party has „done. Ballance is gone ;
M‘Kenzie is gone; Reeves is gone;
Seddon has become an insane Jingo ; and
Joseph Ward has turned traitor—if he
wasn’t one all along. Seddon, since the
Boer war commenced, has forgotten almost
everything except his ridiculous thirst for
bloodshed, and for months he has been
drifting about England, making himself the
butt of a wearied empire by his diatribes
against the Afrikanders, by airing impossible
plans of Imperial Federation, lecturing the
King at public banquets about his error in
being civil to the Boer generals, and offering
advice to the world in general. And while
Seddon has been a contemned posturer and
Imperial bore in England Joseph Ward has
been running the Government on disastrous
lines at Wellington. By nature a financier,
company-promoter, jobber, trader and dealer
in “cats,” Ward has dropped his ill-fitting
democratic mantle, or at best only wears the
tail of it on high days and holidays. He has
gone on the simple policy of making friends
of the mammon of unrighteousness by re-
ducing taxation, spending borrowed money
lavishly to fill the gap, booming on the mad
M‘Millan and Parkes and Duncan Gillies
and Julius Vogel lines, dropping all serious
attempt at progressive legislation, and earning
the dangerous friendship and approval of the
Tory press.
* * * *
If Seddon fails to come back in time, or if
he comes back still in the state of idiotic
blood-drunkenness into which he has lately
degenerated, and with his thoughts still
concentrated on the alleged necessity for
hanging De Wet, the coming election will
be a curious affair. An overwhelmingly
strong Democratic party—overwhelming so
far as mere numbers are concerned —with
two distinct secessions apparently preparing
in its ranks, will go to the poll with no very
distinct programme, but rather on the
strength of its past reputation. It will go to
the poll as the anti-borrowing party which
is piling up debt more recklessly than it was
ever piled up in Maoriland before. It will
have, as its deputy-leader, a man who has no
sympathy with it whatever, except the
sympathy which enables him to keep his
billet, and who receives the cordial blessing
and approval of its enemies. All these
things make up a curious complication.
While Maoriland continues on the flood-tide
of prosperity, and while no dangerous rival
leader turns up, the great, though now badly-
cracked Seddon party may hold together for
a long time, even with all these drawbacks,
but the two years in which Seddon has been
howling for blood, to the serious neglect of
things that were less gory but more im-
portant, have made sad havoc with the
stability of his throne.
The First Federal Session.
The Federal Parliament closed its first session
after 17 months of toil. It put up an exceed-
ingly good record in that time, as Australian
Parliamentary records go, though it is a pity that
any qualifying clause has to be inserted. Even
its bitterest enemies and most unscrupulous jour-
nalistic slanderers, when they sum up the work
accomplished, can find only seven measures post-
poned against 37 passed. Compared with the
pitiable paralysis of some of the State Legislatures
(and the Commonwealth leaders had to do their
work by the aid of a Parliament whose members
had mostly grown up in that atmosphere of
paralysis) the results shown are remarkably good.
They would probably have been better but
for the dull, senseless, malignant stonewalling
of the crowd which represents British and Ger-
man and Yankee manufacturing interests in
the House of Representatives—the M ‘Millan
and Pumpkin Smith push, that blocked business
through long days of repetition and weary nights
of absolute uselessness. What work the Federal
Parliament did must be judged, to some extent,
by the material it had to do the work with. The
largest consignment of that material came from
the N.S.W. Legislature, which thinks for 45 years
about a Local Government Bill, and for 40 years
about building a mile of city railway, and for 35
years or more about a North Shore bridge, and
which has been 16 years reducing the deficit with-
out reducing it at all; and it is difficult to keep that
kind of material in motion. As for the Dobson
element, and the Victorian Upper House element
(imported into Federal politics largely because of
David Syme’s private friendships), motion of any
kind is quite foreign to their habit of mind.
* * * »
The Federal Government was also hampered by
the weakness, incapacity, laziness, and constant
absence of Premier Barton, the weakness and
woodenness of Minister Bill Lyne, and the over-
anxiety of Acting-Premier Deakin to please every-
body. New South Wales supplied a larger con-
tingent of Ministers than its population entitled
it to, and its contingent, barring the brilliant
O’Connor, was a deadly failure. Then, in addi-
tion to other things, the Ministry had to meet
all the preliminary difficulties that arise when
a multitude of strange monkeys are put into
one cage, and told to become acquainted,
and shake down comfortably, and not drag
at each other’s tails. The Reid and MAI ill an
party was fully as responsible as the Government
for any flaws in the Constitution, but being in
Opposition it was able to treat those flaws, when
they came to light, as the Government’s special
iniquities, and to make capital out of them. And
the grinding of many discordant axes made con-
stant trouble. The White Australia policy aroused
the intense malignity of the corrupt nigger-
driving party in Queensland; and that party’s lying
power, and its capacity for button-holing silly but
influential preachers, and attracting them to its
side by food and flattery, proved to be something
extraordinary. The proposal to prevent wealthy
newspaper proprietors cadging further on the
community for gratis postage roused a swarm of
open or secret enemies. The splendidly liberal
and democratic Federal franchise made more
enemies—not among the people, but among
newspapers which misrepresent the people. And
the policy of aiding and encouraging Australian
industries aroused the frenzy of the influential
gang which acts as agents and buttoners here for
British, German, American, and other manufac-
turers — the anti - Australian M‘Millan gang,
which loathes Australian industries that threaten
to make the importer superfluous ; and to reduce
the enormous profits which the rich absentee
importing boss makes out of Australia and spends
in London. And the refusal of the Government
to allow importers who attempted to defraud the
revenue to escape by backstairs influences caused
great indignation. By the end of the first Federal
session there was hardly one wealthy lie, fraud,
swindle, or hypocrisy in Australia of which the
Commonwealth Government hadn’t made an
enemy.
* * * *
It had also to contend, in some measure, with
the hostility of State politicians. When the
Commonwealth was established it was stated,
quite truly, that it would supply the chance for
great economies in administration. When the
States were relieved of their largest responsibili-
ties there was a splendid opportunity to cut down
the number of State Ministers and politicians, to
sack the separate Agents-General, and to reduce
the State Governors to the level of corresponding
Canadian officials, who receive about £lOOO a year
and are kept for purely business purposes. Ine
State Governments, being loth to part with the
jobs and the pomp and the patronage, and being
afflicted with that public paralysis which keeps
almost everything save borrowing at a standstill,
availed themselves of hardly any of these oppor-
tunities. Therefore the cost of running the
Federal Legislature became an extra, and among
State politicians there grew up a habit of speaking
severely about the expense of Federal administra-
tion—they had to say something like that to cover
up and disguise the fact that the extravagance and
corruption of State administration were really at
fault.
* * * *
With all its failings and weaknesses, which are
many, the Federal Government, judged on its
record so far as it has gone, is worth all the six
State Governments put together, and more. The
beginning of every Federation has been marked
by an almost immediate counter-agitation among
a few loud-mouthed malcontents who professed to
represent a secessionist reaction, and the begin-
nings of Australian unity are no exception to the
rule. But the miserably inferior stamp of the
politicians who represent the alleged reaction is in
itself a proof of its bogus character; it is only a
tenth-rate movement that has to be content
with tenth-rate leaders. Speaking for itself, The
Bulletin only knows one man who voted for
Federation and has since perverted tosecessionism.
It knows any number of men who voted for
Federation and who say emphatically that they
would vote for it again, but who have a sorrowful
impression, based on the statements of that section
of the press which has been parochial all along,
that someone else has gone back on the policy of
union, and that, if the question were again put to
the people, the wholesale defection of someone
else would leave the Federal party in a minority.
The fact that that alleged, but unfindable, some-
one else only lives in the imagination of the
parochial and Tory and nigger-labor press, and in
the minds of a party which wants to suppress
Australian industry in the interests of foreign
manufacturers, is not so generally understood as
it should be.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Men to Build the State.
“It is all very well to say, as some people do, that a
democracy prefers to be governed by ignorance. It does
not prefer to be governed by ignorance any more than
does anyone else. It desires to be led by its best men.
We are a democracy here if ever there was a democracy,
in the world, and we are likelj' to be one. Let the leaders
be the best men. Although there have been many dis-
tinguished men on the rolls of this University there are
not so many now in public life as I should like to see.
Many have gone to the south, some to the north. You
have supplied three Judges to Queensland, a Premier to
Australia, but in the number of members of the Legisla-
ture graduates of the University are fewer than I should
wish."—Chief Justice Griffith, at the Sydney University
Shivoo.
0 People of this large
Lone Land, where all is new,
Take care who has the charge
Of making laws for you !
God only knows the harm the ignorant can do.
Let them be men of sense,
The Coves who legislate;
Chaste in their eloquence,
Classic, but not ornate;
These are the men to build the Temple of the
State:
Good men who can with ease
Talk Greek till all is blue,
And quote Demosthenes
With Attic accent true —
They are the men you want; they are the Blokes
for you.
Unhappy is the land—
Though prosperous in show,
It never can be grand—
Whose legislators know
Nothing about the grace and charm of Cicero.
Sad, sad, it is to see
Men Premiers become
Who would, if duty-free,
Prefer low beer, or rum,
To the Pierian spring or sweet Elysium.
The sordid things of life
They know, with wisdom mean ;
Each, like a kitchen-knife,
Is rudely, coarsely keen,
Untempered in the fine old crystal Hippocrene.
The Present bounds their ken ;
Its squalid feast they spread;
They do not mind that men
Live not alone by bread,
Nor know that wisdom lives in languages long
dead.
No Alma Mater dear,
No Academic Home,
Nursed them at breasts austere,
And taught each oaf and gnome
“ The glory that was Greece, the grandeur that
was Rome.”
O, People of this land
Choose men for you to speak
Within your Council grand,
Well-versed in lore antique,
High-cultured gentlemen, who understand theii
Greek.
Then you shall see an age
Of glory and renown ;
And Sydney, we engage,
Shall be the Muses’ town —
The Southern City fair, of the White Violet Crown.
Then Learning will wax fat,
And Science bear the bell,
And Labor doff his hat
And graft for them like h—• —l;
The Golden Age will coftie, and all things shall
be well.
CREEVE ROE.


The Inebriates Act in N.S.W.
N. S. Wales Parliament recently, in a spasm of
common sense, passed an Act giving the State,
and, under proper supervision, private individuals,
power to deal with habitual inebriates in a scien-
tific way. Before the passage of the Act, the law-
in N.S.W., as in most other countries, only took
cognisance of the drunkard when he or she
became a public nuisance ; then it fined him
or her ss. or more. The Inebriates Act
authorised a more rational way of dealing
with the habitual drunkard who has become
a public nuisance segregation in a hospital
for two or three years, until the alcoholic craving
should be cured by treatment and the flight of time.
Also a way was opened for dealing humanely and
sensibly with the vast numbers of drunkards who
do not sink to the level of the common public
nuisance, and who do not enjoy even the
limited remedial influences of being arrested
and fined ss. A very well-conceived Act
was this piece of legislation, calculated to
reduce the gaol population enormously and
to repair and refit many _ shattered lives.
And the Act being so good in its purpose and so
wise in its plan, one might have calculated witii
certainty that something would go wrong and
prevent it from becoming a living power of
regeneration. Something did go wrong. The
State Government having passed the Act, the
Supreme Court was asked to draw up regulations
governing its application. These regulations,
designed by a bench of Judges with evidently no
medical knowledge, and with just as little com-
mon-sense—men who, on this occasion at least,
were hide-bound by precedent and blinded
by the dust of mouldy custom made _ it
impossible that the Act should have any applica-
tion in regard to the private drunkard —the man
with an alcoholic mania which is ruinous to him-
self and his family, but which stops short of the
police court stage of notoriety. To legally con-
vict a man of habitual alcoholism and subject him
to restraint was made as difficult as to legally con-
vict him of envy or pride or uncharitableness.
The State Government might have insisted on these
absurd rules of theSupremeCourt being abrogated,
and have had a fresh code drawn up by more com-
petent people. But it didn’t. The Aot was
simply dropped. No effort even was made to es-
tablish any institution for the cure of inebriates.
The See Goversment is a very “progressive”
Government, but it is only progressive when there
is a brass-band accompaniment, and a show of
bunting, and a loan in the offing. To save the
country and the family the hideous loss which is
yearly caused through inebriety, by a little dili-
gence in enforcing an excellent law, is quite out
of its line, for the saved drunkard can hardly be
made the subject of a brass-band glorification or
a banquet, and he can’t be offered as security for
a loan, nor can he be “opened ” by the Governor
as if he were a new railway. The Inebriates Act
havingbeen passed, one sincere politician, by the ex-
ercise of a little pushfulness, could probably shame
the Government into having it administered. But
Abraham might look as vainly for that one as for
ten honest men in the Cities of the Plain.
Glory to Minister Lyne.
The Bulletin has learned never to pin its faith to
a politician—until he has been dead ten years and
all his correspondence has been published post-
humously. Still politicians, it must be admitted
and should be admitted, do good things some-
times, from good, bad, and indifferent motives as
the case may be. W. J. Lyne has been doing well
as Acting-Minister for Federal Defence, and his
good work claims recognition. It will be some-
thing of a calamity if Mr. John Forrest is
allowed now to take over control of the Defence
Department and upset the course of treatment
which his locum tenens has been following for
the reduction of military fever. Mr. Bill Lyne
has his patient’s pulse and temperature almost
normal, and General Hutton gnashes impotent
teeth as he sees drifting away the last relies of his
scheme to make Australia a recruiting-ground for
the English War Office. But it is a well-known
fact that he hopes for something of a reversal of
the present Defence policy when Mr. John
Forrest resumes control, and, with Parliament
out of session, is left free to indulge the Imperial-
istic notions he got as boss official of a Crown con-
vict colony. If Mr. Barton and his colleagues
are wise they will take all sorts of care that General
Hutton is disappointed, and that the Bill Lyne
Defence policy is strictly adhered to. During his-
term of office as Minister for Defence the Home-
Secretary has definitely told General Hutton
that Australia will countenance no proceedings
which would put her soldiers under any outside
authority ; and that, whilst it might be General
Hutton’s idea to consider the Imperial War
Office first and Australia second, it was not his.
That is the attitude which Parliament approves
and the people approve. It should be main-
tained, even if, for that purpose, a rearrangement
of portfolios is necessary.

The Financial Pot and the Monetary
Kettle.
The recent great Parliamentary row between Pot
Waddell and Kettle Carruthers over the
N.S.W. finances was interesting in itself, and also
interesting for reasons with which neither the Pot
nor the Kettle had much to do. The facts that
were stated by these two disreputable cooking
utensils were already well understood by every-
body who really knows anything about the shock-
ing cash-box of N.S.W. So were some facts which
neither cooking utensil thought it safe or advisable
to mention. Last financial year the Pot closed
his accounts with what he said was a deficit of
£84,000 —this after being relieved by the Federal
Government of many of the burdens which
the N.S.W. State Treasurer used to have
to carry, and after spending the biggest
revenue that a N.S.W. State Treasurer ever
spent. But, in addition, he postponed payment
of £lOO,OOO due to certain sinking funds; he
used £200,000 or more of what were really trust
funds as revenue, and he left some £84,000- of
accounts unpaid and unprovided for. All these
things the Pot did, and dhe remarks that the
Kettle made about them were justly severe ; the
Pot’s real deficit, as the Kettle had no difficulty
in showing, being about £500,000 on these items
alone. In addition to all this he used about
£1,000,000 of loan money for purely revenue
purposes, hut the Kettle, having been Treasurer
in the shamefully dishonest Reid Government,
which worked this same political swindle for
all it was worth, made but small allusion
to this. Also the Pot failed to charge himself in
the accounts to 30th June, 1902, with interest
due, and in part actually paid, on the public debt,
but as it was the awful Government to which the
Kettle belonged that invented this fraud, it was
impossible to say much about it either. The
real N.S.W. deficit for last financial year was
more than £1,500,000, but the Kettle could only
speak indignantly to the Pot about one-third of
it, for the other two-thirds represented his own
brand of political fraud and swindle. The
disreputable banging of the two cheap and nasty
cooking utensils, however, roused up Labor mem-
ber Edden to denounce the talk of retrenchment
as a Tory device, the real aim of which was
to drive the Labor party out of politics. These
were not his exact words, but the meaning is all
there. Such statements, when the Labor party
lets them pass uncontradicted, are paving the way
for the worst kind of disaster. Retrenchment and
more taxation will be a necessity very soon. The
time is not far off when a retrenchment party will
be able to sweep the polls in N.S.W., and to
shove both the Pot and the Kettle aside as dead
and gone ironmongery. And if the Labor pafty
is not that party, then the Tory party will be that
party instead. The time will come before long
when N.S.W. will want economy and Labor poli-
tics or economy and Tory politics, but it will
want economy in any case. Labor member Edden
is reported to have described the wranglings of
the Pot and the Kettle as merely calculated to
injure the State’s credit; but, considering all the
circumstances, the State’s credit is past being
saved, if the present methods continue, by any
hush-it-up processes. The local disturbances of
the Pot and the Kettle are not heard in London,
but the borrowing of £7,000,000 inside 12 months
is beard there, and it speaks louder than words.
If the Labor Party would come to the front now,
as the party which was ready to abjure equally the
Waddell fake, and the Carruthers fake, and
the See fake, and the Reid-Parkks-M‘Millan
fake, and to reach out for the privilege of govern-
ing N.S.W. itself on an absolutely honest and
solvent basis, it might have a great future before
it. But it can’t hope to do any permanent good
by lending itself either to Pot politics or Kettle
politics. Both these leaky vessels have kept
themselves boiling on it too long.

That Alleged Naval Agreement.
Premier Barton comes back to explain how he
fell in over the new Naval Tribute agreement,
and it will be found, if there is any truth in current
report, that he has fallen in more badly than was
generally supposed. Certainly his promises,
whatever they are, bind neither Parliament nor
Commonwealth to anything; but if it is really
true that Premier Barton has pledged the
Government to stand by the alleged agreement,
then the Government will either have to repudiate
its nominal head, or go out of office. The com-
mon statement is that Canada (population over
5,000,000) is to pay nothing; Natal, £35,000 a year;
Cape Colony, £5O, OOOayear; Maoriland (population
770,000), £40,000 ayear; the Commonwealth (popu-
lation 3,800,000), £200,000 a year. Compared
with the other contributing States, Australia is
not badly taken down; compared to Canada, it
has distinctly fallen among thieves. But the
point on which Premier Barton must almost cer-
tainly either abandon the whole business or aban-
don his billet is this: Under the old agreement
Australia paid £105,000 a year for the services of
a small and inefficient fleet, over which it had no
control, but it was at least a part of the agree-
ment that, in time of war, the-fleet, such as it
was, would be somewhere in South Pacific waters,
so that it might meet, and possibly sink, any
enemy that attempted to attack Australia. Under
the new agreement, Australia is to pay
£200,000, and the promise that the fleet
will stay here or hereabouts for the defence
of Australia in war time, is wholly withdrawn.
Australia is to pay for defence, with a specific
assurance that there may be no defence at all
when it is needed, and that any enemy may bom-
bard us with impunity. If'the agreement proves
to be anything like the reports concerning it, all
the refreshments that the British Government
could have offered to Barton won’t compensate
for the outrageous badness of the bargain. Fur-
ther, it has been stated, unofficially yet very
openly and significantly, that any idea of Aus-
tralia creating a fleet of its own must be severely
discouraged. This is a broad and conspicuous
hint that Australia can’t be trusted ; or that the
marine glory of the empire, with the pomp, dignity,
salary, and brass buttons attached thereto, are not
for common people like the Australians, but for the
superior Briton only; and that Australia must
be compelled to rely on Britain for defence —and
be reminded unpleasantly at intervals that it
doesn’t defend itself but hides behind the skirts of
its venerable parent. The theory that the
empire’s great weapon of offence must be wholly
in the hands of that small fragment of tbe empire
called Britain, embodies the idea of subjection and
inferiority on the part of the rest of the empire. It
puts it on the same level as the Spanish dependen-
cies—subject provinces, inhabited by an inferior
race which is to be overawed and kept in a state
of humility by a constant show of force. If Pre-
mier Barton comes back for the purpose of ex-
plaining that he has really fixed up an agreement
by which the Commonwealth is to double its
subsidy for the purpose of being probably deserted
in time of war, and strongly impressed with its
inferiority in time of peaee, he will probably find
that there isn’t enough explanation in the Com-
monweath to fully smooth out matters. And
Australia must needs begin to notice that every
time a Premier goes to England there is something
dreadful to explain on his return.

No Quorum.
“ Counting out” the house is quite a favorite bit
of strategy with N.S.W. Government just now.
If an awkward question is raised on a motion for
adjournment, Ministers suddenly find that they
have important business outside; and their sup-
porters make the same discovery. Some play
snooker, or billiards, or dominoes, or cards, and
become so engrossed in the game that an earth-
quake wouldn’t attract their attention—much less
the quorum-bell. A few members find that
they’ve been neglecting their education of
late and must read up Constitutional history
or Hall Caine ; you may find them in the
library and in various quiet rooms —also deaf to-
the bell. Then a Government supporter slips into
the chamber and “ calls attention to the state of
the House.” The Speaker has the quorum bells
rung (everybody who is not stone-deaf should hear
them), and nobody comes in, and a quorum isn’t
present, and the sitting lapses, and the awkward
question is effectively shelved. Sometimes, if a.
quorum of members will persist in remaining to
“make up a House,” the honorable member for
Blank is summoned to one Minister’s room to dis-
cuss the proposal to build a State hennery in his-
electorate ; and the hon. member for Dash
finds out that now is his chance to see
about getting that little favor for the
strenuous constituent with 11 sons and two
daughters, all grown-up; and the representative
of Anywhere is called out to see about some most
pressing local matter ; and they all go out because
by these things are politicians saved or lost in the
day of ballot-box judgment; and then there is no
quorum. Mr. Arthur Griffith's Bill to abolish
fees in the schools and make education iu
N.S.W. free as well as compulsory—a reform
which is suggested as much by expediency as by
logical justice—seems to have been treated in this
way on Oct. 7. At any rate it was counted out
and a most awkward question, on which the
Cabinet is bitterly divided, shelved. Surely the
Labor Party might have “ kept a house ” on this
occasion ? The Free Education Bill has now
probably been killed for the session, though it is
favored (in a sort of a way) by a majority of .the
Assembly. Sinister secret influences, however,
conspired to slay in the dark a reform bill to which
members or Ministers dare not show open hostility.

Greater Sydney and the Lesser Mind.
The petty parish-pump spirit which envelopes
Australian municipal politics as a fog, and makes
young new cities in a bright, clean climate murky,
grimy and unlovely, was conspicuously displayed
at a recent sitting of the Greater Sydney Commis-
sion. The representative of an important
suburb (Paddington) pleaded in opposition to the
scheme of reform that as yet the population of
Sydney “was not big enough.” It was all very
well for London and Glasgow and Birmingham to
have advanced notions of municipal statesman-
ship ; they were big cities; Sydney was too
small for such great ideas, and should
walk on her heels a long while yet and grow
more dirty and dismal and disreputable with each
decade, and wait until she had a vaster popula-
tion and a bigger accumulation of dirt, and a more
appalling lot of jerry-built suburbs. Only old
rakes should reform, the Paddington man seemed
to think. Young, new sinners among cities
should keep on in the old evil ways, learning
nothing and reforming nothing, until suffi-
ciently decayed to justify an era of modern
municipalism. Paddington, by the way, is one of
the oldest of Sydney’s suburbs, and one of the
most dismal, and ugly, and dust-infested, and
crooked, and generally disgraceful—without a
decent green spot in its boundaries, a wilderness
of cracked asphalt and the broken metre of John
Cash Neild’s poetry; and it should be sufficiently
far-gone In squalor and unbeautiful degeneration to
call for reform. But there is always a class of
people from whom the suggestion of reform brings
the reply either that “it is premature,” or that
“the time is past.” Their lazy conservatism,
shuddering at the idea of a change as a medieval
baron would at the idea of a hot bath, finds refuge
in “too soon” or “too late.” Sydney certainly
lacks as yet millioYis of population; but that
makes the task of setting its civic government on
a clean and up-to-date basis the more easy. With
each year’s additions to property values, to ill-
regulated buildings, to badly-designed streets,_ to
municipal debts, to private enterprise exploitation
of concessions, the task of the municipal reformer
is much more difficult. Now is the acceptable
time. It’s never too soon to mend.

More Dry Dog.
Ex-Premier George Reid— he of the Cash Dog
and the Dry Basis—has lately been on the Free-
trade stump in Victoria, and, according to the
large heading in Sydney Telegraph, has supplied
some “Remarkable Factory Figures,” whereat the
Victorians were “Again Astonished.” As all
Mr. Reid’s figures are remarkable, being mostly
invented as be goes along, or else borrowed from
that amazing figure-dodger, Maximilian Hirsch,
it is no wonder that the Victorians were astonished
—considerably so. But in all the years that
George Reid has been beating the drum of the
foreign importers, and acting as political pimp
for British and German and Yankee manufac-
turers —in fact, for everybody except the Austra-
lian manufacturer—there are six or eight im-
portant questions to which it has never been
possible to get an answer, even when Reid was _
whirling his Dry Dog (most volubly, and these
questions remain unanswered to this day. Some
samples are these :
(1) Why is it, since Freetrade makes everybody Jolly
and Fat, that all the countries whose competition begins
to grow really dangerous—the rapidly rising countries
whose names are in everybody’s mouth, such as the
United States, Canada, Germany, and Argentina—are
Protectionist? Why is no new Freetrade State rising
anywhere ? .
(2) Why were none of these countries dangerous rivals
in their Freetrade days, for they all practised Freetrade
at one time, and stagnated under it, despite their great
resources? . „ ,
(3) Why is it that the Australasian State—Maoriland—
which has by far the largest number of people employed
in manufactures in proportion to population, and whose
manufactures are making the most rapid progress,
is about the most Protectionist of the seven States?
(4) Also, why is it that the State which has proportion-
ately the next largest manufacturing population—
Victoria —is also strongly protectionist ?
(5) Why is it that the best that Reid and co. can say for
their-semi-Freetrade policy is that under it N.S.W. has
nearly as large a number of people employed in manufac-
tures as a State half its age, and one-quarter its size, and
grievously hampered by an insufficient coal supply as
well ?
(6) Also, what is the good of any political liar ascribing
N.S.W.’s industrial progress to Freetrade, when N.S.W.
was partly Protectionist till 1891; still more Protectionist
from then till 1896 ; and partly Protectionist again from
1896 onwards?
(7) If Protection is such a good thing, why didn’t Reid
try it when he was in power? Also, why didn’t M’Middan
and Carruthers and the two Smiths and the Labor rene-
gade, Joe Cook, try it ?
(8) Further, why have the Sydney Freetrade papers in-
sisted for all these years on having 50 per cent. Protection
under the postal laws ?
(9) Finally, were all these papers and politicians lying
when thev said they believed Protection was bad for the
country? ’ Or did they believe it, and simply practise a
certain measure of Protection because they were hired
by some foreign enemy to damage the country ?
If George Reid would give up his loose and un-
fixable whirling of the famous Dry Dog and
definitely answer these nine questions, he would
clear up a lot of obscurities.

The Treachery of the Victorian Factory
Bosses.
For bare-faced, shameless trickery the attitude of
the Victorian Chamber of Manufacturers towards
the Factories Act would be hard to equal. The
Act lapsed with the close of the late Parliament.
With a general election before them the manu-
facturers set about quieting the fears of the
workers. They were the first to meet and declare
that as the Factories’ Act had been lost by acci-
dent they would not take advantage of its tem-
porary disappearance from the statute-book. In-
deed, they passed a resolution to the effect that
they would support its re-enactment in its old
form until the report of the Factories’ Act Royal
Commission had been considered by Parliament.
At the time The Bulletin warned the workers
not to be lulled into a sense of security by these
fair promises of assistance from their enemies. It
is evident that the warning was not heeded.
If it had been the workers could have wiped
out all the manufacturers’ friends at the poll.
They did not do so—many of them voting
for men who, while professing friendship for the
Act, have done their best to wreck it. Now what
is the result ? The elections are no sooner over
than the Victorian Chamber of Manufacturers,
led by Derham, and men called Jacobs, Levi, and
Scarlett, holds another meeting, at which
its members declaim against the re-enactment of
the vital provisions of the Factories Act —the
Wages Board sections. Derham, among other
fine Tory things, said that
The employers did not object to the sections of the Act
dealing with sanitation and the limitation of hours for
females and boys, but they strongly opposed the re-intro-
duction of the Wages Boards for one day. They were
vicious in principle.
The employers thereby admit that, as far as the
men are concerned, they want a free hand to work
them as long as they like. Another bright
specimen of the manufacturers, T. E. Varley,
said (Argus report, Oct. 7):
Luck or Providence had taken the neck of the Chamber
from the collar, and it should take good care it did nob
get in again. He had himself enjoyed a little freedom
for some time, and life had been a good deal pleasanter
in his business relations since the Act had ceased.
(Laughter and “ Hear, hear.”) Those little modifications
which he had deemed advisable he had made—(laughter)
—and he expected that others had done the same. (Hear,
hear.) The employers of Victoria should determine not
again to endure these abominable labor boards for a
single half-hour. (Hear, hear.)
Now how do the workers feel who, trusting the
promises of T. E. Yarley, Derham and company,
voted for any other than democratic candidates ?

The Verdict—Manslaughter.
The Drayton Grange horror having been investi-
gated by Royal Commission, the responsibility
for the loss of life which it caused has been
variously located ; but there is no attempt to
shirk the issue that the chief blame 3 rests
with the British War Office. After all,
there was only one definite issue before the
Commission. “Who was responsible for sending
an over-crowded, disease - infested transport
away 1 ” And to that there could be only one
reply—tbe Imperial authorities. Outside that,
it might be argued that the officer in charge,
Col. Lyster (since promoted by that very
strenuous Imperialist, General Hutton), might
have bearded the War Office and refused to
sail under the circumstances; or that greater
efforts on the part of officers and men might have
been made to alleviate the terrible conditions
created by the initial act of inhumanity. But
those issues were necessarily ones on which
opinions only, not decisions, could be given.
On the issue that the vessel was _ sent
away overcrowded and with an_ epidemic of
measles on board, the decision is. against the
Imperial authorities, and it is practically one of
manslaughter. The duty rests now with the
Australian Government to demand reparation on
behalf of the relatives of the men sacrificed by
tbe grossly criminal carelessness and haste of the
War Office ; and that’s a duty Mr. Barton must
be kept up to.
Brisbane Courier, in its leading article of Sept.
30, announced its decision that the principal
cause of Queensland’s troubles was the fact that
MTIWRAITh’s great continental land-grant rail-
way swindle fell through in the long, long ago.
Comforting discoveries of this kind are frequent.
When a community or a party is in trouble, it is
either its own fault or somebody else’s fault, and
the latter idea is the easier. If Queensland had
paid the costof government out of taxation, as many
other countries do, and devoted its land revenue
to public works purposes, it could have had its
transcontinental line without debt or trouble. As
the dominant Tory party of bygone years wouldn’t
pay taxes, this didn’t happen, and the con-
sequences, if the Tory party co.uld have them all
to itself, would be only what it deserves. There
was no Parliamentary Labor Party in Queensland
when MTlwraith’s' stupendous folly fell through,
but the Courier gets what satisfaction it can by
blaming the L a bor Party all the same. As a
handy, comprehensive scapegoat for things that
happened before it was born the Labor Party is
unrivalled.

Sydney D.T. last week :
Mr. Lyne returned to Melbourne to-day. He
says that he will during the week confer with Major-
General Hutton and see that the wishes of Parliament in
regard to retrenchment are given effect to. It is painful
for him, he says, to have to do this extra retrenching, but
the Labor party must be obeyed.
S.M.H., same issue, same information :
Mr. Lyne also said it was painful to have to do this
extra retrenching, but Parliament must be obeyed.
There’s a good deal of difference. Now, was
Lyne foolish enough to use that very improper
remark about the Labor party to the D.T. ? Or

just now :

The “Corona Outrage,” one of the last incidents
of the shearing row. was a very small outrage
when stripped of its newspaper scare-heads.
The station, which is 60 or 70 miles outside Broken
Hill, started shearing with a “team” imported
from S. Aus., under M.S.U. terms. The A.W.U.
men formed a camp at the nearest township,
Tarrawingee. News went in there, by “ mulga-
wire ” and otherwise, that the men were sick of
their job, and were ready to throw it up, and the
camp at last decided to go out and invite them in
quietly and without any fuss. Next morning,
though, when it was explained to them that they
had broken agreement, and might be hanged, or
something of that sort, for it, and when
the police started to buzz round, some of
them began to go green, and said at the second
time of asking by the police that they wanted to
go back. Also, of course, they said they hadn’t
wanted to leave. Two of the men who went from
the camp to Corona were charged with riot,
assault, and fake imprisonment; and the jury,
though acquitting them of the riot and the
assault, was bound to find them technically guilty
of the false imprisonment, because admittedly, when
some of the men wanted to go out for an early-
morning drink, one of the camp stopped them.
Even as to the technical offence, though, the jury
recommended the accused to mercy on the ground
that they didn’t know it wa3 an offence. And
Judge Pring gave them mercy and 12 months
(three months off on finding sureties for three
years good conduct). If they had cracked a head
or two in an ordinary row, they would probably
have got 12 weeks. It was perfectly > obvious
tl'.at they gob the 12 months because they
were living in a strike-camp; if they had
not been doing that, all the “false imprisonment ”
to which they subjected anybody would have been
too small a matter for even the newest J.P. to
trouble about. Bub Judge Pring said there
