“ A Million of Money,” revived at Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday, is the joint work of Henry
Pettitt and Augustus Harris, and when Henry
and Augustus wrote it they produced something
quite above the usual melodrama level. . The
“ Million of Money ” comes into the possession of
Baker at the conclusion of Act 1, after he
has annoyed Parson Maythorne by becoming
secretly married to his daughter. Baker’s down-
fall is occasioned by his habit of devoting his
coin to asylums, institutions, deadbeats, etc.,
and his after resolve to perish nobly out-
side of England gives scope for a good deal
of horse and soldier and mesmeric woman.
Baker, as the large, beautiful hero pur-
sued by the Fatal Millions, is much as usual.
There is never much variety about Baker nowa-
days, but his love scenes with Frances Ross and
Harrie Ireland (as the mesmeric lady) are con-
vincing. Miss Harrie Ireland is Well cast as that
siren. Mrs. Bland Holt, as the parson’s niece,
has not quite the part calculated to bring out her
best points, and her race costume might be better;
but Bland Holt, as a frisky divinity student,
makes a good deal of humor, which is rather
better than usual. A good, though small, part
study is that of Arthur Styan as the adventuress’s
first husband. The scenery includes the finish for
the Derby, a shipwreck, several glittering halls of
light, and some dens of sin.
** * *
“Sherlock Holmes” commenced his
statuesque career at Sydney Royal on Saturday,
but the city has not yet quite decided how to
treat the large, impossible drama. There are in it
some traces of Conan Doyle, who supplies Holmes
and Moriarty; but the girl, and “the papers,” and
the girl’s deranged parent, and Larrabee, who has
the girl a prisoner and twists her arms to make
her give up the documents, are extraneous
matter. Sherlock himself, as done by Cuyler
Hastings, is an interesting study. He is tall and
pallid, and beautifully dressed ; his face is iron,
and his voice is American ; and he withers people
up by the way he puts a long, cold, instantaneous
finger on their iniquities. The Professor (Sher-
lock’s great enemy, who runs a gigantic
criminal business and inysterious-murder-factory)
is one of Atholwood’s triumphs, a long, bent,
grey reptile in a long, brownish coat. He slinks
Where Sherlock stands Upright, and he has atti-
tudes that suggest a recently-hanged vulture. But
as he doesn’t do one successful crime in all the
drama he is at a disadvantage. Miss Mabel Lane
acts her part powerfully as the wicked lady of
the story, and Miss Chevalier has many good
points as the girl who is mauled to make her give
up the papers. Something good should also be
said for Augustus Glover and Hamilton Stewart.
But, apart from the two principals, the brightest
stars are Edmund Gwenn, as the hired burglar,
and Sherlock’s boy Billy. Billy is an amazingly
swift boy, and what with him and Sherlock, and
the constant neglect of the hulking persons whom
he employs in his wholesale criminal factory to
close the door, the Professor hasn’t a chance.
* * *
Melba sings like a sky-lark. That was the
first impression given by her pure, limpid voice
trilling in ecstasy through Handel’s “Sweet
Bird ” —an artificial composition which is only
tolerable with good singing, but which a Melba
makes exquisite. The Sydney audience, which
had received the singer with marked coolness,
thawed with this item, but not completely. It must
have been a sincere satisfaction to Melba to recog-
nise that her name, her advertisements, helped
not an atom to the splendidly enthusiastic greeting
which ultimately marked the first Sydney concert.
Every step from indifference to a passionate tempest
of approval she had to sing for, strive for. It was
not until after the last notes of the Ophelia aria
from Thomas’ “Hamlet” that Melba’s listeners
gave themselves up completely to her spell, and ad-
mitted that they had heard the perfection of sing-
ing. The whole gamut of the mad girl’s emotions,
horror, joy, exultant love, crooning sorrow,
distempered mirth, abandon of grief, was
presented by Melba’s voice—a fairy texture of
mist on which were thrown all the passions of the
human heart. The audience listened spell-bound,
eager, breathless, in many a face the reflection of
Ophelia’s ravings showing; and then gave itself up
to enthusiasm. That was theapogeeof theevening;
afteritnothingmattered much. The valsesongfrom
Gounod’s “Romeo and Juliet” was charming in
its melody, wonderful in its display of vocal grace,
but lacked the passion of the other; it did not
give the effect of making the whole air vibrate
with music and feeling.
* * ♦ *
Melba strikes Sydney as a great singer,
beside whom none yet heard in Australia can be
compared ; and, withal, she is an affable monarch
of song: her ways on the stage are superbly con-
fident, entirely graceful. Even when she frivols,
shakes empty hands with humorous appeal to the
audience, she frivols with dignity: it seems a
condescension, not an affectation.
** * *
As for the star’s three-men-and-two-girls
support : Baritone Signor Bensaude sang his first
song well, but without expression, and the effort
wearied him so much that he sang the next very
badly. Louis Arens, who was here with Mus-
grove’s Opera Co. two years ago, sang an aria
from “Der Freischutz ” without restraint. Miss
Davies should develop into a fine player one of
these days, and the little harpist has genius.
** * *
“ Mrs. Goldstein,” at Sydney Criterion,
continues to brighten weekly by means of new
ditties, a pretty item being the “Bose of Killarney. ”
Minnie Hooper shines in the song-arid-dance line,
her only inappropriate song being that of the
coon who had ceased to love Minnie, This should
be sung by a plainer girl. Mrs. Goldstein had a
snarling word or two for Celia Mavis, in act 3,
when she trod on his yellow skirt the other night
and loosened a bit of chiffon. Mr. Sheridan con-
fesses that he does not mind being a woman in
some respects, but he is bitter about the necessary
tightness and length of his beautiful clothes.
Everybody behind the scenes seems to have
designs on John’s gussets; even the calm horse
that wins the race by standing still evinces hostility
towards his accordeon pleating, when he can get
a chance to do so out of sight of the audience, and
wants to stand on John’s train with all four hoofs
** * *
“The Messenger Boy,” now at Sydney
Palace, is a sad illustration of the senile decay
of British humor. It is a woebegone, be-draggled
musical “comedy” with not a decent joke, or a
brightly indecent one, and not a catchy air. If
it were musical, it might be pardoned for not
being comical; if it were comical, its want of
music might be forgiven ; but it is neither. There
are some bright spots. Mr. Fred. Leslie’s sketch
of “Captain Pot” is one of them; though this
young actor has a tendency to inartistic exaggera-
tion at times, he is always lively and his dancing
is excellent. Miss Lilian Digges is given hardly a
show at all, though what little she is permitted to
do is very well done. Mr. George Lyding, on the
first night, was allowed no song in which to
display a tenor voice far above the average ; that
mistake was rectified afterwards, and an interpo-
lated ballad by him is now the best circumstance
of the show. Outside of those patches of bright-
ness there is little worthy of favorable comment.
Miss Rose Musgrove (as a maid, Rosa) and Mr.
Graham (as the Messenger Boy) do moderately
well, their best effort being a Dutch dance; but
they get no assistance at all from the author, and
are not equal to the task of making the play. One
or two ballets, in which Miss Annie Cobb leads,
do something to shore up the show, and the
dresses are superb. If the experiment were tried
of a re-allotment of parts, making more use of
Miss Digges and of the very promising young per-
son who played “a Pell-st. Girl” in the last produc-
tion, there would be, perhaps, some hope in “The
Messenger Boy.”
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Sandow, the Wiltons,
and the Fawns are the chief attractions, b 1 whilst
Sandow shows exactly the same arms a 1 lifts
the same ironmongery, the Nawns have a new and
very bright sketch. An imitation of Sandow by
one of the Wiltons is more amusing than the
genuine article. On Monday afternoon the man
of mighty muscle gave a private view of his sinews
to doctors and pressmen. Dr. Camac Wilkinson,
after testing his heart, certified that it was in
perfect condition and not in the least affected
by hypertrophy, which is rather common among
athletes.
** * *
“The Toreador,” which had been put
through some trial performances in Adelaide, was
tried at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) for the first time
on Saturday with the usual opening night results
as regards applause. Burdened with more encores
than it could carry, it went lame at the finish. As
a spectacle, assisted by a brass band and some
vigorous chorus effects, the entertainment is
bright and impressive at times, also there are a
few flashes of humor in the dialogue, and an
occasional snappy duet or dance. But, on the
whole, “ The Toreador ” inclines to dulness, partly
because it is full of conversation, and partly be-
cause the method of all the comedians is heavy.
Lauri spreads himself out at ease in his part as
though it were a very droll one, which it isn’t.
Hugh Ward, on this occasion, appears to
be imitating Lauri, and the new arrival,
Reginald Roberts, plays a British military
dude in a labored, mechanical style that length-
ens the performance in its weakest spots.
Roberts’s languid swell is described as a lieutenant
in the Welsh Guards, but he shapes like a con-
torted tourist, and drawls with an American accent,
until at last he comes to be rather tiresome. There
is a tenor singer in Melbourne—Charles Rose, one-
time a member of the Williamson Opera Co.—who
could walk through the same part, eyeglass and
all, without the least noticeable effort, and make
it look naturally humorous. And there were
comedians of yore, such as John Gourlay and
George Walton, who might have been rapidly
frivolous, incisive and amusing in one or other of
the principal characters. A comedy, mostly dull,
and never different to any previous work of the
same co., needs enlivening with touches of ridicu-
lous gaiety. Every now and then the still, small
voice of memory whispers “Where is Harry
Shine?” “What price Tom Wootwell?” and
other remarks of similar import.
** * *
The plot of “The Toreador” amounts to
a fantasia on well-known musical comedy stories.
Lauri, as Sammy Gigg, is the Flipper of the
“Runaway Girl,” before he gets changed into
Biggs of “ The Circus Girl.” Biggs was required
to wrestle the Terrible Turk, and Gigg is under
contract to slaughter six bulls in a Spanish arena,
bull No. 3—a tricky bit of beef with black and
tan spots on him—having been specially trained
to attack the toreador unexpectedly. Hugh Ward
is a dealer in menagerie goods instead of a circus
proprietor. Carrie Moore, as Susan, is simply
Flipper’s faithful Alice over again, and she and
her Flipper Biggs Gigg perpetrate a cheap
burlesque of a scene from “ Sherlock Holmes” to
the delight of exactly the same people who
applauded their other burlesque scenes. Some
people find nothing but weariness in these eternal
Lauri-Carrie Moore mimicries, which are not even
very clever of their kind. Harold Thorley, Maud
Chetwynd, Laurence Hanray (out of his element),
Rose Hamilton and Maud Chetwynd help to
point the resemblance between the present show
and its predecessors. Florence Young, as Dona
Teresa, a gaudy Spanish spinster of ginger
character, comes to hand rather late in the first
act and shakes things up musically and otherwise
whenever she gets a chance. The new lady (Miss
Lulu Evans) plays a character that seems to have
been dropped into the broken narrative at the
last moment. Miss Evans is a plump little lady,
and a tasteful little warbler, who will probably
improve on acquaintance. Her principal duties
this time are to masquerade as Maud Chetwynd’s
husband in a knickerbocker suit, and sing the
prettiest song of the evening as pleasingly as her
voice will permit. Two or three more of such
songs, and a generous curtailment of the talk
might improve “The Toreador” considerably—
perhaps. The ways of musical comedy are so
mixed that it is easier to prescribe physic than to
guarantee a cure for a sick patient.
** * *
“Charley’s Aunt” is being revived for a
week at Melbourne Princess’s, and if that historic
she-male gets sufficient inducement, she or he
will, no doubt, consent to be revived for one week
longer. Frank Thornton’s season expires on 31st
Oct., therefore the nights of “ Aunt ” are drawing
to another periodical close in any case. Thornton,
nowadays, is a more convincing old woman, and a
less genuine-looking young man, than he used to
be, but the'non-skirted Thornton is a compara-
tively unimportant feature in the proceedings.
Furthermore, the shortcomings of the com-
pany clustered around Aunt matter very little to
the good old farce. There are only ten characters
all told. Two of the ladies —Miss Eva Macdonald
and Miss Mercer —take the minor honors, and
Fred Cambourne is a neat edition of the college
scout, whereas one of the undergraduates has an
exasperating habit of smiling with his lower jaw ;
and Joseph Wilson plays a baronet from India
like a handsome pretender from coster climes.
His cockney accent defies dissimulation. A
splendid accent for knocking ’em in the Old Kent
Road, but it hasn’t a ring of truth amidst present
surroundings.
** * *
At the Bijou, the ponderous elocutionist
from “ Mars ” is droning out his gospel of Other-
dom to an unregenerate Parker, and Parker, under
compulsion, is earning the gratitude of a poor but
attractive Polly out in the snow, as before. “ A
Message from Mars ” has been blessed anew by
the local clergy, so The Bulletin herewith
exhorts Christian Endeavourers to come forward
and assist the good work—at popular prices—
before Friday of this week, when Parker will fall
a victim to Otherdom for the last time. On
Saturday, a new and original farce, by G. W.
Elton (son of “ Billy ” Elton), will be produced
for the first time. The title of the experiment is
“Oh ! Mr. Penny Love,” which doesn’t sound
like another moral lesson.
** * *
The Fitzgeralds are putting up another
galvanised-iron house adjoining their circus-
building at Princes Bridge, Melb. It takes the
place of the menagerie tent that last year got
blown to shreds at a cost of several hundred
pounds for brothers Tom and Dan. This new
fixture is calculated to withstand every sort of
storm except the tempestuous rage of Lizzie, the
elephant, who is getting such a big girl now that
she could boost her way through a brick villa resi-
dence. The circus starts next Saturday with new
electric light, and a blaze of astounding attrac-
tions. !
** * *
Paul Cinquevalli, the original Sultan of
Jugglers and Shah of Equilibrists, took the floor
at Melbourne Opera House, the other night, and
started to make business hum again. Business
had not hummed for a week previously, but the
advent of Cinquevalli brought a rested populace
back to Rickards’s without further delay. The
juggler has seemingly lost nothing of his skill and
grace since the first time he twined a wreath of
cannon-ball around his manly neck, and caught
the affections of a Melbourne audience. Moreover,
his strength remains unimpaired, judging from
the dog-cart he trifles with. Also Walter Burford,
the humorous element of the show, has added con-
siderably to his comic relief work during the three
years that his rueful countenance has been roam-
ing. Willie and Charlie, a pair of remarkable
acrobats from Paris and other places, made a first
appearance last Saturday. The rest of the bill is
chiefly composed of the Sydney Drews and Fred
Poplar.
## * *
Only a sparse crowd at Melbourne Royal
on Saturday night, when Maggie Moore, billed to
“create the name-part” in “Peggy Peters,”
cheerily carried out her ‘ ‘ creation ” contract, re-
marking casually that, though business seemed
slack, she meant to be as happy as she could.
Perhaps it was a mistake to advertise “Peggy”
as a domestic drama. The typical Royal patron
gets all the domesticity he wants, gratis. Besides,
the production in question is not so inordinately
domesticated as to demand a label to that
effect. The cast, for instance, includes no
chee-ild reciting its prayers in the night-
gown of stage innocence. The deserted wife of the
villain refrains from staggering home to die on
the snowy doorstep of her old home. On the
contrary, when her husband is safely jailed, she
settles down to a snug grass-widowhood at the
expense of the friends of her youth. With all its
faults, this is a chirpy little American play
brightened by two quaint bits of character study
— viz., Peggy and Peggy’s pa, a shrewd old
Yank, convincingly depicted by J. P. West.
With the exception of a coon (Lance Yane) and a
village constable (J. F. Forde), the other charac-
ters are stereotyped, and get better treatment
than they deserve from the company. The name
character, as created by Maggie Moore, is a large
lump of a girl, vulgar, self-sacrificing, cunning,
pert and amorous all in one. She is oafishly in
love with a bashful young farmer (Vincent Scully)
who suffers from an engagement arranged by the
old folks between him and Miss Osgood Moore.
The latter, however, discards the shy agriculturist
for a surveyor, and that sinful estate-agent con-
spires with the blushful hero’s wicked uncle to
cheat Nevvy out of coal-seams and railway con-
cessions rightfully appertaining to the dear old
farm. Peggy’s habit of going through the mail-
bag in her Pa’s post-office defeats their plots
but, in return, she is barred in a mill, in company
with a faithful coon, and set fire to by the vil-
lains. The victims escape by sliding down the
mill-shoot, but the shoot was a tight fit for
“Peggy” on first night, and it looked as if she
would be done brown before she could get enough
way on to slither down the switchback. In the
end, the timid farmer endeavors to get his arm
round Peggy’s waist, with only partial success,
the plump heroine having grown no slimmer, in
spite of foreign travel and Parisian modistes. The
play ends with everyone, but the gaoled villains,
starting to live happily ever afterwards.
♦
Melba deserves well of her compatriots for one thing, at
all events. She did not sing “Home, Sweet Home,” at
her opening concert—an act of forbearance worthy of the
highest praise.

State finances for September, 1902, so far as
they are available (with certain reservations as regards
Westralia, the telegrams about the month’s finance being
muddled and none too intelligible):

The Queensland figures look absolutely shocking, but the
explanation is simple enough. As a concession to the
pastoralists the payment of pastoral rents has been post-
poned for two months, therefore there is a decrease
under this heading of nearly £259,000. Under other
headings, in the aggregate, there is an increase, and the
pastoral item is only postponed—unless the Government
finds an unlooked-for difficulty in collecting the money
two months hence. N.S.W.’s increase during September
arises almost wholly from the tariff.
So far as the financial year has gone results are
as follows:

New South Wales has £377,646 more revenue from the
tariff than it had during the July-September quarter of
1901, so this accounts for more than the whole increase ;
under other headings there is a decline. The decline
of Queensland’s revenue, as already explained, arises from
the postponement of pastoral rents. South Australia’s
trouble lies in the great decrease of its railway revenue,
which has slumped to the extent of over £29,000.

So far as New South Wales is concerned, these results are
sufficiently ghastly. It is the only State of the three
with a very large increase of revenue and at the same
time such a huge increase of expenditure that the deficit
is worse than ever. It would be interesting to know
what kind of revenue would keep the See Government
properly on its feet.
The only other important point is loan expendi-
ture, under which head, so far, complete returns are
available from two States only :

At latest advices the N.S.W. loan fund was overdrawn by
£4,229,459 —in other words, that amount is taken out of
the trust funds. Even with all the money that can be
borrowed in Australia, and reckoning the instalments of
the last loan still to come in, N.S.W. will need another
big loan in December or January to keep it off the rocks.
And already its credit has slumped so heavily that its 3
per cent, securities are lower in London than those of
Victoria, South Australia, Maoriland, or even Natal, and
another big N.S.W. loan is likely to send them still
lower.

* * * *
Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run saiely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*

A revolution in smelting furnaces seems pro-
bable. The aid of gasoline has not been recognised.
Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, supplies
forms to suit assayers, which are marvellous in their
efficiency.*

* * » *
The great cost of compressing air for rock
drilling, especially for small mines requiring to use only
two or three drills, has hitherto prevented mine managers
adopting machine drilling. The introduction of the
Gardner Electric Drill has removed this objection, small
mines can now be equipped with machine drills which
will do as much work as the air drills, and at the same
time provide the lighting of the surface and underground
workings at less cost than for air drills alone, while the
cost of operating them is less than one fifth of air drills
doing similar work, and the necessary power being so
small oil engines can be employed. Usually, however, any
mine using machinery will have sufficient boiler power
available to spare the three or four horse power required
to operate a couple of electric machine drills, and so
reduce their working costs and save time. The Gardner
Electric Drill is in use on many mines, and a proved
success ; the Austral Otis Company of South Melbourne,
who are the Australian agents, will be pleased to give all
information to anyone interested.*

The best investment in Australia is buying land
at Esperance, Western Australia, the natural port of the
Kalgoorlie goldfields. With a railway, Esperance will
become one of our busiest ports, as the trade with the
goldfields will pass through it. A permanent survey is
now being made. The railway is a certainty. Get in
early and make a fortune by purchasing a few blocks
before the great rush sets in. Price, £2O block; terms,
£1 a month. Write for plans and particulars to The
Perth Land Company, 101 Swanston-street, Melbourne.* .

A mild reefing boom is on in M.L., owing to
recent rich finds in the Bunker’s Hill and Hauraki mines
Coromandel. The district is famous for its rich patches,
and both mines seem to have got on fine streaks of ore
Shares which a few weeks ago were to be bought for a
few pence are now eagerly sought at as many shillings.

* • • 9
N.S.W. State Treasurer Waddell has been
figuring things out. He says that the resumed area of
Sydney and the country lands repurchased for closer
settlement will not only pay interest on cost, but leave
enough over to pay off, in 40 years or so, the money bor-
rowed for the resumptions. It isn’t in the least degree
likely that this surplus will eventuate, but if it does
N.S.W. may rest assured that it will never get a chance
to accumulate if Waddell is left at the Treasury, or if
Oarruthers is there either. What is the good of talking
about what will happen if the surplus isn’t stolen or
diverted, when the surplus is dead sure to be stolen or
diverted ? It is as safe to leave a hyena alone with a fat
baby as an Australian State Treasurer with a sinking-
fund that isn’t chained down.

cover all losses arising from the realisation of the re-
mainder (£68,857 nominal value) of the foreclosed proper-
ties which fell into the society’s hands in the days of the
great collapse. Assuming it to be all required for that
purpose, the actual reserves are only £4006, but the con-
tingency fund may pan out a surplus. The Langridge is
an eminently sound building society, trading on about
£lOB,OOO of its own funds and only £10,549 of deposits. It
has no bank overdraft and no mortgage on its properties,
and is earning a shade over 5 per cent., while the earn-
ings show a steady increase. The great essential is that
it doesn’t relapse into the old habit of paying 9 and 10
per cent, dividends, and that it aims at having a £50,000
reserve-fund, instead of the utterly inadequate £7866
with which it was content in the days of the last boom.
* * * »
On Oct. 7 Adelaide Stock Exchange held its
morning meeting without any business whatever result-
ing. This is not an absolute record, but it has not hap-
pened before since the days when the Broken Hill boom
had declined, and Kalgoorlie as yet was not.

The Citizens’ Life Insurance Co. (Sydney) has
been struck with a verdict for £650 damages, and a
lot of costs, through one of its officials publishing a
libellous document in the course of a business wrangle.
There have been three trials over this same matter, and
by this time costs, which was quite an obscure animal at
first, must be about full-grown. And the end is not yet.

The reserves consist of reserve fund £160,000 ; re-insur-
ance fund, £90,000 ; and undivided profit, £24,817. This
is a splendid record for a company with a paid-
up capital of only' £64,628, the reserves being now
considerably more than four times the capital. No
Australian fire office has ever put up anything like these
results. The revenue from interest on investments alone
is £15,237, which in itself is sufficient to pay over 23 per
cent, interest on capital. The only drawback about the
South British co., from an investment point of view, is
the immense and utterly ridiculous liability on its shares,
which are £1 paid-up, with a call liability of £lB. It
would require an enormous amount of fraud, disaster or
bad luck to turn the South British co. into a call-making
institution; but the liability—an utterly unnecessary
liability—is there all the time, and it isn’t a pleasant
thing to look at on paper. If the South British were
turned into a co. with, say, £100,090 paid-up capital and
no call liability whatever, it would be better for all
parties concerned.

have only eight more to pay, it will most likely be better
to hang on to your co. You will probably get your
money back, but very little more ; still, the better terms
the A.M.P. would give you would, in all probability, be
counter-balanced by the loss of your two years’ premiums.

The Federal Palace Hotel still plods away at the reduction
of its big mortgage—£ll2s paid off this year reduces the
burden to £56,375. But as the mortgage is reduced the
bank overdraft seems to increase, so that things are just
about as broad as they are long, or even a little broader.
The following is the situation before the dividend was
paid in 1900 and again in 1901; also the situation as it is
now:
Owing on Less Bank aw„i
Mortgage. Credit.
July, 1900 .. £59,000 £459 £58,541
Net Over-
draft.
„ 1901 .. 67,500 404 57,904
„ 1902 .. 56,375 1976 65.351
The insane 21J per cent, dividend paid last year has a
great deal to answer for. This process of reducing the
mortgage by increasing the bank overdraft is un-
satisfactory. It would be much better if the manage-
ment decided to pay a steady 4 or 5 per cent., and
dropped even that in bad years, instead of having an
occasional sensational dividend, with a period of extra
deep gloom to follow. And it ought to be a fixed rule
that, if there is any profit at all, the total liabilities will
be at least a trifle smaller each year than they were the
year before. A total debt of £58,351 requires a lot of re-
moval, and no time is too soon to seriously undertake
the work.

year out of its cheap-jack shows, it holds its ground rent-
free, and makes practically no effort at its improvement;
so it ought not to be hard-up. Then, why the beg?

North Cobar shaft down about 500 ft. It is
intended to crosscut both ways at that depth in the hope
of finding a continuation of the Great Oobar lode.
Opinions differ as to whether the shaft is deep enough.
Nothing whatever was done on this property for over 20

Reported that the Crowl Creek mine, Shuttleton,
near Nymagee, which has been held under option by a
Melb. syndicate since the beginning of the year, will shortly
be floated into aco. Extensive prospecting work has been
done which has revealed a great length of high grade ore.
Understood that the purchase consideration, £30,000, will
have been netted out of ore won in development work.
With copper at about £5O per ton, and with all the dis-
advantages of the location, surely this is a record ! Pros-
pectus when issued ought to be attractive, but the public
are likely to have the poor satisfaction of being notified
that all the shares have been allotted.

A battle royal has been raging between local
and Sydney directors of the Cope’s Creek Tin Dredging
Co., Inverell, owing to an effort being made by the former
to remove control of co.’s affairs from Sydney. Stated
that the local shareholders outvoted Sydney, but were
beaten on a legal point. Correspondence between the
two sets of directors was decidedly' warm, the gem of the
controversy being a local man’s suggestion that it might
be advisable “ to shift the dredge to Sydney and use it
for any other purpose than winning tin.” There are
several dredges in N.S.YV. which would be more likely to
succeed as gold-winners in Sydney harbor than where
located.
* * * *
Inverell diamond-mining lacks lustre at present.
Inverell Diamond Fields Co. of London—4oo,ooo shares—
has closed down. Co. has been an ignominious failure ;
tens of thousands of pounds have been spent, and not a
four-shilling diamond for each share has been produced.
The far-famed Monte Cristo mine (?) was submitted to
auction the other day without eliciting a bid. Five years
ago Captain Rogers is said to have refused £500,000 for
the show. It would make you laugh to look up the
rhapsodies of the Sunday Shellback concerning that
show.
• * • *
There is a great deal more meat in the N.S.W.
Mines Dept, report for 1901 than usual, though the
delivery is late. Also, though it is served excellently, it is
good meat only in places. Coal alone, in fact, saved the
situation last year. But for that the State’s mineral out-
put would have slumped over a million ; as it was, the net
slump was £617,341. The coal people never before got
within £400,000 of their total of £2,178,929. Their
tonnage output (5,968,426 tons) was never so great; and
since ’92 they have not had so good a price. In ’9B the
average was ss. 4d. ; last year 7s. 3d. That is for the
whole State. In the northern (Newcastle) district it was
Bs. 4d., in the western 4s. 9fd., in the southern ss. 3Jd.
But, while there was a big jump in the average price,
it only applied to Newcastle ; the western district
actually averaged ljd. less for its product than in
1900 and the Southern district 2d. less. Newcastle
averaged 2s. more. The most productive half-dozen
collieries were—Newcastle 370,167 tons, A.A’s New
Winning 362,922, Wallsend 320,086, Metropolitan 297,036
Mb Kembla 261,350, Seaham 255,052.' After that South
Bulli, close up with 246,361. Aggregates for the three
districts—Northern 3,999,252 tons, 9157 work people •
Western 424, 720 tons, 535 men ; Southern 1,544,454 tons’
2499 men. Coke production shows a splendid improve-
ment in late years—in ’96, 2635 tons, worth £21,850 •in
1901, 128,882 tons, worth £105,665. Certainly there wasn’t
much of a jump last year from the point reached in the
previous year; but, in view of the metal slump which
affected smelting, it did well to even hold its own.
Silver-lead, an easy first in most recent years
comes this time after coal. Total value, £1,959 021 or
£828,429 less than in 1900. For this knock-out the
market slump was responsible. But the waste in the
industry is appalling. After they had taken out all they
could, the “ refuse ” from the Broken Hill mills last year
contained 5,861,1380 z. silver, 50,029 tons lead, and 143,776
tons zinc. Of every 20s. in the ore they get out now only
about ss. The 15s. is the margin which the chemist and
engineer have to work on. Unfortunately some of the
co’s are sending down stuff still rich in mineral to fill up
the workings. The Broken Hill mines, though they don’t
rely much on timber nowadays for permanently staying
worked-out ground, put in 13J million feet of Oregon and
used 180 tons of explosives underground.
Copper production, like the market, is most
erratic. In 1883 it got up to its top, £577,201 ; in three
years it had slumped to £167,665 ; recovered again in a
couple of years to £275,034, and by ’93 was down to
£58,426, which was the worst since the sixties. Last year
it was £413,302, of which Cobar proper supplied £192,989.
A new mining inspector, a man of much practical experi-
ence, was put in charge of the Cobar district during the
year, and these are his main conclusions “ More tanks
wanted, and especially deeper tanks. Firewood and mine
timber are becoming exhausted ; hence, for the outlying
mines, railway connection is a vital necessity. Wood-
fuel is burned in a reckless and wasteful way. Few if
any mines are equipped with wooden economiser plants
to make use of the waste gases, and little or no attention
is paid to boiler and engine efficiency.” The struggle is
getting keener, and modern plants and men of the highest
attainments are now essential to success.
The gold yield, £921,282, was the poorest since
'93 ; still it was three times better than that of ’BB. (From
about ’B7, when Broken Hill began to talk, there was an
extraordinary revival in N.S.W. mining. In the three
years ’B4-5-6 the State’s total was £8,599,576 ; in the three
years ’9l-2-3, £17,253,565 ; in the last three years,
£18,735,014.) So far as the department is able to find out
the average yield is 9dwt. lgr. per ton. But the whole
gold statistics are unsatisfactory. Cobar gave 42,2990 z.
of the total of about 267,0000 z. (crude) ; and Wyalong’s
little lot was 21,7170 z., as against 44,6750 z. in ’99. At the
end of the year the dredging plants were of the declared
value of £289,333, and the 43 of them had managed to
find £89,628 worth of gold—cost of doing it is, of course,
not stated.
• * * *
B.H. Central (Sulphide Corp.) has had its first
underground collapse ; but at time of writing little can
be said of it except that, though serious, it will not affect
the output for longer than a few days necessary to
strengthen the workings near the collapsed section. This
mine is worked on the “ block ” system, which it was
claimed was theoretically perfect, in a word or two the
lode (very wide and regular here) is divided into 50ft.
sections. After a drive has been put along each wall of
the lode at a deeper level than the one hitherto worked a
block 50ft. wide is attacked. The ore is taken out from
the bottom, and as they work up they fill underfoot with
mullock (shot down through passes from the surface),
leaving a closely timbered gangway on either side of
the block. The mullock filling is, of course, kept to within
working distance of the roof (from 4ft. to 9ft.) When
the 50ft. block has been taken out right up to the level
above and mullock has replaced the ore, the next 50ft.
section is attacked in the same way. By being worked
upon like this the mullock filling is consolidated, and on
each side of actual operations there is always a 50ft. body
of either ore or filling, with gangways at convenient
intervals. Thereis nevermore thanafew feet of open space
(a few feet, that is, in height). So far, for upwards of
four years, the system has worked without a serious
hitch. Now, something started to go somewhere, as
with a castle of cards, and there followed a collapse
in that part of the mine. Only three men were at work
there, putting in mullock ; two of these were caught,
and, of course, killed, and their bodies may possibly not
be recovered for months. With this system in vogue,
however, any disturbance, though almost certain to be
felt right to the surface, is not likely to be felt over a
wide area. A collapse would be vertical rather than
lateral; and that is a distinct advantage, because it is not
so likely to affect the shafts.
* • « *
To set Zeb. Lane’s coal-briquetting works going
at Bunbury (W.A.), using up the friable stuff from Collie,
1200 tons of Scotch pitch was brought out, in bulk, under
special charter. The pitch started in cubes, but was
melted in the tropics, and on reaching port had to be
dug out. The plant has had a trial run ; it can handle
120 tons per shift. The briquettes weigh about 151 b.
each.
• • * *
Golconda, Lake Austin (W.A.), again recon-
structed. Of its 100,000 shares, 90,000 paid up to 17s.
have been allotted pro rata to holders in the old co. Co.
originally floated in ’94, and started crushing in July ’95 ;
6859 tons treated for 14,8520 z. gold, worth £53,000.* Ex-
penditure totalled £96,750; one div. of Is. 6d. per share,
or £6750, paid in ’99. Despite this expenditure, no work
was done below 200 ft. level, which is highly significant of
the style of management. Co. holds 90 acres adjoining
the Island Eureka, and, if properly and economically run,
should pan out better in the future. Since resuming
operations, the reef has been cut at the 256 ft. level, 2ft.
wide and worth 30dwt.
• « * •
Westralia Mt. Morgans has now got its second
30 head of 15001 b. stamps ready for starting, but when
they will start is a moot question. The lode down to
300 ft. level is pretty consistent; running up to 70ft. in
width, and worth, according to returns, nearly 25dwt.
per ton. At 460 ft. level there is no lode at all going
north, and it is not too good going south. At 500 ft.,
nothing but a stringer has so far been located in the cross-
cut, although it has been carried 100 ft. past where the
lode should be. Latest battery return is 3491 tons, 40200 z.
—very satisfactory, if continuance could be relied on.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) is now carrying
the main shaft from the 900 ft. to 1100 ft. level. Down to
500 ft. levels were opened up every 100 ft., but, below that
depth, intervals of 200 ft. are allowed. To get rid of the
tailings, a tram-line is being erected to the Great Boulder
South, nearly a mile away. The Perseverance’s quarterly
div. of 10s. per share, equivalent to a distribution of
£87,500, was payable on 14th inst. The oxidised ore is
now exhausted, so that the sulphide mill will have to
produce about IOOOoz. extra to maintain output.

Shareholders in Bottomley’s Associated Finance
Corporation are promised returns from their alleged
gold mines, the Associated Mt. Jackson (W.A.) and the
Trafalgar (W. A.). Circulars have been issued to the effect
that a cable from the manager of the former, dated July
21, stated that “ mill trial very satisfactory ; commencing
at once three shifts daily,” and from that of the latter,
“ 200 ft. level assays run Bto 9oz. ; potentialities Under
foot unknown." The latter is artistically vague—it may
mean anything or nothing. The Mt. Jackson has been
worked on and off for a dozen years without result, and
the Trafalgar for half that time. Since the issue of the
circular no further news from either property has been
forthcoming. Significant, however, that Bottomley has
been circularising shareholders to take up a batch of
unplaced shares.
* * * *
The new find at Edjundina (W.A.) is still
strongly in evidence, the test crushing of 2400 z. from 10
tons from the Queen’s Birthday having again focussed
public gaze on the field. Govt, has arranged to
survey a town site, and Cobb and Co. are now running
bi-weekly coaches between Kookynie and the find.
Boulder Hamilton is pushing down three shafts on his
acquisition, the Wallaby, in order to have the reef
thoroughly tested before the expiry of his six months’
option.
* * * ,
Paddington Consols (W.A.), recently acquired
from the Standard Exploration Co., has made a very
inauspicious start. Work underground was hardly re-
sumed when a serious fall of earth occurred. The 40'head
battery was erected on the hanging wall of the lode, and
until its removal, the underground workings will be a
veritable death-trap. Co. is operating in a very half-
hearted style, and apparently cash is a scarce commodity.
On Associated Northern (W.A.), a second trial
bore has satisfied the management that pay ore exists
below present lowest level of 500 ft., and the sinking of
the main shaft is therefore to be shortly resumed.
Up to date the mine has produced about 65,0000 z. of gold
from 12,000 tons, a record of developments, which few if
any, other mines can show. In addition some 1500 tons’ of
B°z. ore are now awaiting adjustment of assays. Co has
paid one div. of 55., equivalent to £87,500, and, besides
having its original working capital of £lOO,OOO intact, has
a very considerable magin of cash in hand to meet con-
tingencies, or distribute in divs. Co. has ordered a 20-
head battery much on the lines of the Ivanhoe’s, and
should shortly make a start as a regular producer.

* * » *
Victorian retrenchment policy is being carried
to an absurd extreme. At Fosterville, near Bendigo,
Government sank a prospecting shaft 400 ft., and started
to crosscut the country ; 114 ft. had been driven, and it
was estimated that in 100 ft. more the lode channel would
be intersected. An expenditure of £2OO at the outside
would complete the work and determine definitely
whether or not there was pay ore at that depth, yet at
this critical stage instructions have been issued to shut
down. Fosterville is the field on which three cos., work-
ing on large, spurry formations, make profits on ore which
in bulk is not worth more than 6s. per ton.
• * « »
After years of negotiating, Black Horse and
Egerton co’s (near Ballarat) have agreed to amalgamate.
The two co’s have paid over £500,000 in divS., but latterly
have been under a cloud, and, owing to unreasonable
jealousy, or failure to make a common-sense business
arrangement re the cost of draining, development work
has not been decently advanced. There are enormous
quantities of low-grade ore in both mines, which might
be made to pay if the new co. would erect an up-to-date
100-stamp mill. The equipment on the mines is old-
fashioned, capacity of the batteries probably not being
more than two tons daily per stamp. The Egerton mine
provided the most expensive and sensational law-suit in
Australian mining history. The case of Learmonth v.
Bailey figured in the Vic. law courts for years, and all
sorts and conditions of people, from bank managers to
parsons, were involved in it. The allegation was that
certain of the owners of the mine discovered a large body
of rich ore and concealed the fact from their partners,
whom they bought out at a low figure.
* • * *
An English co., promoted by expert Rolker,
has been doing big prospecting work on the supposed
southern continuation of the Garden Gully reef, Bendigo.
The property adjoins the Great Southern mine, which
occasionally figures largely in the div. list. Rolker’s co.
sank a shaft 1400 ft., and at every 200 ft. below 400 ft. put
out crosscuts to what is known as “ centre country.” So
far, nothing of anv importance has been revealed, but it
is stated that the indications in the bottom crosscut are
the most favorable that have been met with, and it is
proposed to sink shaft 200 ft. more. This is the model
shaft of Bendigo. Property is bounded on the south by
the general cemetery', part of which was bought in boom
time of ’7l in graveyard allotments for the purpose of
mining. An area sufficient (for surface equipment was
thus acquired but nothing further was heard of the
scheme. Writer has an idea that a more likely place for
prospecting work would be on the opposite side of Back
Creek going towards One Tree Hill.

Only in Victoria, still under the monstrous
shadow of gloomy, savage Calvinism, could it
have been decided in the 20th century to wait for
the birth of Mrs. Shngal’s child, then snatch the
suckling infant from her breast and hang her by
the neck until phe is dead. The horror of the
foul crime which'the wretched woman committed
will be utterly overshadowed by the deliberate
brutality pf the law’s act —an act in which every
member of the community will be a participant.
The outrage agairfst the sentiment of maternity
—a sentiment which even protects the wolf
in whelp except against the' wholly callous ;
the long-drawn-out ferocity at the bedside, with
the hungry hangman eager to push aside nurse
and doctor and claim his victijn—will be .of more
mischief to the community than could be done by
a, hundred Mrs. Sangals. The" waiting, the
watching, then tjie writhing woman and the
wailing infant—the hideous horror of it will breed
in the hearts of the people unimaginable thoughts
of cruelty. If Victoria hangs Mrs. Sangal the
State will pay the price of the butchery with a
hundred future atrocities.
Such an execution, The Bulletin asserts, would
be impossible in England, or America, or France,
or Germany. It would even be impossible in any
Australian State save Calvin-ruled Victoria.
There is no English precedent for it for a hundred
years; nor is there a single Australian (convict-
time or other) precedent for it since the morning
when, at the behest of an ice-blooded lag-day
official (who, sh.ameful to relate, afterwards became
N.S.W. Chief Justice), Mary Brownlow’s gaol-
born infant was torn from her breast that the
mother might expiate, at the hands of the hang-
man, the death of the husband whom she had
killed in a casual quarrel, with the weapon nearest
to her hand.

An inquest on the body of an old man a<»
George-street Asylum, Parramatta (N.5.W.),,
again draws attention to that hideous institution.
The old fellow was aged 72 and threw himself
from a second-storey window on to the asphalt
beneath. He was described as being in the last
stages of senile decay and yet was forced to live
on the second floor of the old barrack with some 30
steps between himself and the ground. He had
his choice of a tomb on the second storey or one
below ground, and took the latter.

If poor dry South Australia has to part
with everything else besides her debts, she
has made up her mind that her Dignity shall not
go. Wherefore this happened in the Leg. Council
t’other day :
The Hon. A. B. Addison asked if the Attorney-General
(Hon. J. H. Gordon) would intimate to his Excellency the
Lieut?nant-Governor that it is desirable in future that
when a person brings a message to the Council from his
Excellency he should be properly attired. Last week a
message was brought by a person wearing a great-coat.
The President said he thought Mr. Addison need not
press his question, as he was able to give him an assur-
ance that it would not occur again.
A message brought to a Legislative Council by a
person wearing a great-coat! Now we know w,hy
earthquakes have happened to S. Australia.
* * • •
“Veteran”: In connection with the
Drayton Grange business, we have heard a lot
about the difference between regular and irregular
soldiers. It would be interesting to get a precise
definition of this difference. Both sorts are
armed, clothed, drilled alike, and are apparently
liable to the same punishment if they don’t
behave themselves. One distinction, however,
seems to be that the regulars are looked after, by
their officers, and the irregulars are not. We
used to be told that the Australians in S’Africa
“ never complained.” They most likely knew it
was no use. The regular is always ready to com-
plain, and if he can get no satisfaction from hjs
captain, he goes to his colonel, and, in the last
resort, to the commander-in-chief.

At a Sydney W.C.T.U. meeting—

Marvellous, isn’t it, what acute perception some
people are gifted with !

Onß of the stalwart brothers of the unfor-
tunate Lieutenant Witton is in Melbourne jiJst
now, obtaining signatures by the hundred to
another petition for tlxe prisoner’s release. The
injustice of Witton’s sentence was clearly shown
in the fact that the court-martial recognised his
comparative irresponsibility for the killing of any
Boer prisoners other than the one man whom he
(Witton) shot down in what was practically self-
defence. And, as “the petitioners humbly
submit,” there was no reason (save British foreign
policy) for assuming “criminality in a young and
inexperienced lieutenant, with no previous experi-
ence in the field, less than a month with his corps,
less than a week with his detachment, placing
faith in and yielding obedience to the distinct
assurances and positive commands of two superior
officers having vastly better means of knowledge,
and with all the advantage and power of rank
and authority.” If the Witton petition to the
King is ever allowed to get there by the military
authorities in London, the amiable young giant
who was condemned to imprisonment for life will
certainly be released. If not, the petition may
be closely followed by a demand.

For the past year or two the returned
trooper has been a boon to the Maoriland poli-
tician. The most inveterate billet-hunter could
generally be staved off by the plea that all
vacancies were reserved for those who had borne a
share in the honor of upholding the Grand
Old Flag. Now, however, the excuse is about
used up, for the returned trooper is there in his
hundreds clamoring for billets that can’t be found.
Meanwhile,, a humiliating reaction has s.et in, and
the'papers complain that a decent funeral is not
possible for»a dead trooper, even his own comrades
forgetting to attend.

The contingenting business doesn’t pay,
either in cash or gratitude. For instance, this is
a sample of the literature published about us by a
Jingo paper in S’Africa :
They are all tarred with the same, murky brush, the
Australians, the Tasmanians, and the wretched Norfolk
Islanders. . . . I have been reading the-cables of*“The
Crowdted Transports” business, and the amount of useful
newspaper space given to these carping, crying, vulgar,
vicious Australian malcontents, fairly sickens me. Who
and what are these people that they should receive more
attention, more nursing, more petting, more comforts,
more coddling than Scotchmen, Englishmen, Irishmen,
or Welshmen ? This pampering is repugnant to people
with any manliness or decency in their composition. But
I am satisfied that the Australians are feather-bedders,
growlers, grumblers, curled darlings, and no-account
men. They were cracked up—by themselve.s —during the
campaign, and now they want to travel first-class, and be
feasted on chicken and champagne for evermore. Great
Scot! Who and what are they after all ? Well, we have
all heard about Botany Bay ! We know their breed.
Their progenitors were not wanted in the United King-
dom. They used to sing :
True patriots we, for be it understood,
We left our country for our country’s good.
And now, not a few of the descendants of these bad, old
chain-gangers are crossing the Indian Ocean for South
Africa. Of course, they are a sample of “ the true
patriots.” ... I have travelled throughout Australasia,
and I know the people, and I know little to their credit
outside the boodlers’ bank books.
* * • *
General Hutton, despite the striking out
of the provision for Adelaide drill-instructors
from the military estimates, wishes to keep them
in S.A., but apparently Bill Lyne has pledged
himself to their departure. The men were not
sent to Adelaide because they were wanted there,
but because they had been cut off the establish-
ment in N.S.W. and Q., and Gen. Hutton did not
know what else to do with them.
* * * *
There are persistent rumors that the
“ absolutely competent and distinguished ”
General Hutton finds his position as Federal
Commandant intolerable, and that, after “reor-
ganising” the Australian army into chaos, he has
decided to throw up the sponge and go back
whence he came. Minister Lyne, “on Aug. 5,
sent a minute to Gen. Hutton, stating he could
not agree to any proposition giving control of the
troops, actual or implied, to anyone outside the
Commonwealth.” That was a cruel snub, for
Hutton has taken it into his head that it is his
duty to make Australia furnish an auxiliary force
at the call of the British War Office. The army
which Gen. Hutton has now has no legal exist-
ence. There is no law in Australia empowering
him to punish for a breach of discipline, and if one
of his soldiers stepped out of the ranks and
“landed” him in the eye, the only remedy open
would be ordinary police-court proceedings. Even
Minister Lyne seems dimly to see the absurdity of
the whole thing, and what Lyne sees is pretty'
obvious.

Speaking at Sydney W.C.T.U. Conven-
tion, t’other day, Miss Lucas mentioned the diffi-
culties a virtuous, but destitute, Sydney girl
encounters in search of a shelter. On a recent
occasion, the speaker affirmed, a girl had applied
for admission to the Union boarding-house, and,
when asked if she had applied elsewhere, she
said she had been to seven different homes
without being able to obtain shelter, though, if
she had been “a fallen sister,” she could_have
got immediate accommodation. The irony of it!

A St. Kilda (Melbourne) constable was
shot dead in the street on Sunday forenoon by a
man (supposed by some to be the same who killed
policeman Guilfoyle in Sydney), whom the officer
was pursuing on a charge of molesting a young girl.
The murderer then walked about peacefully think-
ing things over, till he saw another policeman
rushing for him, whereupon he shot himself.
Judging by his actions he was an experienced
criminal of longstanding, and when the body -was
overhauled the wages of sin were found in the
pocket as follows:
A metal watch and chain, pieces of lopk, one barrel of
an opera glass, a bottle and a tin of vaseline, a skeleton
keyp a wooden pipe, a saddler’s awl, a bottle of nitrate of
silver, portion of a metal watch, a thimble, a purse con-
taining 25., a -New South Wales railway time-table, a
chisel llin. long, a two-bladed knife, a packet of sulphate
of copper, a sharpening stone, and a file. There was also
a clipping from the Argus of September 18 referring to
attempt to shoot Constable Davey at Prahran
for which offence a man received li years.
Sin, unless pursued in a large intelligent way, is a
dead failure.
» • • •
“Adelaide” : The earthquake par. in B.
4/10/’O2 does not do justice to the electrical effects
produced in saying that 300 subsoribers rang up
the Exchange forthwith. Everybody rang up as
soon as the shock was over. But quite 300
shutters fell in the Exchange at the moment of
the quake, and as a consequence of it; also, the
fire-brigade was automatically “called” at the
same moment by every alarm in town. The
modern habit of weaving electricity all round and
through a city has great possibilities of surprise
(and possible mischief) when the ordinary condi-
tions of Nature are disturbed.
« * * *
A North Terrace (Adelaide) doctor hap-
pened to be examining a man for life assurance
purposes, when the earthquake came along, and
applicant opened a door and bolted. He shot into
an adjacent drawing-room, where the medico’s
wife was entertaining some ladies, and the sudden
apparition of a half-naked man added to the
panic. Quite a number of people forgot their
clothes in Adelaide that day.
» * * *
Consequences of the earthquake are
gradually working out all round Adelaide. A
suburban town-hall is found to be so badly cracked
' that all engagements made for letting it have been
cancelled. Also a sensitive person whose nerves
went all wrong, and who begins to hear things in
the night, advertises that he has bought a
revolver and will shoot wildly at any nocturnal
marauder.

The Agapemonites, who have
been recently “tearin’ round” in
London, and about whom we have had
cables, are followers of the late Brother
Prince, an ex-Anglican parson, who
claimed all kinds of divine powers.
He managed to wheedle much money
from his rich converts, amongst whom
were the sisters Nottidge, whose in-
fatuation for “the Beloved,” as
Prince was called, resulted in a
law-suit with scaudalous exposures.
With money obtained from dupes,
Prince built the “Agapemone,” a
charming place, at Spaxton (Eng-
land), where all kinds of mum-
meries, and worse,-were carried on.
In the presence of his second wife
(daughter of a parson of standing),
“Beloved,” with due mystic rights,
ook unto himself in
“spiritual marriage”
a bride, and thus
“living flesh was
to have been recon-
ciled to God.” Un-
fortunately, Sister
Zoe, the Madonna,
was in due course
delivered of a
daughter, and this
was a “ staggerer ”
to all concerned.
“ Beloved ” tried to
explain the matter
away by saying
that the child was
the devil’s parting
gift. However, seces-
sions followed, and there
was shortly afterwards a
general burst-up. Now the curious mixture of
cant and cuddle raises its obscene head again.

During the hearing of a*recent Melbourne
maintenance case it was testified that defendant,
though a sober man in steady employment, spent
upon himself every stiver he earned ; swelling it
around, well-clad and well-fed, while his miserable
wifeancbehildren were actually trying to support life
upon cabbage-stumps and other refuse. It even
looked as if he made mock of their wretchedness;
for, on one occasion, after leaving them without
food or money for a week, _he turned up
and graciously presented them with a loaf of dry
bread. The drunkard who incidentally neglects
his family is unfortunately common enough, but
such a coid-bloodecjly callous brute as this man, as
far as The Bulletin knows, is an absolutely
unique specim en.

At the recent S.A. criminal session a
“ false pretences ” prisoner, in asking for a light
sentence, said that as soon as he came out he
would try to make restitution to prosecutor.
Light sentence accordingly.'' After which the
Crown-Solicitor applied to have the money found
on prisoner when arrested handed over to prose-
cutor. But the Judge ruled that there was no-
thing to show whose particular money this was,
and he could not dealwith it without consent of
prisoner. Prisoner refused enthusiastically !
“A LITTLE BIT OFF THE TOP."
“ The fact is so obvious that it cannot be a secret
that Queen Alexandra wears a wig. Before the day
of Coronation, the Archbishop of York, or somebody
on his behalf, had to explain that it would be quite
impossible to anoint a wig. The difficulty was
finally solved by cutting out a piece at the top, in
the shape of a tonsure, and when the ceremony took
place, the Archbishop of York poured the oil of
anointing into the hole.”— Argus.
Mb. Deakin : “ Well, Toby, old boy, what have
you got there?”
Mb. Toby: “Ah, see how our Lady Queen
delights to honor the Commonwealth ! This
is the piece out of the Royal wig which she
sends to be a pen-wiper for ever and ever on
the table of Federation.”

Madam Melba’s suite of rooms at the Hotel
Australia has been got up in Manager Smith’s best style ;
and the day before Madam arrived Manager Smith put in
all his time showing the apartments to the many women
who flocked into the Australia to view Melba’s carpets
and grand piano, satin chairs, silver vases, pictures,
flowers, curtains, cushions, and all the other things that
women admire. Madam’s bedroom attracted most
attention, however ; with its snow-white bed (never used
before) the little table for Madam’s Bible, and the dainty
window curtains. Outside the bedroom is a spacious
balcony warmed by the sun of a morning, beautified by
the poetic moon of an evening. Manager Smith has
always a keen eye for the poetic moon and he arranges
with it to shine whenever available without extra
charge.*
W ild’s, in the Sydney Arcade, is the most
fashionable gentlemen’s hairdressing saloon in
Sydney. Every attention possible is paid to the
customers by six expert hairdressers. Address. —
30 Sydney Arcade, entrance from George-street
or King street, Sydney.*
May Vivienne’s charming book of “Tra-
vels in Western Australia,” which is now being so
extensively read in Australia, as well as in Great
Britain, commends itself warmly to readers. The
authoress seems to be blessed with the great gift of
seeing most things in a rosy light and enjoying life
so thoroughly that she imparts a feeling of ex-
hilaration to those who read what has emitted from
her pen. As a great newspaper critic in London
says, “Truly, a most magnetic and remarkable
woman indeed.”*

The bricked-in
building (“School of
Industry ”) in Darling-
hurst (Sydney), where,
for 30 years, there has
been a manufactory of
homeless little girls into
young women adept at
the long seam and the
raising of light pastry,
has been bought by
Mark Foy for a factory
for slops, leaving 50
little maids flapping
their print tippets,
ready to take wing and
establish a hive of in-
dustry elsewhere.
Lady Darling’s ad-
vanced notions found
vent, not in trying to
get women to vote, but
in endeavoring to im-
part the rudiments of
domestic science to the
rising-up Australians.
So, in 1826, a kind of
cheap boarding-school,
where girls were taught
to make bob-courtesies
to their elders and bet-
ters, and to have a
light hand with pie-
crust, was established.
After a muster in
Parramatta, Mac-
quarie-street was their
first settled home, and
old St. James’s saw
them twice every Sun-
day standing at atten-
tion down the aisles,
whilst other miserable
sinners took their seats.
A love of confectionery
was discouraged by a
severe birching if other
children gave them a
fistful of lollies through
the railings. The birch
was an item of each
• quarter’s expense, sand-
wiched between the
Epsom salts and the
nightcaps. Sometimes
an heiress happened to
be lost or mislaid, and
search was made
through the Home for
some girl pretty enough
to replace her, when
tradition says she rode
away and became a lor-
dess. Many perfectly-
trained domestic treasures Have been let loose
annually upon distingushed households front this
quaint establishment, though whether the pupil of
recent times was up to the level of her more
carefully-birched predecessor of many years ago is
not known.
* • • »
Objection has again been taken in N.S. W.
to trades-unionist J.’sP. sitting on the Bench in
bush labor cases. If to them, why not to
squatters, and to storekeepers whose interests are
bound up with the squatters ? The honorary J.P.
system is essentially bad. It puts upon the seats
of justice, on occasions, men whose main business
in life is not to be just, but to make a living—
perhaps out of kow-towing to a section of the
community ; and these men, in some respects, can
wield a power greater than a Supreme Court
Judge, for on certain matters their decisions are
absolutely final, and cannot be appealed against
on of law or of fact. Every man who
exercises judicial powers should have no other
business in life but to be judicial, and should be
paid well by the State to keep his mind, as far
as possible, free from all bias, his knees from all
subserviency, his palm from all bribery.
This extraordinary argument is put forward by
Sydney D.T. on the honorary magistrates ques-
tion :
The privilege of adjudicating is now allowed to the
Justice as a quid pro quo. He performs a number of
duties which, but for the institution of honorary magis-
tracy, would have to be attended to by salaried officials,
at a very considerable expense to the State. In return,
he is granted a certain political prestige, displayed'in
his appearance on the bench as a fully empowered magis-
trate.
The difference between this and the Chinese
system of selling a judicial office and allowing the
successful tenderer to make what profit he can
out of the litigants, is only one of degree.
* * » *
Minister Bent’s detailed statement of free-
pass concessions granted by Vic. Railways Dept,
shows that the issue of passes (worth £9) to
“ distinguished visitors ” has averaged 52 per
annum. Every week in the year some incomepoop
from abroad, whose only claim to distinction was
his cheap air of “wealth and standing,” has been
invited to assert his quality by becoming a dead-
head on the railways. This official practice of
greasing the fat porker dates back to the dark
ages of Australian “loyalty” when the casual
arrival of every globe-trotting British bounder
with a hyphen to his name was accepted as a
compliment to the country he was contemptuously
contemplating through his eye-glass. But in his
railway-pass reforms the Minister is inconsistent.
He takes free passes away from the engine drivers,
but not from the ex-Ministers of the Crown (at
least not at present), from the families of M.’sP.,
or from the M.’sP. themselves when not engaged
on parliamentary business.

Out of seven cases listed at recent Bendigo
Criminal Sessions, five were offences against
women and children. One lad, a Reformatory
protege, 15 years of age, assaulted his mistress,
and then committed an assault on a girl 11 years
old. He got off with a month’s imprisonment,
and two birchings.
One of the dangers of the “painless-ex-
traction ” method pursued by some dentists was
shown by a Sydney District Court action, the
other day, when a patient recovered £l5 for injury
caused by the use of an imperfectly-sterilised
syringe to inject a cocaine mixture. The “pain-
less-extraction ” method consists in the production
of local anaesthesia by the injection of some form
of cocaine, and has other dangers in addition to
that arising from the usp of dirty instruments;
so the method is rejected by most dentists,
though familiar to all who learn the rudiments
of the business. Nevertheless, it is very
popular among people who don’t know the risks
they are running. The Bulletin’s advice to
people whose teeth are faulty—and that is to prac-
tically everybody—is to avoid extractions as much
as possible, and, when they are absolutely neces-
sary, to take “gas.”
How the dentists do swarm in Australia ! A
country town of moderate size will often have six
of them. In Sydney, a dentist is as frequent as a
publican, and you can see 18 of their brass plates
on one building in a comparatively obscure and
out-of-the-way city street. Some streets are
almost entirely inhabited by the men of teeth.
New Sydney Dental Hospital, to treat the jaws of
the poor, has already eight consulting dental
surgeons, 20 dental surgeons, and 30 assistant
dental surgeons, and “there are many other
applications yet to be dealt with.”

This from “ Adipose,” who seems to bean
altruistic person with a Mark Tapley capacity for
cheerfulness :
’From practical experience, can enthusiastically endorse
all “ H.F.” says (4/10/’O2) re the foolish waste of energy in
rigging oneself into a second-rate Sandow. Writer (who
hasn’t the use of his legs, and whose inactivity is there-
fore compulsory) never gets off his chair, goes about 40
inches round the lower chest, has several chins, and only
sees over his cheeks with difficulty. But notwithstanding
these, and the fact that he eats no more in a day than does
the average man at a single meal, for sustained mental
labor he will back himself against any Sandow-person he
knows. As to what harm, physically, may result from
such bodily inactivity, writer can only say that, his
present condition dating from his second year, he
has reached the middle twenties without ever having had
a day’s serious illness in his life. Also, how do Gibbon
and Hume, the historians (not to mention Dumas and his
1200 volumes), stand as bright, particular examples of
fatty degeneration as an aid to mental effort long sus-
tained? Both were vast men, hardly able to move for
their fat. Yet the former lived to 57, and the latter to
65 —time enough in which to give one’s best to the world.
And that’s all one is here for, you’ll find, if j'iju’ll only
think right ; the rest doesn’t matter.
But doesn’t it? The man who merely lives to
“ give his best to the world ” is not in it with the
man who lives to have a good, wholesome time of
it also. Far less than five per cent, of humanity
can hope that their “best” will be of the
slightest use or interest to the world when they
have given it. Why devote a life-time to throwing
water into the ocean ? Physical culture is a good
deal a matter of constitution, but as a general rule
the man who is in good physical condition will do
better work (mental and physical), and have a
happier life than the sedentary person. Of
course, there are exceptions.

There were 70 drunks at Sydney Central
Police-court one day last week. The water
famine asserting itself.
Not a word appeared in either Melb.
morning daily about the musical profession’s
presentation of a silver casket and a gorgeous
address to Melba. The reason for this was that
the reporters took the huff at being stowed away
in a gallery at the back of the hall to watch every-
body else kowtowing to the star. Of course, if
it happened to be a lot of fat city auctioneers and
stock-jobbers who were doing the knee-bending,
the pressmen would have been humbly content to
take a back seat and chronicle the proceedings at
great length. The reporter, however, has a notion
that he is just as good as the average long-haired
musician, anyway, and as the musician is sort of
dependent upon the crumbs of advt., instead of
wielding the financial influence of the “Going,
going, gone ” person, the papers “chuck off” at
him. The journals on this occasion backed up their
representatives with signal enthusiasm.

come from this sancti-

''•'lnable assistance to
tour Melb. dioceses.

From London Daily Nbws :
During his westward trip the King visited Portland
prison, where the convicts recognised him, and gave him
a ringing cheer. Their loyalty was rewarded with an
extra piece of pudding and plenty of treacle.
The reward of “loyalty”—an extra piece of
pudding and plenty of treacle ! Convicts or poli-
tics—it’s all the same.
“ Robt. A.” re Queensland “ Tammany ” :
The poor man’s strait is the rich man’s opportunity;
hence it is only natural that the effects of the drought
and the “ whipping of the cat,” consequent on the reckless
expenditure during the Jingo fever, should lead to the
formation of “ trusts,” which still further accentuate the
ghastly attempts to make £1 do the work of £2. Certain
crates of bananas, for instance, were recently “ ringed ”
up from 30s. to £2 10s., and but for the exposure in our
one daily paper, this staple cheap fruit would jjave re-
mained at that prohibitive price. Still more recently
has this journal exposed the medico-ethical “ combine ”
whereby medical men attached to Associated Friendly
Societies were coerced until broken in spirit and ruined
in pocket. Next, the bakers’ “ trust ” was challenged
and an exposure made of its tactics in increasing the 21b.
loaf from 2d. to 3d. on a falling flour-market, and
threatening a further rise of £d. now that flour is station-
ary. “Dressed” timber, again is at a higher price than
for the past 17 years—a fact due to a “ ring ” organ-
ised ostensibly—(l) “for export-trade”—(there’s none!)
—and (2) to compel architects to use a certain kind of
timber. The biggest “show-up,” however, was that of
the meat “ ring ” as regards the pressure it brings to bear
on the “ small ” man who dares to undersell. At present
chops are “ ringed ” up to Bd. per lb., rump steak to Bd.,
fillet to 9d., and suet to lOd. The cost of the animal is
only 3Jd. per lb., so that the slaughtering and distribu-
tion is responsible for more than the breeding and
droving. One experienced producer asserts that not the
slightest benefit has accrued to his class from the in-
creased price. Again, therefore, the middleman “ scoops
the pool.”

WHEN MELBA SINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Melba sings her sweetest lay
The world is bright that erst was gray ;
A glad surprise
Lights weary eyes:
The woman brings
A charm to cozen cares away,
And we forget
The vain regret,
The sighs, the tears of yesterday.
When Melba sings.
The mellow music of her song
Makes tirbd hearts beat quick and strong.
And cynics smile
For joy the while,
And dead love springs
To life again. The list’ning throng
Are “ feeling good ” ;
They sit subdued,
With lips apart, for just as long
As Melba sings.
