BUCHANAN WHISKY.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 14, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
“Oh, for a fist to push a fancy quill” on the
subject of Melba—her voice, her appearance, and
her platform clothes ! A better gowned or more
fashionable or numerous audience never crowded
Sydney Town Hall than that which faced Melba
at her first concert in Sydney. There were some
shockingly-dressed heads and bodies, but these
were chiefly in the guinea seats. Melba appeared
graceful, smiling, very handsome, at the end of
the first part of the programme; and the audi-
ence received her much as a collection of stalag-
mites would. There was a perfunctory hand-clap
in the vice-regal patch, and here and there in the
hall, but the stalagmites waited for her to justify
the praise that had been blown in all men’s faces
with the common air for so many years. She
justified, even with Handel’s “Sweet Bird.” Her
pure light soprano rippled and purled like laughing
water ; it rose in silver shafts of sound as of bird
answering bird, and the audience came to life, and
proved its possession of musicianly ears and
palms—especially palms. Melba’s voice has a
girlish freshness, which I had not looked for after
so much hard work—the youthfulness you find in
Mendelssohn’s Spring Song.
Melba’s platform-appearance is most attractive.
Her tall, fine figure, almost slender, was clad in a
clinging ivory Princess gown of Mechlin lace over
faint-rose chiffon, The corsage was embroidered
with raised pink roses, her hair, dressed low on
the neck, was clasped with a diamond comb,
which emphasised itself whenever Madame
turned to bow to the audience behind her, for the
platform was packed right back up to the organ
pipes. Her smile, and manner of acknowledging
: the audience’s homage, are in the front rank of
charm ; in fact, I thought Melba even finer than
her voice—if that were possible.
A big section of the audience at Melba’s first
concert got its feet very wet in the tremendous
storm that broke over Sydney on Saturday night.
The dressing-room steamed like a laundry. A
dense pack of people waited on the front steps of
the T.H. to see the star get into her carriage. One
of the crowd was a girl with a fluffed-out mass of
hair. Presently she said to her friends : “There’s
such a smell of burning hair”; they had noticed
it also, and there was a great turning of heads to
see who had brought a tongs and spirit-lamp with
her. The speaker craned her head entirely round,
and it was discovered that it was her Own coiffure
which was smouldering, set on fire by a man’s
cigar. *
Friday (to-morrow) the Mayoress of Sydney,
Mrs Tom Hughes, will be “at home ” at the Town
Hall, to meet Madame Melba. Invitations have
been issued, and a great and gorgeous crowd is
expected to roll up and trample upon its own
draperies, and gather impressions of Melba as she
is off the stage. @
Their Exes, the Rawsons unveiled portraits of
King Ned and Queen Alexandra at the Royal
Naval House, Sydney, last week. The pictures
are large, framed in oak, and signed. The King’s
signature is absolutely anything you like to read
it. If his face wasn’t above it no cryptographist
could make affidavit as to its being Edward R.
and I. The Quoen’s is a beautiful picture—
chiffony draperies and the usual curly wig. It
looks at least 20 years younger than a portrait of
her taken 20 years ago, which stands on the
mantelpiece in the same room of the same Royal
Naval House.
On Tuesday, Lady Rawson unveiled a medallion
portrait of the late matron, Miss Taplin, at the
Infants’ Home at Ash field.
Mr. Rowe, the State Governor’s private secre-
tary, who is making a very slow recovery from
his attack of typhoid-pneumonia, will probably
go to Bathurst to finish his cure. Sir H. Rawson’s
private visit to the City of the Plain (people) was
to secure a suitable curing spot for Mr. Rowe.
A new terror has crept into the life of the
Sydney electric tram-tripper; the terror of the
firl who flirts with the driver of the car, distracts
is attention, says to him “Oh, gar on,” and
causes him to “gar on” and run down funeral
processions, or anything else that is on the track.
An alderman (probably aged about 70, and with
all the giddy glow out of his eyes) has brought
the matter under the notice of a Mayor of a
Sydney municipality, and the Mayor has pro-
mised to see the Railway Commissioners. Woman
is undoubtedly a dangerous human as well as a
ministering angel. Women-passengers caused
the driver of a car to run down the carriage
of the President of the U.S.A., and give him a
bad knee and kill one of his staff. A woman’s
birthday party was the primary cause of a mail-
boat recently going on the rocks. Now woman is
a danger to the Railway Commissioners when she
jumps off the travelling tram (and sues for
damages), but a bigger danger when she sits
giggling on. the driver’s platform dressed in a
sailor hat with a big, brass initial pin in it, a pink
blouse with large blue choux, a tight-fitting,
three-quarter lustre skirt, cotton openwork
stockings, burst tan shoes, a rolled-gold ring or
two, a Chinese glass bangle, and long, water-curl
tendrils about her face. How can the driver help
running down even a Commissioner when such
damsels are asking him how he spells his Christian
name ! The Corns, may have to settle the matter
by abolishing the platform and placing the driver
in a closed cage, from which he can only see the
street in front of him.
A M.L. girl, Miss Ada Sievwright, who was
studying music in London, has been married
there (the cable says) to a Mr. J. H. Davis, of
New York, a reputed millionaire. Miss S. is a
nice-looking girl, well-known in Dunedin; her
father was a solicitor of that city, and once in
partnership with Chief-Justice Stout. Most Ameri-
cans are “reputed” millionaires. In America a
millionaire only needs to own 1,000, OOOdols., which
is £200,000 English money ; so it’s fairly easy.
Some of the best dressing, especially under-
skirting, Sydney has seen, is in “The Messenger
Boy.” When Lillian Digges puts her No. 1 feet
and Italian-greyhound legs into a boy’s garments,
she looks about nine years old. One of the finest
floral trophies ever seen in the Palace was the
harp of pink roses handed up to Elsie Moore in
recognition of her pretty squeaking of the song
“ Maisie.”
Sherlock Hastings, his melancholia, his pallor,
his beautiful black wavy hair, and the secret
sorrow that has preyed on his lean olive cheek and
worked a deep furrow on that cheek only, moves
at the Royal (Sydney) like a great natural force,
such as an earthquake. No human ingenuity need
try any criminal tricks on the man with the
deadly calm, gunboat shoes, and the morphia
habit. Sherlock Hastings is, in one act, dis-
covered sitting on a cushion on the floor, wearing
a red dressing-gown, as he glances over magazines
and puffs, puffs, puffs at his pipe or cigarette. A
violin and bow are by his side, and all the while he
knows what sparrows fall to the ground without,
and which buttons drop from his friend’s waistcoat.
There have been several girl theatre-parties at
each performance, and when Cuyler Holmes wasn’t
on they talked an date chocolates, and, when he was,
their eyes assumed a ping-pong stare, and when,
at the last, he decided to love a woman and live,
instead of dying to his own violin accompaniment,
some of the parties wept—whether with grief or
joy not known. Miss Mabel Lane, as the hand-
some, wicked woman, Madge Larrabee, covers
her wickedness with some good clothes.
Men laugfh and riot till the feast is o’er
Then conies the reckoning, and they laugh no more.
The following melancholy circular now circu-
lates in Sydney :
Dear Sir, —Owing to the Premier failing to keep his
promise with respect to refunding the amount expended
by the committee on street decorations (for the Royal
reception), we are unfortunately faced with a deficit of
£274.
This amount has to be made good by members of the
committee.
The members of the Finance Committee have each
donated £25, and other members have given amounts
down to £5.
As it is imperative the matter should be closed up
forthwith, I should be glad to have your contribution.
If you want any details of the matter, either Mr. Sar-
good'or I can furnish them to you at any time. Awaiting
your reply, I am, yours faithfully.
(Sgd.) Wm. Trotter.
Mr. Julian Ashton, the well-known artist, has
married Miss Edna Morley, a Sydney resident.
Mr. Ashton’s first wife, who died nearly two
years ago, was well known in journalistic circles.
The present Mrs. Ashton is a large, dark, creole-
looking young lady, with, report says, a tendency
to picture-making.
Fewer persons now travelling on N.S.W. rail-
ways carry with them Bath buns, bottle of cold
tea, and packages of bananas, for the Commis-
sioners have instituted a shilling table at the
refreshment rooms. The 2s. table remains for
those with larger purses and appetites.
Brisbane is just now sympathising with Gov.
Chermside and his wife over the loss of their first-
born ; the little life flickered out at birth.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Federal Parliament opened with a
deafening blare of bugles and wealth of pageant.
It prorogued on Friday with little more than a
suburban milk-and-water vendor’s trumpet note.
Subsequently there was a violent scramble to a
Federal Tea Party at Lady Hutton’s. A military
and political element reigned at the ‘Grange,’ and
the golden-haired dame hosted it smilingly, plea-
santly, wittily. For grace she easily heads society,
and with her face and fortune does wonders. It
is hard to say how terrible a time hubby would
have were it not for his charming wife.
“There was a fierce feminine rush for Senate
seats at the close of the season, or is it session ?
The whispering in the galleries and the rustle of
skirts at last irritated big, plain Tom Playford,
who remarked to a fellow-member in a Hibernian
sotto voce, ‘There ought to be a sessional order
against the admittance of women-folk. See the
effect of to-night’s gathering. You can’t get the
married fools off their feet.’
“It appears that Melba has to guard her-
self against all manner of cheap nobodies who
try to crowd into her dressing-room and about her
exit doors, and at her third Melb. concert this
precaution accounted for the drollest of scenes.
A certain dignitary and his wife are always
to the fore in battling through all kinds of
door-keepers, usually by the aid of tips. On this
occasion the ponderous identity came through the
Mayor’s private quarters from the balcony end of
the Town Hall, and found a watch-dog hanging
on to his door who was beyond tip. The attendant
hung on like grim death, but the intruder proved
the stronger of the two. So the attendant sud-
denly let his end of the door go, with the result
that the dignitary fell sprawling on the ground,
with a bump on the back of his head as a souvenir.
His spouse stepped over his prostrate body,
evaded the attendant, and succeeded in gaining
the much-coveted audience with Melba; her
troubles about Popper!
“ Marshall Hall, the terrible—who, that heard
it, can ever forget the wildly exuberant, extrava-
gant, passionate tornado of words that he swept
over Melba’s head at the Conservatorium kow-
tow ? It was a hurricane of emotion. His long,
lean body was buttoned up closely in a blue sac
suit; his loose, lank locks wore a dishevelled look.
There was a hot passion in his face. He was at a
dizzy pitch of excitement. The wild panegyric
was delivered between brief, quick gasps. The
black, shaggy brows lowered and lifted all the
time, like mental pedals. The eyes shot up looks
every now and then at the electrified object of
adoration. Melba was palpably nervous, not
quite sure whether to smile or give serious atten-
tion. She was faced by an educated Dan O’Con-
nor. Occasionally, a demure shake of the head
negatived a too-tropical compliment. Heaven
only knows what the keen, clever woman thought
of it all! The students who were gathered around
wore expressions of solemn joy. Their master’s
genius subdued them quite. The mighty man
at last made a long bow to Melba, gave one of his
Titanic laughs, and brought his wild Pegasus to a
halt. The Star was silent. And no wonder.
“ Despite all the talk about Melba and her old
chums, her private entertaining was confined to
the very Nicest. Three great dinners helped to
make things merry and delightful for immediate
relatives and a few 22-carat guests; Old friends
and all the once-or-twice-removed cousins were
sent tickets for the concerts, where they had the
picked seats in the hall. Melba seems to have had
a Better-Not-Wally to advise her in these delicate
distinctions.
“At the Mayoral reception in Melba’s honor,
Mayoress Gillott, new to her land legs, received
the full flood of guests with a tired air, despite the
orthodox greeting smile. His Worship, on the
other hand, showed how he had been licked into
shape socially, and tried his hand and tongue at a
hundred high-grade graces.
“An ogre in gold lace was the first to seek a
flower from Melba’s trophy at the Gillott spree.
After that the tide set in, and Melba tore her
glove in a frantic effort to give the flowers and get
rid of the rush. In the end, she dragged the torn
kid off, and, with a pair of handy scissors, nipped
off the wired Iceland poppies. Then she pleaded
honest fatigue, and the trusty Musgrove, with
Rupert Clarke in tow, gripped the star’s arm and
sent heralds ahead to clear the way. A large
foreigner stood on the illustrious one’s train. She
stood still and waited. The lethargic person
apologised, and made a profound bow, but forgot
to move. ‘ You’re still on it! ’ said Melba, laconic-
ally, and then the hoof was raised, and the pro-
cession moved on.
Melba, at this Mayoral ‘crush,’ had a decided
look of Mdme. Dreyfus. Our diva has certainly
something Semitic in her face. Melba’s Frenchi-
ness she probably owes to her Parisienne modiste.
Gallic art was conspicuous in the creation of
creamy tulle over pale-blue silk, worn by her.
Every detail of the toilette cunningly added to
the wearer’s apparent height and slimness. The
long French waist, the sequins running to narrow
points down the skirt, the ‘ handkerchief sleeves,’
falling scarfwise over the elbow, even the hair in
a long ‘figure of eight’ coil, all drew one’s eye
downward, and suggested length, not breadth.
Her coiffure was a blend of old Greece and
modern Paris ; a chaplet of green leaves crowned
the smooth dark hair in classic style, while a small
diamond comb topped the knot at the back.
“The Cup carnival is not going to see the
squattocracy in any special Solomon glory this
year of drought. Leading drapers have given
their‘hands’ holidays, an unprecedented slump
in the stitching market having set in. What
with the dry season and other things, the dressy
class have had their pockets drained. ‘ The
squatters always made things brisk for trade at
Cup-time,’said one dress oracle, ‘but, this year,
not 20 per cent, of the orders have come to us, and
we are giving workers a “ vacation," instead of
taking on fresh hands.’
“Weather or retrenchment affected the Caul-
field Guineas attendance on Saturday, and the
general outfitting. Few fashion - plates were
abroad. But oddities abounded. An eminent
golfer not long from the Big Smoke tricked forth
her ample form in a Crimean-shirt costume.
Another she-tourist wore slate-grey, with a slate-
grey complexion. A stout little party, heading
Melbourne’s richest, dressed up to her damson-
plum cheeks with an indigo-blue gown of great
gorgeousness. One luminous turn-out, blazing
with a full battery of electrical silver, struck a
garish note. Trim, taut, and built up to the day’s
barometer was Mrs. Stewart M‘Arthur’s black
voile, an emerald silk gamp, and emerald mil-
linery lending an honorable mention to the
outfit. Trainer Macdonald and his new bride
were en suite— in all grey. Dunky Gillies,
bell - topper over his ears, haunted certain
feminine parties on the Stand. Perhaps the
State Speaker-elect will have a partner this
year, after all. Dunky’s getting notions matri-
monially, they say. Everybody, almost, comes to
mates some day.
“Vice-regal parties played the amiable at
Saturday’s meeting. Lady Tennyson was rigged
in sapphire-blue with lace ‘motifs’makingmusical
margins _on her wide collar. Next door, the
State Vice-Regals made merry. They paid a
‘command’ visit to the first-class V.R. depart-
ment. Lady Silverlocks looked bright and quite
smart in a magpie blend. The little dove of the
household was also in a spruce magpie mixture
with gold embroidery. Gov. Clarke’s tie was an
eyesore. It was a soft silk washed one, scarfed
baggily.
“ How to grow old elegantly was the lesson to
be learnt from the radiant appearance of Judge
'Hartley Williams and his upstanding dame at the
Guineas. The bench, salon and pulpit philoso-
pher was the very pink of white-haired perfection.
None of the young dandies was as dapper as he,
none so bright-eyed, so rose-cheeked. And his
canary gloves won the cake by a shade. Nor did
any of the other swagger women boast a figure
to come within measureable distance of Lady
Williams, in white silk and voile gown with
ginger laces ruffling about the shoulders.
“The straight-front corset makes its wearer
look utterly unlike a woman ; but, as the genial
Schopenhauer remarked, ‘women are ugly, un-
built creatures at best, and any change in their
figure must be for the better ! ’
“ The elections are over, and men sigh dismally
as they allege reasons for the unlooked-for rejec-
tion of tried and trusted patriots. One of these
victims, who represented a country district, traces
his fall to his having accidentally smashed a clutch
of pure Wyandotte eggs, entrusted to his care by
a constituent. Another very high Parliamentary
personage, now down, had been in the habit for
years, one hears, of choosing in the city, millinery,
etc., for the ladies of his rural electorate, accord-
ing to directions and funds supplied by the country
mice. But the shopper is growing"old, and his
tastes are out-of-date. So he has had to give
place to a younger man with a more up-to-date
idea in ribbons and feathers.
“Artist Nerli has been showing, in Mr.
Summers’ studio, a life-size portrait, in oils, of
Dr. Marshall, of the Scots’ Church. It is a good
likeness of the thoughtful-looking Presbyterian,
but the good doctor, ye ken, looks oddly out of
place among all the marble nymphs and undraped
little chubby cupids that smilingly surround his
picture.
“ Melbourne loses a picturesque figure : Father
Kantoupolos—the handsomest Greek we have
ever had in this part of the world —now
returns to Jerusalem. The Greek flock and
the refined Raphael-like Father have fallen
out over some monetary questions, and the faith-
ful, regarding their shepherd as only a spiritual
guide, do not like his interference (well-meaning
though it be) in their temporal affairs. Hence
the tear-away. Father Kantoupolos’ presence at
a State function has always been looked upon as
highly ornamental. The high, white brow, the
aquiline nose, the gazelle eyes, the olive skin, and
fine silky pointed beard made a beautiful picture
head. Then there were the black, graceful robes
of his order, the light figure, the soft movements.
All the girls adored in the distance, and romanced
about the Raphael priest.
“The two sons of the late John Wallace
boasted two lovely wives in the crowded first-night
‘ Toreador ’ audience. One Mrs. Wallace is
large, handsome, and of the magnolia-flower type
of beauty. The other is tall, fair, substantial,
shapely, and of the arum lily order. Both young
matrons dress superbly. The Japanese theatre-
cloak made its summer debut that night. Two
smart women flourished the new order of shoulder
cover, a light, unlined Japanese silk, of jacket
shape, the sleeves belling from the elbow. Wreaths
of roses, marguerites and vine-leaves might have
been seen in many heads of hair.
“A billed baronet—Sir Edward Mackenzie —
drew a packed house to some tableaux out
Essendon way t’other night. That suburb seldom
sees a baronet, and much interest attached to a
special tableau in which the noble bearer of the
noble title was to appear in a state of suspense
standing over ‘ A Sleeping Beauty.’ It turned
out to be a very giddy tableau, and another frisky
one was ‘The Judgment of Paris,’ when three
sweet sirens ogled the beauteous baronet for the
apple. This was followed by a moral tonic in the
form of ‘O Divine Redeemer,’ sung by a devout
contralto.
“Sharp, clear, strong, frosty, was Miss Vida
Goldstein when the Athenaeum platform held her-
self and a little grey gentleman from Sydney, a
Mr. Gandon, in debate. S’MacEacharn, shining
with rich respectability and scented soap, umpired
the play. Daintily-dressed Miss Vida rattled off
her shots with a dispatch that held the audience
breathless. With hands and knees visibly vibrato,
Mr. Gandon started to plead his anti-cause. But
male barrackers at the back of the hall bombarded
the grey little man with questions, and the
whole of his allotted time was taken up with
a hesitating return fire. A poor debater,
and unprepared for interruption, he was suddenly
brought to his seat by the umpire’s announcement
of ‘time.’ Then Miss Goldstein, sharper, clearer,
colder, frostier than before, dealt with her
opponent’s feebly-put case, and absolutely sat on
him, and snuffed his light. She grows daily less
attractive in the feminine sense, but more admir-
able from a platform standpoint.
“ The women at that Suffrage joust badly
wanted to know all the evil of Mary Wollstone-
craft’s tenets, hinted at in shocked and startled
tones by Mr. Gandon. ‘ Does the audience know
what this infamous woman taught ? 5 The audience
sat up in its collective chair, thirsting for the
teachings of poet Shelley’s best girl. ‘Do they know
who she was, what her theories amounted to ? ’ the
husky little man asked indignantly. ‘ Then I’ll
tell them.’ The audience was in a state of high
curiosity. Their expectant air alarmed the
speaker, and he said in a low tone of disgust,
‘ No, I couldn’t! ’ The audience collapsed, disap-
pointed. No wonder only a dozen hands voted
for him at the crisis.
“There is a sudden and inexplicable outbreak
of baby-adoption by Sassiety dames. The other
day a leading matron, married to a N.S.W.
squatter for many years, landed from a Conti-
nental jaunt with a two months’ infant in her arms.
‘l’ve bought a baby,’said the dame, buoyantly.
It was a simple, every-day child. News also
reaches Melbourne of the adoption of another
baby in Paris by a very wealthy Western
squatteress. Are babies coming into vogue again 1
And must they be simply adopted ?
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Sundry Maoriland Irishmen are wroth
with Premier Seddon, in that, though he made a
tour of Ireland, he never once uttered a sympa-
thetic word for the cause of self-government, and
was the guest of some of the most obnoxious
landlords. Whereat a Bulletin correspondent
offers to lay odds that when Seddon lands he will
make sympathetic reference to “dear old Ire-
land,” followed by a tribute to Irish hospitality
and concluding something like this : “ They want
our land policy there to settle the people on the
land.” This will evoke sundry “ hears, hears,”
and at the ensuing banquet Seddon will sing
“ The Wearin’ of the Green.” Which will about
fix things for the elections.

AN/EMIA.
You can always tell anaemic men and women.
They’re pale, weak and languid. They have backaches and headaches.
They can’t eat—or they can’t digest what little they do eat.
Their unstrung nerves kill sleep—their temper is irritable—their vitality vanishes.
And it all comes from poor blood and unstrung nerves.
Banish anaemia at once by enriching your blood and toning up your nerves with
Dr. Williams pink pills for pale people.
They bring good appetite, sound sleep, bright spirits, and perfect health.
They’re death to all debility.
“My blood grew watery,” says Miss F. Rea, Jasper’s Brush. “My appetite failed. I was so weak
that I often fainted. Despite medical treatment I grew thinner and weaker. Two boxes of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills gave me an appetite and strengthened me. Six boxes cured me.”
Sold by retailers and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d. post free.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
It is announced that parson Tremayne
Dunstan is coming back to Sydney to explain
everything.
After 42 years’ experience of political life
in Victoria, Duncan Gillies, at the late election,
had his first walk-over.
On 24t.h Oct., King Edward will, it is
cabled, hold at Buckingham Palace the largest
investiture of honors on record. Honors are very
easy, nowadays.
Mrs. Robert Shield ( nee Rebecca Cook),
who died at Molong the other day, claimed to be
a grand-niece of Captain Cook, and the claim was
well supported. She died at 84.
T. Bent is now quite a dude, and swaggers
in a frock coat (artfully cut to disguise his drop-
chest) and the glossiest of Paris hats. The Argus
rejoices with a holy joy over the Minister’s
“ improved appearance.”
Alleged by an indignant Federal member
that in N.S.W. Mounted Bifles there is one officer
for every three privates. This should be looked
to at once. Quite a number of the officers have
an intelligence which is barely equal to looking
after two privates.
Frank Anstey, one of the ablest writers
and speakers in the Vic. Labor movement, easily
topped the poll for E. Bourke Boroughs. The
vote was the largest ever reoorded in any Vic.
election. The electorate is the largest in the
State, having over 10,000 on the rolls.
Ethel Turner, on the word of her pub-
lishers, is “ the most widely read of any Australian
writer.” Since 1894, when-“ Seven Little Aus-
tralians” was printed, nearly 250,000 Ethel Turner
books have been sold, and their merit has been
recognised by a large edition in the Dutch
language.
Sandow asserts that his little Nibs of
Wales (nee Duke of York) is a muscular personage
with a “beautiful back,” as the result of follow-
ing the System. The Yorklet certainly had a
pltysical culture compartment fitted up for him on
the Ophir, but he probably used it for smoking
purposes.
F. C. Mason, ousted from Vic. Speaker-
ship is, like, N.S.W. Speaker McCourb, a North
•f Ireland man. Mason began his Victorian
career by defeating the political heavyweight
Archibald Michie; at that time he was the
youngest member to claim a seat. He was
originally a schoolmaster.
The fixed blush which adorns Premier
Irvine’s face is an apt motto, or figurehead, or
danger-signal, or coat-of-arms, or something for a
Government which, by reason of its skilfully-
eoncocted Factories Act catastrophe and its in-
clusion of Bent, has every reason to be perpetually
ashamed of itself.
MeIb._ARGUS on Labor leader J. C. Wat-
son and hie influence on Federal legislation :
The supreme fact is the dominating- personality of Mr.
Watson. He has suggested and shaped most of the legis-
lation ; he has governed the Government; parties and
Houses, sometimes reluctantly, but with a sense of his
irresistible power, have done his will.

“There is no denying the financial trouble. It has to
be met, and the public servants cannot fairly claim that

Harness again offered to Duncan Gillies,
the Glasgovvite. He has been nearly half a
century in Australia and most of that time draw-
ing screw from tlie State, and brazenly arguing
that men should be self-reliant, strike out for
themselves and wait on no Government. Duncan
drew state pay before Berry pocketed a Ministerial
shilling.
Died, the other week, an old Melbourne
identity in Edward Duckett, who started business
as a farrier, in Bourke-st., in 1849. Duckett and
John Pascoe Fawkner built the first hansom cab
Melbourne ever saw, and, having gained much
profit and renown by that pioneer vehicle, the
former expanded into an ironmonger and lived
happily ever afterwards.
The man who was sentenced for threaten-
ing to shoot M.L. acting-Premier Ward,
and who was afterwards released on condition
that he left the State, has just returned to M.L.
with the avowed intention of contesting a seat in
Parliament. In granting pardon, M.L. Govt,
overlooked the small matter of mentioning the
duration of his absence, so the man returns, and a
flabbergasted Cabinet can only swear.
Newspaper advance paragraph :
There is some probability that the Federal Government’s
representative at the Great Durbar at Delhi, at which the
King is to be proclaimed Emperor of India, will be Mr.
Malcolm M'Eacharn.
It is a poor sort of bone for a gilded potentate
who aspired to the High Commissionership, but
it is a bone. And it is about “upto ” the Sydney
Burdekin of Victoria to make a splash among
niggers.
Colonel Stuart, late acting-Commandant
•f the S.A. military forces, who is retiring on a
gratuity, is believed to have been unpopular with
Major-Gen. Hutton because he gave information
to the press on matters which the public had a
right to know of but the administration wished to
keep secret. Lieut.-Col. Lyster (the Drayton
Grange hero), who tills Stuart’s place, is endeavor-
ing to win promotion by holding his tongue very
- teadfastly.
THE SANDOW CRAZE.
BRRFY LADY: “I could measure with ’im any
day, but I wouldn’t be so indecent.”
Mr. Michael Scobie, the Maitland cen-
tenarian, whose portrait appears in this issue, cele-
brated his hundredth birthday, Oct. 3. Mr.
Scobie was born in Stirlingshire, Scotland, 3rd
Oct., 1802, and left for Australia with his family
in June, 1839. After a short stay in Sydney he
settled -down on property in Maitland district,
where he at present resides. Mr. Scobie enjoys
fairly good health, and his eyesight is good. He
does not smoke, and is a total abstainer.
David Syme is now quite a social butterfly
in Melbourne, and maybe seen hobnobbing with
“society ”at many a “function.” Which, possi-
bly, accounts for the idiocy of the remark in his
paper that in the Federal Parliament “ there was
a boisterousness and want of manners in the
N.S.W. members that grated on the finer feelings
of the representatives of other States.” In the
days when David used not to be a “dainty
affettuoso,” the Age was a better democrat.
At a recent sale of old books, documents,
&c., in Melbourne, aparchment, that was expected
to run up to a big sum, was finally knocked down for
a miserable 3s. This was a fan-simile copy of the
address presented by colonists of 25 years’ stand-
ing to Alfred of Edinburgh when this country
suffered his visitation. In the long list of
pioneers were the names of Ed. Henty, Francis
Henty, ’34 ; John Pascoe Fawkner, ’35 ; all these,
and the loyal associations to boot, went for 3s.

executive disowned all sympathy with Meudell’s
crow, and the bantam sec. sent in his resignation.

was a prominent feature of last Anglican' Church

have advanced the cause of justice and truth much

As a matter of fact the Vic. Labor Party (untruth-

Considering the number of miners in Vic.
how rarely does a man from underground seek a
softer “pitch” in that State’s Parliament! Not
one offered himself the other day for the Ballarat,
Bendigo, Castlemaine, Clunes, Gippsland, and
Ararat sea.ts. In fact, there were altogether only
two candidates who called themselves miners.
Stawell turned dog on one of them, and Dunolly
on the other. In N.S.W., on the other hand, at
least a dozen men have gone straight from the
mine to Parliament, and are still there, and, in
proportion, still more in Queensland.

Edward Holds-
worth Sugden, mas-
ter of Queen’s, the
Wesleyan denomi-
national college of
the Melbourne Uni-
versity, is a versa-
tile don. He is a
classical scholar,
local authority on
English literature
(in which he has been
lecturing at the Uni-
versity since Alexan-
der Sutherland’s
death) a student and
lecturer on Assyrio-
logy, Egyptology, and
the corpses of other
dead ’ologies, and a
music-enthusiast, who plays in a heavenly fashion
on the ’cello. He has made the instrument moan
beautifully under Marshall Hall’s baton. He
stood by Marshall Hall during the famous heresy-
hunt, on which account the Wesleyans have
cherished a distrust for him ever since. They
suspect him of having a soul more than half-an-
inch high, which is the regulation denominational
size.
Premier Seddon made no reference to his
Ministerial colleagues in any one of his speeches
in England. He mentioned the late John
Mackenzie twice, but that was all. Speaking at
his native place, St. Helen’s, Lancashire, he re-
marked, with characteristic modesty : “ I am the
white-haired boy .of the women in Maoriland. I
have been in power ever since they had the fran-
chise.” Later on, Seddon informed the audience
that since the M.L. women were enfranchised,
the doors of Parliament had been closed to all
men of doubtful character.
A day or two before he lost his billet
Speaker Mason desired Melbourne Herald to
mention, “for the information of inquiring
friends,” that a certain Captain Mason who was
on his way back from S’Africa was not his
(Speaker Mason’s) son. That would have
been sufficient information for inquiring friends,
anyhow, but the proud father went on state
that his son—Captain Mason—had written hi ;
latest letter from the “country seat of Lord
Methuen.” The joy of getting a chance to let
“ inquiring friends ” know your close relationship
to a young man who has been lodging with a lord !
“Unitarian ” writes
Your par. about “Parson” Worthington in issue of
Oct. 4th is, on account of its brevity, somewhat mis-
leading. A.B.W. was never a recognised Unitarian
minister. Fact is the Sydney Unitarian church jas, of
recent years, fallen upon evil days. Parson Walters
dealt the first blow when he went out “on Bis own.”
The vacillating Dr. Zilhnan failed to stop the dry rot that
Walters started, and the spiritual Mr. Kemp, sent by the
British and Foreign Unitarian Association, was too deli-
cate for aggressive work. He returned to England, and
left the pulpit t 6 anyone the local committee could
obtain. A.B.W. saw the opportunity and volunteered.
Melbourne and Sydney Unitarians opposed. He got in
and rejoiced. Another breach ; but A.B.W.’s fervid elo-
quence brought others by the dozen. No Unitarian
wishes to exult over a fallen idol; but since the forced
exit to Melbourne the “ alleged ” Unitarians who hung on
his words have mysteriously disappeared from the pews.
Melba, during her Melbourne stay, had
four policemen to her own cheek, and paid 10s. a
day per head for the boon. The policemen were
said to be kept to restrain the criminal classes
from dabbling in Melba’s jewel-cases (the song-
stress is credited with wearing £200,000 worth at
Mayor Sam Gillott’s reception). But there was
another very urgent need for special guardians—
to keep eager, curious, brass-faced people off
Nellie’s premises. From the moment Melba
reached her home the invasion began ; heroine-
worshippers swarmed through the gates and over
the fences, and invaded the grounds—some stony
females even rushed the house —to gaze upon the
tremendous Australian who had plucked the
whiskers of kings. Melba’s insurance agairfst
Melbourne assurance cost her £l4 a week.

)aily Chronicle, for instance
General Lucas Meyer expressed his inability to accept
the King’s invitation to the Coronation. It seemed
likely that he would not survive to attend the ceremony ;
and it is evident that his plea of ill-health was no empty
excuse. In a somewhat ill-timed speech, Mr. Seddon
assumed that the refusal was a studied insult to the
King. General Meyer has taken the most direct method
in his power of confuting that view. He has died.
Seddon hasn’t even apologised to the dead man’s
memory.
Major General Hutton is a religious man.
He told Adelaide Church Congress t’other day
that he desired to see the military forces of the
Commonwealth based upon the great principles of
religion. “History,” he said, “shows how
intimately the religious and military life of our
people are interwoven.” True, true ! snavel and
snuffle-bust; the Bible and the bull-dog revolver.
“Religion,” said the commandant piously, “is a
refining and adjusting force. I commanded 6000
men in S'Africa and didn’t have to punish a single
delinquent.” Picture the 6000 holy Tommies and
their sanctified Commandant spreading grace
around. “Attention for prayers, you lot
of ! ” Booh ! Likewise Yah !
Panmure Gordon, the London Stock
Exchange man who died the other day, aged 65,
was married to an Australian lady whom many
visitors from this end of the world have found a
charming hostess. Mr. Gordon was scion of a
family of “ tone,” had acquired a big fortune, and
Eut on unimaginable frill, as anyone who has ever
ad to cool his heels for an hour while waiting to
see one of the magnates of the firm of Panmure
Gordon and Co. at its city offices can amply
testify. He entertained on a princely scale, was
hand-and-glove with Lord Lonsdale, Kaiser
Wilhelm and co., made a hobby of matters
vehicular, and owned more carriages than the Czar,
and over and over again gave hundreds of pounds
for a dog he fancied. Also, “he had a mania for
clothes, and after the fire at Loudwater, two or
three years ago, it was computed that he had lost
1100 neckties. He had an enormous drawer full
of new white flannel trousers, and another full of
evening waistcoats. He had at one time 500 pairs
of trousers in his possession, and new boots stood
in rows and rows in his cupboards. On one occa-
sion it was said that he had 13 new overcoats
which he had never even looked at, and in one
comer of a room was a stack of about 50 new
sticks and umbrellas.”

Hear the nice, pleasant thud as those who
called themselves “ Gentlemen ” in their nomina-
tion papers strike the bottom of the Vic. poll or
thereabouts: Foster Rogers, Billson of Bogong,
Francis Conway Mason, poor Old Jock Mclntyre,
Outtrim, Dan Lazarus. In fact, save in the cases
of Gillies and Labor-man Tucker, where nothing
much counted, to put oneself up as a mere
“ Gentleman ” seems to have been the deadliest
handicap in the ballot.

The grim humor of the Australian land 1
Lake George, one of the few inland water-sheets
on which this dry country prided itself (its fore-
shores were proposed as a Federal capital site
on account of its extensive and permanent
water ”), has dried up completely at last, and at
present is being bitterly fought for as a—grazing
farm 1
* * * •
The effects of the drought as shown in
Molong (N.S.W.) district. Larras Lake Station
shows only about 50 lambs from 10,000 ewes,
while over 4000 ewes died during the lambing.
The percentages of lambs on other holdings in same
district are in a like ratio or worse; one station
near Cumnock had only 6 lambs from 4000 ewes.
m * m *
Many southern N.S.W. squatters are
buying goats on which to rear valuable stud lambs
—the ewes being too weak to discharge maternal
duties. “Nanny ”is bailed up for a few times,
and soon takes kindly to her adopted family,
often three in number. Sometimes the lambs
show a decided partiality for a special “wet-
nurse,” but she will allow none near her but her
own charges.
* * * *
Latest curse to the man out West (N.S.W.)
is the advent of the fox. Lately several tongue-
less sheep have been found between Nyngan and
Cobar, a sure sign of reynard.
• • • •
How many young ones does reynard’s
vixen have at a litter in England ? A Congupna
(Vic.)_ farmer killed a fine fox and eight young
ones, in a hollow log, on his farm recently.

“ Rap ” : Struck a drougftt-lunatic t’other
day. “Got a field-glass?” he asked. “What
for?” said I. “Why, ter watch that blarsted
sun with. He’s watchin’ me all day long; every
time I look up I see his ole eye winkin’at me.
He’s try in’ to burn me. I know it. If I on’y had
a glass I could watch an’ see wot he’s doin’. Then
at night them dam moon an’ stars come out ter
watch me ; they’ve all got glasses, twinklin’ an’
bobbin’ about. If Igo ter bed in a dark room the
moon watches me through a clink. They’re all
waitin’ till I’m not thinkin’, and then God
knows.” This poor wretch, literally fighting the
universe, was ruined by the drought, and became
insane through watching the sky for signs of rain.
• * * *
“ A.P.R.” : During the last five years the
swagman has become almost extinct, mainly
through O’Sullivan and the drought. The grass
famine has caused stations ordinarily employing
four or five hands to keep a retinue of 20 or 30
scrub-cutters, etc., for years at a stretch. The
first good season will bring wages to bedrock, since
there will be but one-third of the flocks of 1890,
and no station work except burr-cutting. A
mighty rush O’Sullivan wards will ensue. Sheep
will be at fearfully high values after the break-up,
through the great demand for ewes for breeding.
At present stock-trafficking is at a standstill, and
the commission agents are living on their fat.
* * • *
Cane-tops, they now say in Queensland,
make quite as good chaff for cattle as wheaten
hay.
“ Tonbur ” on the drought:
After seven years’ drought the settlers in Gondobolin
(N.S.W.) d.strict have now to face a plague of caterpillars,
which devour the young grass as soon as it appears above
ground. Three brothers came up there from Victoria,
nine years ago, with the respectable capital of £2OOO. To-
day they have nothing but the fences, a humpy, and a
mortgage. Three crops in nine years, and this year no
hope of one. The squatter offered them £lOO to clear
out. In Temora district, a man from the Murrum-
bidgee took up 2560 acres, and started with £3OOO ; he
left recently, after seven years’ toil, with five horses—and
his brother became insane. Another, in same dis-
trict, started dairying with 50 cows ; to-dav he has nine
left ;-and another, out of 1500 ewes, has 150 left. The
chief food of these farmers is now rabbits, and ordinary
meat is out of the question.
In Narandera and Hay districts, things are in a de-
plorable state. Hardly a spring in the grass and the
crops just showing above ground. Shearing is going
on, but many sheep die on the board, and hundreds after
leaving it. Thousands of sheep are being bought at from
, : to 2s. per head ; the “ flesh,” salted down in casks, is
being sent to Sydney to be canned. Needless to say,
various kinds of sheep go in. There is no Government
inspector here.
The stations in Narandera and Hay districts are all
hand-feed,ng, which costs thousands per week, and most
of the se.ections in Hay district are deserted. In Hay
there are mostly untenanted houses, and the pubs
are deserted. The misery in the back country is simply
appalling, and when the returns come in next year they
‘will stagger humanity.” By the way, you very rarely
meet a horseman now, hut bikes are everywhere ; on one
station there are no fewer than 40 machines. Not far
from Hay I met a man leading an equine wreck, carrying
his swag. He told me he kept the poor animal alive upon
damper.
• * » *
“Mac.’ : Owing to drought and scarcity
of employment the marsupial is having a bad time
in Q.. Very many men and boys are engaged in
trapping, poisoning and shooting. Some of the
marsupial hunters must be brutalised ex-wariors ;
last week I found a freshly-skinned native bear
still alive. _ These harmless creatures possess
wonderful vitality, and Heaven knows how long
it would have lived and suffered had I not killed it.

If the drought lasts much longer bottle-
tree and mulga will be extinct in many S.
Queensland districts. In most localities mulga,
in place of being trimmed, is cut down remorse-
lessly, and the effect is already painfully evident.
Iramsloaded with prickly pear, bound westward,
are no uncommon sight in Queensland.

‘ *\Sclinapper ” writes from the north of
South Australia:—
In spite of the daily paper headings—" Break-up of
the Drought,” “ Splendid Rains,” “ Harvest Saved,” &c.,
we are here face to face with the worst times on record.
There are over a quarter of a million acres in this State
that won’t return seed. Hundreds of strippers will never
he taken out of the sheds, and all stock able to travel are
being moved as quickly as possible.
For hundreds of miles there is not a vestige of teed,
the country being as bare as a metalled road, and black
with grasshoppers. A hav-famine, too, is imminent.
Nearly all the hay has either been sent to, or sold, for
Sydney, and there is nothing like enough growing for
this State’s own requirements. Unless phenomenal rains
fall within the next six weeks the wheat in the settlers*
districts will mature, and as most of the crops are
not high enough to cut with a binder the outlook is very
black.
Within 20 miles of Adelaide, where some of the richest
land in the State is to be found, farmers are turning their
stock on to the crops, which have gone beyond recovery
—a fair index to the state of the country in the North.
The Government cry is—“ Make the land produce its
utmost.” The Northern farmers have tried the experi-
ment, and have made the North a barren wilderness. .
Some Queensland stock foods. Jondaryan
i.s feeding 10,000 sheep on prickly pear. Degilbo
is trying to keep 2000 head of cattle alive on
tree, gangs of men felling and preparing the trees
for fodder. Many small settlers on the Burnett
River are feeding their milkers on wild castor oil
plant. Bottle-tree is being hauled to Eidsvold
(where chaff is £22 a ton) a distance of 12 miles,
the cattle being too weak to drive to it. Eidsvold
is 40 miles from the nearest railway station, and
the freight on goods is £1 per 1001 bs.
* * * *
Mount Buller, near Jamieson (Vic.),
5900 ft. high, is always covered with snow in
spring; this season the snow is blood-red in
color, probably through being mixed in falling
with suspended dust particles from the red-clay
plains.
• ¥ * *
A W. Queensland paper tells how a resi-
dent, while fishing in the local creek, saw an
immense cod swimming about. Resident sprang
in, collared the cod by the gills and hauled him
in. Turned the scale at 37£lbs. Miraculous that
a W. Queensland creek, after a four years’ drought,
should have water enough left to float any kind of
a cod.

“ F. 8.” : He “ S.A’s. ” par. (B. 4/10/’O2) :
Fresh water is frequently obtainable, not merely a few
feet from the sandy margin of the sea, but actually on a
sandy beach half-way between high and low-water marks.
I myself have a never-failing well of perfectly fresh water
not 20yds. from the shore of Bass’ Straits, about three
miles N.E. of the Split Point lighthouse, but I have
pretty good evidence that the water comes from the
gravelly hills 50 to 80 chains inshore, and drives the salt
water out before it, dissolving any salt remaining in the
sand and carrying that to sea also. “Discovered” the
well at low-water one hot-wind day when the rest of the
beach was dried up and crusted with salt. I found
several minute “springs” welling up, the apertures of
which might have been made by a knitting-needle, and,
constructing a “ coffer dam” with a piece of 4in. drain
pipe, I found the water fresh. Shorewards I noticed a de-
pression in the clay hills just opposite this point, and on
digging Bft. struck my present well which, even during
the long-continued drought, has remained at a level
midway between those of high and low water. Would
not careful investigation in all similar cases show that
the fresh water only comes from the sea in the usual way
— i.e., via the clouds ?
• » * *
“ M.L.A.” writes :
Re brutality to camels (B. 27/9/’O2) allow me to contra-
dict the par. I’ve examined camels at Broken Hill, ditto
at Bourke, seen them when lying down with saddles off,
and they were in good condition, some of them pig-fat,
and all with sound backs. I was at Pera bore when two
teams came into water - about 70 camels—and I closely
overhauled them as they drank at the trough. Although
some had white patches of hair suggestive of former
scalds, not one had a saddle-sore the size of sixpence. I
certainly saw cows heavy in calf started on a trip, but
they were, comparatively speaking, lightly packed.
Camels are too valuable to the owners and too sacred to
the Afghan drivers to be cruelly treated ; besides they
are fiercely vindictive to anyone who abuses them, and
will use tooth and heel to get square with him.
“ Dave Randall ” : Middle of
’B5, self and mate were sent from
Melbourne via Normanton to Dur-
ham, on the Etheridge, with two
teams. We were bathing in the
Gilbert River, when we discovered
a hole which contained hundreds
of fish. We decided to block the
passage from the hole to the river
with grass, and built a firm wall, a
foot wide and six or seven inches
higher than the surface of the
water, and the fish were securely
paddocked. Next morning we got
there with two buckets, only to find
that the fish had left. They either
climbed or jumped our wall !
Handling jew-fish in this river I got
several stabs from their fins, and
my hands were frightfully swollen
and stiff. The agony for 24 hours
was intense. Hands got right again
in a few days.

“ T.A.D.” : Anyone know the
true derivation of the phrase “ long-
sleever as applied to beer. Heard,
the other day,that Bishop Barker
(consecrated to the Sydney see in
1854) was fond of his long beer.
Hence Sydney’s barmaids were
asked for “a lawn-sleever, please,”
as a compliment to ‘ ‘ His Lordship. ”
That degenerated into “ long-
sleever.” [“ Long-sleever ” comes
from the resemblance between a
pint-pot and a “long-sleeved hat.”
A “long beer” used to be called
in Sydney a “Bishop Barker” —the
bishop was a very tall man.]

“ Nomad,” re shearing tallies—
Sheep are, as a rule, much harder to shear now-a-days
(through the introduction of the Vermont strain). The
following tallies were made by a few Queensland
“ringers” this (1902) season. At YVinton wools;our,
in May, Harry Livingstone (age 23) shore 211 and 213 in
two days ; at Marathon, in Aug., he shore 217, 217, 21f>,
213 in successive days, and, later, he got through 23(1
in oue day at Teleinon, Hughenden. At Guambetook,
Denny Noonan shore 203, 220, 210 in three days of 8
hours. At Gracedale, G. L. Smith shore 201, 213, 209,
220, and 108 on Saturday (half d ay), Same shearer shore
194, 208, 215 in succession at Berkeley. He is also
credited with shearing 123 lambs in four hours at
another shed. W. Livingstone (brother of “Harry”
referred to above) shore 209 and 211 at Winton wool scour,
and 228 at Ayrshire Downs. In ’96, Louis J. Pully shore
33,875 Sheep in one seasoii, his cheque being £338 15s.
“ Jimmy ” Power, probably-the fastest machine-shearer
Australia (or the world, for that matter) has ever seen, is
said to have shorn 40,000. sheep in one season. This man
owns the world’s machine-shearing record of 315 sheep in
7hrs. 40mins.

“H. J. Mac.” : Be the immersion cure
for snake-bite. As far back as ’B9, I set forth, in
S.M. Herald, that immersion in cold water,
preferably a running stream, was an, effectual cure
for man or beast, and cited instances within my
personal experience. Many contradicted, and
nobody agreed with me, so that it is curious now
to find sundry Bulletin correspondents advocat-
ing the immersion-treatment.

Wyom” : Left) a line recently all night
in the Namoi River, and next morning hauled in
a 27ylbs. cod. But he wasn’t on the hook ; that
was in the mouth of a 11b. “ yellow-belly ” which
had been swallowed head-first by the cod, the
latter being then firmly held by the spiky back
fin of the former.

“ J. Mack” : Recently noticed on sand-
banks on the Gwydir River what appeared to be
the nests prepared by the Murray cod for its
spawn. The rim was composed of coarse sand, 3
or 4in. high, encircling a double layer of coarse
gravel, about 3 to 4ft. across, and, as I found upon
examination, two stones deep, with coarse stones
underneath. These nests had evidently been pre-
pared under water for the coming spring, but the
drying up of the river had left them exposed. As
most of them were built on banks of pure sand,
the stones must have been carried into position by
the fish in its mouth. Or, if not, how otherwise?

Driving in to Port Augusta (S.A.), C. B.
Richards, owner of Mt. Ive station, did the 320
miles in five days. The four ponies, half-breds,
drew a buggy,- two men, and a fair load of luggage.
The last day’s tally was 120 miles, and the ponies
were landed fit to go on next day. All this, of
course, over bush-tracks. Apropos fast bush-
going. If the man who has recently stood his trial,
charged with a big mail robbery, really did
the job (the jury couldn’t agree about it), he
must have covered over 160 miles between 7 one
evening and 3 the next afternoon !

“ YV.M.B.” : Be Jimmy All Sin, of Cairns
(B. 27/9/’O2). Said Chow has 800 acres under
sugarcane, and 1200 acres sub-leased to his brethren
in blocks of 20 acres upwards. Employs “ white”
ploughmen, and has over 100 indentured “boys.”
When Lord Hopetoun was at Hambledon, Jimmy
gave a.Chinese dinner to his Ex. and party, and a
couple of prominent white residents. All the other
guests were Chows, including Willie Muy, the
man whom the whites take their hats off to, and
who, not long ago, spent £2OO on a dinner in
honor of his firstborn Chow.

Autopsy on a cow at Quirindi (N.S.W.)
revealed that death was due to a 7in. hat-pin em-
bedded in the heart. The cow had evidently
digested the rest of the girl.

Is Farming - a Failure?
From an Australian Standpoint.
‘‘ls farming, under the climatic conditions
which prevail in Australia, a failure?” This is
a question which many a farmer has turned over
in his mind, and which is likely to receive more
attention in the immediate future. Bile Beans
' for biliousness will, however, insure that a
farmer does not fail because of ill-health. Mr.
William Brown, a well-known agriculturist, of
Maitland, N. S. Wales, has a most interesting
statement to make on this subject. He says:—
“I am seventy-four years of age, and beyond a
few rheumatic twitchings had never suffered
from ill-health till recently, when, I suppose, old
age making itself apparent, I was seized with
great nervousness, trembling, and very bad head.
I tried various remedies, but in vain. My daugh-
ter, living in Newcastle, recommended me to take
Bile Beans, as they had given her great relief
and satisfaction. I did so, and the result is that
lam a firm believer in Bile Beans. They have
given me great relief, and I feel quite a new
man, can rest well, eat well, and am enjoying a
comfort in my old age that I never thought would
be mine, and I feel very grateful indeed for the
beneficial effects I have received from Bile Beans,
and confidently recommend them to old and
young.” Not only for nervousness, but for general
debility, female weaknesses, constipation, piles,
pimples, face sores, blood impurities generally,
spring ailments, anaemia, insomnia, &c., Bile
Beans for biliousness are the most effective remedy
known. All chemists will supply them, or you
may obtain them from the Bile Beans Manf’g Co.,
39 Pitt-street, Sydney, b.y sending price, one and
three halfpence. As their composition is purely
vegetable, in a certain sense they may be said to
be “ raised on the land ” for the workers on the
land.*

“6x8”: A curious suspicion in the
bush, especially amongst shearers, has it that
a man who has no hair on his chest is a thief.
When a swag-strap or anything else is lost a chest-
inspection is commenced. If no hairless chests
are present the man with the least wool gets
blamed. Wonder how this originated ?
“H.G.”: Stump-grubbing is practically
obsolete in Gippsland, where the roots are burned
out by a process which seems in direct conflict
with scientific principles. The stump is lighted,
and when it has burnt level with the ground it is
covered with earth completely and left. The fire
travels along the roots and consumes them com-
pletely. Perhaps this is practised in ether parts
of Australia, but I have never seen it. How
those roots keep alight when there is practically
no air I dunno.

A little bit of Australian history from
Tit-Bits :
It was a kiss
which practical-
ly depopulated
Eden, a town of
some 1,400 in-
habitants, in
Queensland. A
stranger tramp-
ed into the town
one day, and,
after refreshing
himself at a little
eating-house, in-
sisted on kissing
the buxom wife
of the proprietor.
The latter was
present, and
laughed heartily
at what he con-
sidered a fine
joke, for his
spouse was past
her prime and
far from attract-
ive. The stranger
passed on along
his way inland.
Next day the
woman was.
taken ill ; within
forty eight hours
she was dead and
her husband and two children
were dying. When news went
round that a violent disease
had broken out in the town
most of those people- who
could leave hurried away at
once--Some struck towards
the coast, others went in-
land. A few of the latter
passed on their way the body
of the stranger who had
come into Eden and kissed
the innkeeper’s wife. Of
those Edenites who chose or
were forced to remain, more
than one hundred lost their lives through the stranger’s
death-dealing kiss. It was many months ere the epidemic
passed away altogether, and numbers of people will still
travel miles out of their way to avoid the town, which is
even now practically deserted.
There is no Eden in Queensland, there was no
deadly kiss, and the town which doesn’t exist
wasn’t depopulated by the kiss which wasn’t
given. That’s the sort of stuffing the British
goose-public gets from its cheap newspapers.
A tale of Wilga Lagoon
Randall, the storekeeper at Wilga, was
re'uctantlv ending a prolonged spree at the
Wilga hotel, when Mills, the new “ experi-
encin',” arrived. Mills looked out at the
dust.v track and the wind-swept plain—
“Don'tthegwoundhere ever gwowgwass? ”
Randall winked at Murphy. “ Yes, we
had some seven years ago. But now we are
under a curse—perhaps I'd better tell yon,
you're sure to hear of it sooner or later.”
Mills pushed the whisky-bottle nearer
Randall.
“Murphy knows,” began Randall, im-
pressively, “just as much as I do about
this affair. There was a governess at Wilga,
seven years ago, a pretty, fluffy-haired gill,
who made friends with everyone on the
station—and one day she disappeared. JSTo
one knew where she was till a bound’ry-
rider found her body in a lagoon on the
run.”
Randall pushed the whisky-bottle to-
wards Mills, and Mills pushed it back again,
and Murphy nodded. “ Now comes the
curious part,” continued Randall, ticking
off his words on his fingers ; “ never—since
—her—body—was—found have any sheep
or cattle been known to drink there, and
if you throw a stone in that lagoon it won’t
sink.”
Mills smiled derisively—“ Oh ! draw it
mild.”
Randall jumped up. “I’ll bet you five
pound it’s true.”
“ Done,” said Mills, placing a fiver on the
table, while Randall handed Murphy a
‘ Tattersall ’ receipt.
“It’s like this, Mr. Mills,” said Murphj',
apologetically, over the bar a month later,
“ if Mr. Randall hadn’t took your money
one of the others would, but I did think a
sharp young gent like you would have
dropped to the lagoon being as dry as a
bone —upon my word I did."

“Tom 0.”: The farmers’
daughters of Northern Victoria are,
as a rule, divinely tall; and sometimes
diabolically elongated._ At a country
pub. one Saturday night, I met a
friend, an English remittance-man,
who had been on the razzle for about
a week. At the expense of a night’s
sleep I kept him off it till Sunday
morning, and then took him out for a
walk. Presently he began to pant
and tremble, and, following his eye,
I noticed across a narrow paddock of
tall oats the head-dress of some girl
on her way to the local tabernacle.
“Do you see anything there, Tom?”
he asked. “ No, Percy. Do you ?”
“ I’ll swear I see a woman’s bat across
there,” he faltered. “Of course,” I
said; “so do I.” “But she’s on
foot J” he wailed. “ Certainly she is.”
“But the crop is seven feet high!”
“ Yes ; about that.” Then he uttered
a yell and sprinted for the Gqulburn,
with me in chase; while the young
giantess looked across the crop, won-
dering what on earth was the trouble.
A curious point in connection with a N.S. W.
recent prosecution by the Animals Protection
Society for dehorning cattle, is that, whereas
a Government publication contains elaborate
instructions for the performing of this operation,
the proceedings under notice were taken at the
instance of a member of the same Government.

Origin of the Riverina gag—“ Going to
see the agent ! ” In the ’7o’s, the wife of a Vic-
torian carrier sent her 10-year-old boy to keep the
old man company during the wool season. On
their return she privately questioned sonny:
“ Where did you stop of nights when you were at
Hay?” “Oh, I always minded the waggon.”
“ And where did your dad stop ?” “ Dunno ; he
was always away when I went asleep, and he was
always boilin’ the billy in the mornin’ when I
wakened up.” “Do you know where he’d been ? ”
“Yes; he always said he’d been to see the
agent.”

The Finger Post to Health
This is an Interesting Story
for Liver Sufferers.
The case of
Mr. G. HEATHCOTE.
(BY a special reporter.)
Since enlightened humanity requires that
the trials incidental to sickness should be
minimised wherever possible, and since, by
kindliness and consideration much can be
done in that direction, a reporter called
upon Mr. (leorge Heathcote, of Surry -
street, Waterloo, who willingly assented
to answer any questions.
“Were you once a lesident of Bunda-
berg, Queensland ? ” asked the pressman.
“Yes,” answered Mr. Heathcote. “I
was living tlmre when it was necessary to
cross the bridge by boat instead of walking
over it, or, to be more explicit, during the
time of the great flood, and it was during
that exciting and tlis sirous period that hiy
blood was so impoverished through exces-
sively hard work for long hours at a stretch
that sores broke out on my hands and arms,
and my legs swelled terribly. Previous to
this I had suffered from fever and ague,
which left me very weak and shaky, and
for some weeks I noticed that my appetite
was falling oil ; hub I put that down to
fatigue more than anything else, until other
symptoms cropped up to show me it was
not so, hut my i£,\er and kidneys which
were responsible.”
“ What reason, were there to enable you
to arrive at that conclusion ? ”
“Any number of them, one of the most
unmistakable being a nasty pain which
caught me here (Mr. Heathcote indicated
the part affected by placing his hand on the
Sinai! of his back i. My back ached so in-
tensely thatfmy wife made flannel belts for
me to wear, thinking that they would afford
me some relief. If I was doing anything
Avhich caused me to bend down, the pain on
straightening myself was something terrible,
while walking also provoked great distress
and inconvenience, Other proof that my
kidneys were in a had way was also in
evidence, and as for tlu rest of my ail-
ments—well, I hardly know where to begin
first.”
“ Tell me a few of the most important—
those that are uppermost in your mind ? ”
Oh ! I lirve not forgotten any of them,”
was the q-ick response, “especially the
heavy feelings in my chest, which made me
short-win- ed. After eating, a weighty sen-
sation appeared in that qua-ter, and before
I knew where I was my stomach began
swelling out with wind, va hicli ina»le me
very unoomforta le always, and at times
gave 1 ise to sharp cutting pains. The
Soui gases gradually worked their way up,
and caused me to belch, after which, and
until l took a little more food,. 1 felt
slightly easier, but the same unpleasantness
followed each meal, however trifling. When
making my morning preparations for work
I used to feel as i,- I would like to go back
to bed again, as 1 was entirely devoid of
energy, and had no desire to attempt any-
thing. This languid ness was due, no doubt,
to the restlessness every night. I could go
to bed feeling so drowsy that my eyes
seemrd swollen for the want of rest, yet I
found myself awake the greater part of the
night, and when I got up my limbs were
ail aches. Nearly every day I was dis-
tracted with intense headaches, which ap-
peared to bring a mist before my eyes, as
it my sigut was failing me, and without any
warning my head became so giddy that I
lad to take a rest till the sensation passed
away. Every month I was getting weaker
and more wretched, being continually be-
set with pains under my shoulder-blades,
which travelled right through to the heart,
and caused me considerable anxiety. The
white coating on my tongue explained the
unhealthy condit on of my stomach, too,
and what I wanted was a real good medicine
to clear away all disorders and impurities :
ut in those days I did not know as much
as I do now, or I would have been out of
my troubles very quickly. I went from
bad to worse, and had arrived at that
melancholy and desperate stage when the
least and most simple thing excites a feeling
of irritation, and lam sure you will admit
that such a life of misery is not worth en-
during. Had I gone on a little longer in
that sorry plight I question whether I
would have been as sound a man as I am
to-day, for I was fairly in the grip of my
complaint, when, owing to the recollection
of some conversations between a few of my
friends, 1 hit upon the idea of taking some
Clements Tonic. I took immediate ac ion,
as I firmly believed that further delay
would have brought irretrievable disaster
to my constitution.”
“ Your good fortune came at a very ac-
ceptable time, I should imagine ? ”
“ Yes; and a very critical time, too, and
I regretted that I had not bought a few
bottles of Clements Tonic at the outset ;
but we all have to buy our experience, you
know, although I intend doing all I can to
help other sufferers by letting them know
what Clements Tonic can do. Personally,
I can truthfully say that it generally re-
vived me from my lethargic state when I
had drank but a small quantity, and I found
myself rapidly improving. Clements Tonic
had a magical effect on my kidneys and
greatly relie ed the pains about my back
and shoulders. This relief, coupled with
the abatement of my headaches, had the
effect of making me feel more restful at
nights, and the sound sleep I got through
using Clements Tonic did me an immense
amount of good. My slumber was not in-
terfered with by dreams, and when I awoke
at the usual time for getting up I arose with
alacrity and hastened to my morning meal,
after which I was ready for work. It was
I a marvellous change to be full of energy
and cheerfulness again, but the invigorating
properties of Clements Tonic were so re-
markable that I could not have restrained
my feelings of joy had I wanted to. Wind
never formed in my stomach after Cl.ements
Tonic got to work, neither did my chest
trouble me, while in the matter of palpita-
tion the same medicine was equal to the
prevailing requirements. There is no
denying the fact that Clements Tonic
was wonderfully effective as a blood
purifier, for the sores were gradually
effaced from my hands and arms, and the
cause of my legs swelling—whatever that
cause might have been —was entirely re-
moved. My illness had considerably
affected my nerves, which became shaky
whenever I was disturbed, but to my heart-
felt thankfulness I must say that Clements
Tonic was as effective on my nervous
system as it was on my other deranged
organs.”
“ What was your appetite like then ? ”
“As it was soon after commencing on
Clements Tonic—very good, indeed, and it
has remained so ever since. Hut the
strengthening qualities of my ueuly-fouud
medicine surprised me, for although my
work was very heavy I always got through
it without feeling any ill effects from the
time Clements Tonic cured me, and I have
not once had the faintest return of any of
my old symptoms, in acknowledgment of
which I give you permission to publish my
story as it pleases you.”

“ THE AUSTRALIA ” is loftily situated ; from
its dome, the Pacific Ocean can be seen, and the
whole of Sydney.
To its favorable and airy position, “THE
AUSTRALIA ” adds every comfort and appoint-
ment. It is fireproof from tower to basement;
and an efficient staff patrols the building day and
night. It possesses the most elegant of drawing-
rooms, handsomely appointed dining-rooms, cosy
bar lounges and winter gardens. The system
and attendance of the hotel are unsurpassable.
J. URE SMITH (Late of Menzies’), -Manager.
Castlereagh-st., Sydney.
POLITICAL
POINTS
Wanted—to know a Customs-mistake case
in which the clerk of the poor harassed importer
has over-paid duty.
Bent’s abolition of free railway passes for
railway employees on leave in Victoria amounts
practically to another reduction in their wages.
Free education is one of the foremost
planks on N.S.W. Labor Party’s platform, yet on
Tuesday night week, when the Free Education
Bill was counted out, there were but three
members of that party in the Chamber, McGowen,
Griffith and Edden.
Philp the Unstable says that he is full-up
of Federation and that Queensland is with him.
Well, let him try a straightforward referendum
(one white person one vote) on these issues :
1. Are you tired of Federation ?
2. Are you tired —dog tired—of Philp and co. t
Tom Mann (Eng.), King O’Malley (Tas.),
J. C. Watson (N.S.W.), and Senators Stewart,
Pearce and Page (Q.), rendered valuable service to
Vic. Labor candidates at the recent election.
Indications are that if the Labor party had
a tempted more it would have accomplished more.

Mr. Crick : Whilst we are here, the reign of newspapers
is done.

it something to eat.

is the survival of the old primitive instinct of
savage and barbarous peoples to grab from others
without giving.” And Freetrade in Australia
lms been a survival of the primitive instinct of a
simple people to sell their birthright for a string
of glass beads.

m ide the same mistake in Victoria as the D.T. did

Vic. Ministry, while agreeing to Minister
Bent’s savage assault on railway employees’ pass
privileges, refuses to sanction the abolition of
executive councillors’ lifelong free passes and calls
them “a trifling advantage.” War on the poor
man’s penny for the preservation of the plutocrat’s

M. Speaker, Maurice O’Rorke, gave a
dig to Seddon, as well as spoke to the point, when
he told the House of Reps., in a closing-session
speech, that “it would puzzle the historian of the
future to account for the desire of the people of
M.L. to federate with the dusky-skinned Fijians
instead of with their white brethren of Aus-
tralia.”
One result of the Age and Argos coalition
to crush Liberalism and the working classes is to
emphasize the growing want of a good democratic
daily. The alteration in the Age’s circulation
since the election campaign must have been big.
The civil service has quietly thrown it over, as
well as many disgusted democrats, who could not
stand its slobbering of a man like Gillies.
N. Premier See has given notice of a
Reform Bill which, it is expected, will pro vide for
the election of a convention to draft a new State
constitution, and a subsequent referendum on the
result. No better or straighter or more sensible
method of reform could_ be devised —if only John
See is in earnest, and his Bill isn’t allowed to get
lost down one of the many cracks in Parliament.
“ When you see unity between these two
great organs (Argus and Age), it is time for the
people of this country to become suspicious of
what they are supporting. Because, lying out at
the back of their united support is usually some
ulterior design upon the liberties of the people.
—John Murray, now Chief- Secretary in the Irvine
Argus-Ageocracy in Vic. Leg. Assembly, Aug., IJUU.
The first items on the programme of Vic-
torian candidate J. W. Foster Rogers :
(1) We must not borrow for any purpose except for
renewing existing loans. . , ,
(2) We must gradually reduce our State debt by about
£200,000 a year.
Rogers didn’t get in. A man with sensible ideas
like that seldom gets in.

Signifi ant that, while Shiels at the Vic.
post-election smoke-night classified election results
as Ministerial responsibility, economic reform,
and crushed civil service, he did not even allude
to the reform of the Leg. Council as to qualifica-
tions of members and electors, although these

the electors by the Irvineites, as the three items
referred to.
The game of Brisbane Courier just now
is to push Premier Philp out of the Q.
Premiership. Almost every week that journal
prints a biting article insinuating (and sometimes
openly asserting) that if Mr. Philp is too weak to
hold the reins, there are others quite willing and
able to do it for him. Attorney-General Rutledge
is, of course (for family reasons), the Courier s
fancy man.
Importer M‘Millan’s whine to a D.T.
interviewer because his minority wasn’t allowed
to run the Federal show reads a very poor-spirited
affair. When M’Millan’s crowd of sweaters
carries its measures by a big majority he always
says that it has the confidence of the country, and
the heart of the people is with it. And when the
majority is against him he says that the other
fellow is ruling by “ brute force.”
Curious that while Melbourne dailies de-
nounce the Labor Party as the party of ruinous
extravagance, they agree in denouncing the
Federal Labor Party for compelling Federal
expenditure to be paid out of income and not out
of loans. To harmonise the denunciations it is
necessary to define ruinous extravagance as the
folly of paying one’s own debts when one has a
helpless posterity to assume the liability.
Queensland Leg. Council passed the In-
come Tax Bill by 17 votes to 16, despite the fierce
injunctions of both Telegraph and Courier to
throw it out at all cost. The dreary old councillors
hated the Bill like poison and thought the scan-
dalous poll-tax its only redeeming feature, but the
exigencies of a deficit-stricken State forced their
hand. Moreover, rumor has it they were threat-
ened with a Land Tax in case of recalcitrance.
Q. Premier Philp is the vicarious politician
who at first was “ agin ” Federation ; then gave
it lukewarm support; later, fearing that Dickson
was taking the wind out of his sails, he was for
Federation at any price and stumped the country
in its favor. He spoke from the same platform as
the (by him) detested Labor members, and heard
them avow, time and again, that Federation
meant an honest endeavor to have a White Aus-
tralia. Now Philp thinks that he didn’t know
it was loaded and cries for the moon.
One of Minister Lyne’s reasons for the
proposed expensive tour of Australia by a ship-
load of Federal politicians is that “ Members
should see the Northern Territory, for instance.”
Perhaps they should, but nothing that they see or
don’t see there can make any difference in the
necessity for the Commonwealth taking the Terri-
tory over. However bad it may be, it is part of
Australia and we have to make the best of it. So
us good an idea as any would be to take it over
first and go and look at it afterwards.
The James Ministry (W.A.) is trying to
'..fuse a little life into a rather dead constitution.
Its Reform Bill provides
(1) For a redistribution of seats on a population basis,
or something approaching it.
(2) Reduction of the Council to 24 and Assembly to 47
members, and the Ministry to five members.
(3) Every Minister to be allowed to vote only in the
House to which he belongs, but to be allowed to sit and
speak in either House.
(4) A double dissolution in case of deadlock, and a joint
sitting of the two Houses if the deadlock still continues.
WITHOUT PROVOCATION.
THE COURT: “But did you give no ‘provocation?"
THE COMPLAINANT: “ Divil a bit, your Worship. Cornin’ forninst him, I sez, sez I: ‘I ain’t
goin' to quar'll void you, you dirty, low scut! Don’t think ye can provoke me to fight,’
sez I, ‘becaze I wouldn’t dirthy me hands wid the likes of such a thavin ’ mongrel! ’ Wid
that I tuck him a skelp wid a bit of a brick, an’ not another wor-rd was said."
Sydney City Council election muddle
shows what a lot of use the Leg. Council is as a
guardian against “ hasty legislation.” The origi-
nal Act forgot to allow time for printing the
rolls, the time for collection of names being up to
Sept. 30, while the printed rolls had to be “ex-
hibited” on Oct. 1. The Upper House didn’t
notice it. A hurried amending Bill was rushed
through in 1901 to provide for the election that
year, with just one week to spare, and the matter
was promptly forgotten. In 1902 the Consoli-
dated Act dealing with municipal elections
perpetuated the blunder. Again the Upper
House didn’t notice. Now another amending
Act has to be hastily carried in order to hold the
elections this year. It would seem that the
necessity is not so much for a second Chamber as
for a .first Chamber that will attend to its business
properly.
It is a fact worth noting
that the coming States of the
world, the ones that are con-
stantly held up as models, and
the ones whose rivalry we are
taught to dread, are all Pro-
tectionist. The United States,
Canada, Argentina and Ger-
many are, in fact, about the
most intensely Protectionist
States on earth. There is no
rising Freetrade State any-
where which troubles us by
its rapid expansion and its
overwhelming rivalry. All
the nations whose growth
disturbs Britain in her sleep
are Protectionist. And they
all began to disturb Britain
only since they became Pro-
tectionist. There was no rush
of American compet-tion, and
no “Made in Germany”
bogey in the Freetrade or
semi-Free trade days of those
countries, and Argentine
rivalry didn’t keep Australia
awake o’ nights when Argen-
tina was a Freetrade republic.
The demand for new
men for N.S.W. Supreme
Court Bench raises the old
question of appointing solici-
tors to the office. The dividing
line between barristers and solicitors is mam y
imaginary, and, under recent State legislation, is
almost wholly wiped out. Making the Supreme
Court and District Court Bench a preserve tor
barristers looks like an offensive sample o
monopoly.

Already Melb. Argus, inspired by mem-
bers of the Iceberg Goyt., is talking of shelving
the constitutional reforrh scheme for the present
session, and there is little doubt that Premiei
Irvine will do so as soon as he feels that he may
with safety. So long as there is an anti-Fao-
tories Act majority in the Assembly, pledged also
to saddle the public servant with the responsi-
bility of a very large proportion of the general
taxpayer’s evasion of his liabilities, the Irvine and
Argus Conservatives care absolutely nothing for
the constitutional reform principle. People in
the know predict that the promised double disso-
lution will never come off, and that the Leg.
Assembly will face the electors about three years
hence on precisely the same basis as at present,
and with reform still in the air.
N.S.W. Labor Party should put the
coping-stone on its truly wonderful political
machine by making some provision for muzzling
Dacey and one or two other self-assertive poseurs.
No machine is quite perfect of which a crank can
occasionally go out on a little trip of its own and
hit things that ought not to be hit. The Labor
Party is the locomotive of the N.S.W. Parliamen-
tary train, but wisely likes to make out that it is
only the guard’s-van, or the, baggage-compartment,
or the dog-box, and that the real running power
is in the luxuriously fitted-up Pullman inhabited
by the Ministers. Occasionally, however, Mr.
Dacey, or some other Labor member, blurts out
the truth, and no matter how quickly the others
say “Ssh,” tbere is a snort from the Pullman
and a rough jolt in the running. A party
which is so cute in other respects can surely devise
effectual means of suppressing the unwise candor
of an occasional member.
