“You see, it’s like this ’ere : the Labor Party goes to plunk Irvine, and’
Irvine finding hisself in a hole, as you might say, looks around for some-
thin”' to defend hisself. Seein’ nothing else, what does he do but, quick as
lightning whips up the other fellow’s 'elpless little kid as was play in by,
and to save ’is own skin, ’olds up the pore little infant in front of ’im, and
afore the other was aware the awful blow that was to land Irvine squelched
the pore child.”
Despite Seddon’s failure at the Imperial
Conference to drag the States of Australia, into'
what Laurier called “the vortex of militarism,”
there is a suspicion in M.L. that the last has not
been heard of the matter. Seddon is the white-haired
boy with the conscriptionist Jingoes at ’Ome, and
it is just probable that he brings back with him
the draft of a scheme prepared by Chamberlain
“ to tighten the silken bonds.” This would please
Seddon for many reasons, but it would fit in best
of all with his desire to contrast Maoriland with
the Australian Commonwealth.
There’s no more bitter animosity in the
world than that between Irish Roman Catholics
and English Roman CWtholics; it spreads even
to Australia. In Britain just now Michael Davitt
is keeping the sentiment alive by referring to the
Cfertholics as the “deadly malignant foes of the
Irish Nationalist movement.” Those who fear
that Irish Home Rule will mean Rome Rule fly
in the face of history. It was Rome Rule that
first deprived Ireland of her Home Rule, and the
most conspicuous agitator* of the Irish Nationalist
movement have been Protestants.
Attorney-General Wise’s actions and critics
show the need of allowing a Minister to appear in
either House. Effective Government requires
effective control and management of both Houses,
but under the present system a member of the
Government who sits in one House is treated
as a stranger in the other. Wise worked the
Industrial Arbitration Bill through the Leg.
Assembly, but to get it through the Council .he
had to give up his Assembly seat. In the I ried-
man case his presence was needed in the Assembly,
but the Constitution was in his way.
Despite the Irvine Govt.’s insistence on
its candidates being pledged to support its
measures, the workers will find out when too late
that it will be only the members with any lean-
ings to Labor who will be held to their pledges.
Men like Madden and Gillies will be given
carte blanche to break their promises when
the Irvine Government’s proposals to reduce the
qualifications of members of the Council and to
dissolve both Houses in the event of disagreement
are under discussion. The workers who voted for
the Irvineites will then h e sorry they believed the
Age.
The Maoriland Parliamentary session just
closed is the shortest ordinary one held for years,
and the Tory press has been loud in its praises of
Oily Ward’s marvellously successful leader-
ship. His tact, his firmness, his winning ways,
have been the theme of columns and columns of
eulogy, till even the most thorough-going demo-
crat began to wonder if Oily Joe was not a heaven-
born leader. But even a Tory press cannot keep
a secret long, and now the work is done the cat’s
head begins to protrude from the bag. .Mixed
with the praise of the Oily one’s leadership are
rejoicings that the measures calculated to provoke
“unrest” and “class differences” have been
quietly laid aside, and that there lias, been an
entire absence of “revolutionary legislation.”
The “absence of revolutionary legislation!”—
that’s it. Not only has Joe abstained from the
cursed thing, but he has remitted taxation, and a
larger proportion of works and repairs are to be
charged to loans, the interest of which will, in the
future, do a lot to wreck even the revolutionary
legislation that was passed aforetime. No wonder
the Fat organs whoop for Ward.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.

IMPORTANT TO MINERS.
KIDNEY TROUBLES and
ITALARIAL FEVER
CURED BY. .
VITADATIO
Warwick, Queensland, May 28, 1901.
Sir, —Doubtless you will be glad to receive another
testimony to the efficacy of your valuable remedy—
VITADATIO.
About twelve months ago I went to the Ebagoolah
Goldfields, and hence to New Guinea, full of health and
hope, but it was not long before I became a victim to that
fearful climate, which completely undermined my health.
I was treated in Northern Queensland in two hospitals
without success, finally', and with difficulty that would
take too long to detail, I reached Brisbane, when I secured
your remedy, which I am glad to say at once produced a
magical effect, and ultimately restored me to health and
vigour.
I am delighted to be able to write thus, and would
suggest that you publish this in the papers circulating in
New Guinea, where I am so well-known.
I am not a doctor, but I believe my trouble was a
severe disorder of the kidneys, caused by and attended
with Malarial Fever.—Yours trulv,
(Signed) ANDREW JAMIESON.
To S. A. PALMER, Vitadatio, 28/5/1901.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,

SYDNEY.


The foreman of the jury which tried an
obscure embezzler at last Melbourne Criminal
sittings, relates that, on retiring to the jury-room,
he asked “ What do you think of it?” “Well,”
said a grizzled old juror, scratching his head, “ this
young fetter never done me any harm.” As a
contrast, was the remark of a big miner of Bendigo,
at the opening of a sensational murder trial a
couple of years back. “ What sort o’ men do they
put on the jury ?” he asked, indignantly; “ What’s
Johnson put on for ? What’s the good o’ he ?
They want men o’ intelligence. Now, I’d hang
that man right off ! ”
“Buzz”: The aggressive virtue of the
unmarried lady of 60 summers is not always with-
out sincerity. One morning lately, in a suburb
where the boarding-house system is a disease, a
landlady was discoursing to an elderly maiden
lodger on the shifts to which Brown—a lodger
then under a cloud —was put, even to the extent
of retiring to his lonely couch in the altogether,
because his only pair of pyjamas were at the wash.
“ Do you mean to tell me that he goes to bed
without—without them, ! M The landlady nodded.
“ Then it is quite time that I left the house,” was
the reply, and forthwith she packed her little cap
and nightgown, and departed to a house where,
let us hope, all the lodgers have them.

GROSVENOR STREET
A Modern Fiend.
Never since, the Inquisition has there been his
like. His mind seems so peculiarly constructed
that he appears to delight in inflicting the
most terrible agony upon the most inoffen-
sive persons, and dealing it out to his victims
in such a manner that they are unable to
tell from whence the pain has come. His victims
fill every church-yard, and our greatest intellects
have been striving to rid the earth of his hateful
presence, but he attacks the very judge as he deals
out justice upon the bench, as if to cast derision
upon the majesty of the law. To say that no one
escapes from his clutch is a mistake, for many
have recovered. Mrs. A. Everingham, Nana
road, Grafton, speaks carefully. She says:
“Since girlhood I have suffered from severe
indigestion (the fiend we refer to), which would
catch me with an awful pain in the chest, and
made me believe that I could not live an hour. I
tried many remedies, and saw several medical
men, but their cures were not lasting. Not long
since I tried Dr. Morse’s Indian Hoot Pills. The
first bottle made me feel in good health, and I
used the second. I find they are the best remedy
I have taken and I can recommend them as such.”
Dr. Morse’s Indian Boot Pills are a perfect blood
purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness, indiges-
tion, constipation, headaches, sallow complexion,
liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples, boils and
blotches, and for female ailments. Sold by
chemists and storekeepers, Is per bottle, or
six bottles 6s 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H.
Comstock Co., Limited. (Australasian Depot),
58 Pitt-street, Sydney. Packed in amber bottles
and the full name blown thereon.*
Martin’s Apiol and Steel Pills are sold by
all chemists throughout Australasia, price 4s. 6d.
Agents: Sayers, Allport, and Potter, Sydney.
Sole Proprietor, W. Martin, Pharmaceutical
Chemist, Southampton, England.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A fielder, with early information, dropped in on
a big- bookmaker in an Australian city and asked him to
lay Sceptre tor the St. Leger. “ The race has been run,"
said the layer. “ Oh, yes, but the result is not known
yet!” Moses looked thoughtfully down his nose. “I’ll
bet you fives--£5O to £lO, £5OO to £100.” The man with
early information took it, the money was put up and the
ticket issued. Next day the papers announced the result
and the fielder dropped in to lift the money. “ The race
has not been run yet,” said Moses, calmly. “It won’t be
run for 12 months. That ticket’s for next year.” Then
the air was full of D.
* • * *
Success of Sydney nag, Kinglock, in Toorak
Handicap, Saturday, gladdened the heart of Mo., who
was not having his usual luck up to then. Though a
fairly good performer previously, Kinglock wasn’t a
favorite, and loudly-voiced hundreds to 4 went begging ;
the stable only closed on £2OO of it for fear the nag might
fluke the race. Kinglock is in Caulfield Cup with 7.6, in-
cluding penalty—lib. less than Saturday’s load. Was
bred in N.S.W. by Lochiel from Lady Kingsborough, by
Kingsborough from the Yattendon mare Hussey. Before
the Toorak, Kinglock’s record of successes oniy totalled
three—all welter races. Time (1.44) for the trip (1 mile)
isn’t extra fast, but still it is the best since Paul Pry got
home five years ago, and has only been beaten twice.
• • • •
First day’s results at Caulfield meeting put the
Derby and Cup backers of Strata Florida on velvet
without seriously alarming the disciples of Abundance.
Strata’s victory in the Guineas seemed to stamp him as a
first-class youngster. He came to the front like a race-
horse at the distance, and won bj' three lengths in a time
that has only once been beaten. But this didn’t prove
that the colt had beaten Wakeful on her merits at
Flemington a week previously ; consequently, the relative
form of Strata and Abundance is still a fairly open ques-
tion, although the former has the more attractive
appearance. Wakeful on Saturday got no bumps in
the Stakes race. She just ran away from every-
thing as usual, and finished two lengths ahead of
Abundance, who, for his part, had to make up a lot of
ground to get into second place. If both of them get
along satisfactorily in the meantime, the Derby struggle
between Abundance and Strata should establish one or
other a 3 to 1 favorite for Melbourne Cup, and possibly
one or other of them will do the Newhaven and Merriwee
trick and take the “ double.” At any rate, it should now
be good enough to take a fancy bet of 2 to 1 that the Cup
will fall to a 3-year-old. For there are others beside
Strata Florida and Abundance that might beat the heavy
weights (now that The Victory seems a “ goner ”), and
prove too good for the old ’uns in the middle division of
the handicap.
To see the leggy Duke of Grafton stride away
from his field in the Debutant Stakes was to reckon, for
the moment, that Payten’s flyer had the Maribyrnong
Plate at his mercy. A big two-year-okl that can sprint
oil the mark as smartly as one of the small-and-early sort
always strikes one as a champion. But the history of the
Debutant Stakes doesn’t favor this idea. Challenger, the
giant youngster of ’94, won the half-mile flutter in Hsec.
faster time than Duke of Grafton’s, yet Challenger finished
only fourth for the five-furlong Maribyrnong Plate. The
penalty seems to stop them—big or little—in nearly every
case. All the same, the Duke may be a rare exception.
Astounding tales are told of his private trials, and they
backed him on Saturday as though those yarns were
almost true.
This year’s Caulfield Cup promises well for the
bookmakers, seeing that none of the local horses—bar
Seclusion—has a commanding chance on recent form,
whilst the dangerous ones from other States are’
not so very dangerous as to put the local punters out of
conceit with their provincial tips. Air Motor, King-
lock, Kilt and Acrasia could be expected to beat all,
or nearly all, of the Victorian fancies, if there were no
“ startling reversals of form” where big prizes are con-
cerned. Air Motor must have a good chance to win, luck
permitting. Kilt, the Westralian, ran a respectable race
behind Wakeful last Saturday, and is, moreover, sup-
ported by some of the mare’s best friends. And Acrasia
has the recommendation of being by Gozo from little
Oerise and Blue, breeding that spells gameness and sug-
gests steady improvement in Oxenham’s mare. She may
not win, but she is pretty certain to put up a good fight
if she gets through the crowd. In considering the Caul-
field Cup before it is run, one has always to bear in mind
the probability of a scrimmage bringing a dozen unlucky
animals to grief. Among thelocal candidates present scribe
looks to The Idler, Flagship and Seaport to do something
better than their recent defeats predict for them. The
Idler has been running “ big,” hut a gelding is all the
more likely to astonish the touts when he looks to be
short of work. The Adelaide Oup performance of The
Idler, when he landed 7st. 111 b. ahead of The Victory and
Flagship, gives him a great, chance for Caulfield Cup. He
is possibly a brilliant horse, and certainly he is in clever
hands. This writer’s tip is—
Tub Idler.
Air Motor.
Kilt.
* • • «
“Old-Timer” spreads himself over a coming
event : Picking the winner of to-day’s Caulfield Cup is
the most difficult of many tough turf problems. Final
forfeits Monday left 26 in—nine of them N.S.W. top-
notchers. Though betting up to time of writing hasn’t
indicated the long-waited-for stable-move on Seclusion,
she stands her ground and is being freely nibbled at’
Combat’s winning of V.R.O. Stand Handicap of two
weeks back gives him a good chance. Latest heavily
supported one is Progedior, a 5-year-old son of Pilgrim s s
Progress, most of whose racing has been done in Tas-
mania, where he won the Hobart Oup (1 Jm.), with 7.4 up,
last January ; but that form is much discounted through
Cordon (9.5) finishing a good third in the same race.
Progedior’s third to Combat and Abington in Stand Han-
dicap, run in fast time, gives him a good show for the
Cup were it not that he seemed like winning at the home
turn and then failed. The gallop he was backed on last
Monday was miles at Epsom in 2.45. Loads of wealth
have already gone and are going on Air Motor—the bulk
of it from N.S.W.—and her running in the Toorak Handi-
cap, Saturday, impressed so much that a further heap
was forthcoming Monday, leaving the mare a scorching
hot first favorite at 6to 1. Trainer Burton, when he left
Sydney, thought Air Motor much better, than when she
won the Epsom and filled a place in the Metrop. at recent
A.J.O. meet. Acrasia has been quietly supported at out-
side figures, and though a little ’un, her prospects are not
to be ignored. She rah well iii the Epson!, finishing just
behind the placed nags after getting away last and having
none too good a passage. Just before leaving for Mel-
bourne she traversed a very fast mile, putting Air Motor’s
gallop of the same morning over the same distance com-
pletely in the shade. Summed up, this scribe’s money is
going on Air Motor or Acrasia, with Seclusion as a saver.
• * * »
Manoa-Mystery nag Monarch put up a fine
lepping feat in Hunters’ ’Chase at Rosehill Saturday.
Humping 14st. he was easily in front all the way over Si-
miles, fencing faultlessly and ! showing fine pace.’ Most of
the field just came into, the straight as winner passed the
judge. Lepping all round was much above the average.
Only one of eight fell and he was re-mounted. Members
of Sydney Hunt Club were up in most instances.
* * * *
Nineteen figured iti N.S.W. first 2-year-old race
of season, run at Rosehill, Saturday. ‘ Favorites were
Nest and Electra, with lies, The Bayonet, Kitty Grafton
and Francesca well supported. Nest was in front at the
right end, and is a compact, small, brown Ally, bred by
Mr. S. Hordern, and sold at yearling sales last April for
150 gs. Is by Gossoon (imp.) from Lady Norah by
Cadogan (imp.) from Steppe (imp.) by Saunter from
Seclusion, the dam of Hermit, and consequently related,
on the dam’s side, to Bobadil, Metal, Dalmeny, Stepniak
and Russlcv.
* * * *
I'o^3’all meet, Saturday, saw unlucky T.M.S.
run another second, making the 17th place since he
scored a win. Twelve were seconds, five thirds, and one
dead-heat—all in 12 months. There’s no mistake about
T.M.S.’s grit and capabilities, but his ill-luck is the most
pronounced in Australian turf history, and reminiscent
to an extent of the doings of Secundus, Sardonyx, and
others of years ago. T.M.S. must have cost his late
owner, leviatnan “ Oxy,” a small fortune, and looks like
lowering materially the banking account of the man (Mr.
Oatley) who has him now. His latest second should have
been a win, for the race was unquestionably his—99 of
every 100 onlookers saw that, but the stewards didn’t—
further evidence of the curse that follows the nag.

• * * *
Fate of Mr. E. M. Clark’s Tote Bill in N.S.W.
Parliament is sealed. Mo. and “ the cloth ” are already
organising their forces and the Unholy Alliance promises
to be stronger than ever. First named has been prepared
for years and latter is working up his opposition every-
where. “ Sydney Western Suburbs Ministerial Associa-
tion ” resolved t’other day: “ That this Association ex-
presses as its conviction that the passing of a measure to
legalise the Totalisator would result in a large increase in
gambling, and be detrimental to the best interests of the
community.”
* * * *
In Sydney, on Eight-Hour Day, passengers by
train or tram or Shanks’ pony were accosted every now
and again with requests to buy a ticket in the art union—
“ £5OO for Is.” N.S.W. is getting close to a Government
lottery, having already this Labor party lottery.
* * * *
Passed out, a few weeks ago, at Croydon (Q.),
a good man and a good sport. A. D. Cameron’s was a
name to conjure with in Muttaburra and Aramac (Q.)
district some 10 years back. He was a typical westerner,
long, lean, tough and brown ; hard to beat at anything ;
splendid horseman and amateur rider, and invincible at
grass-fed meetings. Two days before his death (in Croy-
don hospital) Croydon annual race-meeting began, and
shortly after the first race an apparition in pyjamas and
bare feet climbed slowly up the grandstand steps. It
was “ A.D.”, who had dodged the hospital attendantsand
crawled out to the course for a last look at the “ great
game ” before handing in his cap and jacket.

Recently-deceased Victorian sport, Mr. R. W.
Jex, cetat 61, trained Pell-Mell—Nellie hound Miss Walker
for big Waterloo Cup she won 26 years ago. Hazel Bloom,
Marion, Joan, Veracity, Justice, Spread Eagle, Miss
Washington, and Sentinel belonged to him at one time
and another.
* • • *
Sydney National Sporting Club, which seems to
have come to stay, opened auspiciously on Monday night
with an audience of 2000, which paid from £1 down to ss.
for its seats. The programme was a hot curtain-raiser
between bantam North, of Melbourne, and Black, of
Sydney ; and the long-looked-for scrap between Doherty
and M'Call for the heavyweight championship of Aus-
tralia. That the club is going to exercise an elevating
effect on the sport is quite evident. Referee, Harry
Becket, once middle and heavy champion of N.S.W.,
officiated from outside the ring, as was done in days of
yore at bygone Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club, and is
done even unto this day at that swagger institution, the
National Sporting Club, London. Though purse (£300)
and expenses allowed totalled £4OO, and outlay other-
wise was heavy, there was still a substantial profit.
Seconds wore uniform—white pants and full fighting
singlets, and, altogether, the whole display was refresh-
ingly free from the usual associations of a Sydney boxing
contest.
The curtain-raiser lasted nine rounds, when
North’s superior weight and capacity for punishment
crumpled Black right up, in spite of a skilful and effective
left. Then the previous champion (Doherty) and M‘Call,
who is now a flourishing dispenser of wet at Newcastle,
got into the ring. Doherty looked fat-bellied, pallid-
skinned, and weeks short of preparation. M'Oall looked
as hard as nails and browned all over. First round was
tame and unproductive of a solitary decent smite, then
Doherty commenced to force, regardless of his own safety.
Left and right were widely extended, wing-like, leaving
the protuberant diaphragm open enough for a cart
to, back into ; but M‘Call failed to fling himself into
it. Doherty hooked frequently at long range and drove
into ithe mark and ribs of M'Call, who responded by
swinging each limb incessantly, and only using a straight
left occasionally and ineffectively. Spite of careless
fighting, Doherty led easily and looked a certain winner,
while M‘Call tired palpably. But the beer-pumper pulled
himself together, and in the fifth round a straight push
(it couldn’t be dignified by the name of a punch) with the
left collided with Doherty’s jaw, and that pot-bellied
gentleman fell down most inartistically, rolled on his
back, and lay still. M’Call had won the poorest heavy-
weight battle ever seen in Sydney.
• * *
“Jab.” : Re M‘Call-Doherty fight Monday
night. Singular, isn’t it, that Doherty almost invariably
goes under in his first battle with every fresh individual
he faces, and wins the return match. It happened with
Mick Dooley, “ Tut ” Ryan, and Peter Felix. Wonder if
M‘Call will go through the same experience ?
* * * *
“ Ban.”: Boxing (amateur especially) has at
last received a boost-up in Maoriland by the formation of
an association to foster the game. First championship
meeting in Theatre Royal, Christchurch, attracted big
houses on two nights, when about 20 amateurs contested
the various championships. The victor in the “ heavies”
was Jack Fitzsimmons, of Timaru. He a younger brother
of the redoubtable “ Bob,” but there’s nothing about him
reminiscent of the ex-world’s champion, save the face and
the grit. He stands with legs straddled almost as far
apart as he can get them, right elbow to his side, forearm
extended away from the body, and hand dangling as if
the wrist were dislocated. Compensating for his awkward
style, however, were his total indifference to punishment,
and his hearty dealing out of whack when occasion
offered. The bouts throughout the meeting, indeed,
were less remarkable for science than for straight, hard
hitting.
* » * *
The Sydney bruising boom is going strong just
now. The present week is all padded hand fighting—not
a night missed but Sunday. Melba prices were freelv
paid to witness Monday’s battle.
* * * *
Bruising struggle at Sydney Golden Gate A.C.,
Saturday night, between up-country rough-and-tumble
fighter Lynch and ex-Maori contingenter Glasson lasted
eight dull, dreary rounds. Glasson hadn’t “ fix ” enough
to fight two rounds, and shaped like a novice, despite his
alleged record of successes in Africa. Lynch, always
forceful and fierce but never effective, simply roughed his
man to a limp and broken-spirited condition, and then
the Maori “chucked it,” dog-tired and done, on the
groundless assertion that Lynch had struck him “in
holts,” and he w’asn’t going to fight longer where that
sort of thing was allowed. Glasson is billed to meet one
Maher in Sydney a week or two hence, but, as the match
wouldn’t draw beer money after last Saturday night’s
farce, it’ll probably be wiped off.
• * • •
’Rastle at Newcastle (N.S.W.) t’other day, be-
tween Indian Buttan Singh and coal-hewer Tweedie, was
the black’s from go. Catch-as-catch-can conditions
governed, with the strangling hold barred (at the solicit-
ation of Tweedie). Buttan kicked, but the miner was
obdurate. Height, weight, and condition were all on the
Indian’s side. First fall (through a “ half-Nelson ”) went
to the turbanned combination of wire and whipcord in
4Jmin. ,and offers of 4 to 1 on him led to no business.
Tweedie was helpless as a child subsequently, and next
time succumbed to a hammerlock in 9Jmin., thus ending
the match, conditions of which called for two falls out of
three. Singh could easily have won in less time, but he
evidently wanted to give the crowd something for its coin.
Tweedie has followed Singh to Melbourne for another
match, averring that he overdid his training and lost
weight unaccountably, but he would do heaps better stay-
ing at home working in the pit.
* * • •
Hasn’t been published anywhere in Australia
yet that whilom N.S.W. champion swimmer Read (of
whom so much was expected in England, and who did so
little comparatively) last month notched 5 of the 6 goals
credited to the winning side while playing water polo for
Oygnus against Hove Club, and three days later easily
appropriated the Holbein Challenge Shield, contested
over a course something in excess of 2J miles. He
traversed the first mile in 26.55, the second in 54.54,
and the full journey in 63.44. Second man took nearly
llmins. longer.
• • * •
Initial round of Sydney first-grade cricket con-
cluded Saturday. Sydney, Waverley, North Sydney,
and Central Cumberland triumphed. Paddington, which
owns the services of Noble, Trumper and Kelly, when
they are at home, went under to Waverley by an innings
and 256 runs. Losers only compiled 70 and 38 in each
effort. Howard bagged 13 of them for 54. Sydney
settled ’Varsity by 68, mainly through deadly bowling of
M'Beath and Marsh, former of whom got 6 for 23, and
latter 4 for 36. Redfern went under by 102 to North
Sydney. Central Cumberland’s surplus over Burwood
was 40. Glebe-Leichhardt-Balmain match ended in a
draw. Waverley man M'Kenzie had hard luck. Finish-
ing previous week with 97 not out, he only got another,
thus missing the century by 2 runs.

player H. Carpenter, is a 33-year-old son of Robert of that
ilk, who was in Australia with George Parr’s team nearly
40 years back. Carpenter has good endorsement and
should fill the bill well.

Spectacle of possibly the oldest (“Phil”
Glenister) and youngest (“Parvo”) crack pigeon-mur-
derers outstripping all others was witnessed on Melbourne
Gun Club grounds t’other day. Glenister scored a
sequence of 24, and was only beaten by three out of 34
fired at during the day. Boy “ Parvo ” only missed three
of 27 possible to him, and accounted for a break of 16,
shooting all the time from the same mark as his ancient
opponent, so the brutal “ sport” had a good day out.
» * * *
Bathurst policeman O’Reilly, on occasion of
Eight-Hour sports in that city last week, created two
fresh field game “bests” for this continent. Putting
161 b. shot from a 7ft. square he squelched previous best
(43ft. 7Jin.), credited to George Hawkes (Q.), by 7Ain. ;
and throwing the 161 b. hammer from a 9ft. circle he
topped the prior figures (136 ft. 4Ain.), standing in the
name of M.L. policeman Madill, by 14ft. 81 in. A.A.U. of
Australasia has yet to endorse these feats, but, as con-
ditions are certified as thoroughly in order, there can be
no question about them. O’Reiily’s work in practice led
athletic authorities to expect something big from him
sooner or later.
* * • *
“Teta”: “H.E.” (4/10/’O2) condemns physical
exercise altogether “because there is little use in being
able to lift two tons or in the possession of abnormal
physical power.” In no rational system of physical
culture is it recommended to try to lift enormous weights
or to develop “ abnormal ” power. Extreme efforts often
produce disastrous after-effects such as heart-disease and
aneurism. He is wrong, however, when he condemns all-
round development of the muscular system and the culti-
vation of some reserve of strength beyond what is required
by one’s occupation. He instances navvies, smiths
and wharf-laborers as dullards. Are these men dullards
because they are navvies, or navvies because they are
dullards ? The latter is the more likely. Re Chamber-
lain as an instance of high mentality and poor
muscle, “ H.F.”is rather unfortunate in the choice of an
example. The best known about J.C. is that he made
money by rigging the market in screws, performed two
or three political somersaults, and allowed himself and
England to be drawn into a war which he had himself
formerly denounced as unjust and unwarrantable. This
is a poor recommendation. What about W. E. Gladstone
the tree-cutter, and Abraham Lincoln the rail-splitter, as
instances to the contrary? “H.H.” says muscular
strength does not prolong life. Wrong, again. If a
strong, muscular man and a weak one are stricken down
with a severe illness, such as pneumonia, does he think
for a moment that the strong man, other things being
equal, has not a much better chance than the weakling ?
Another statement is that constant exercise is necessary
to maintain the muscular development, otherwise there
is danger of fatty degeneration. It is known to phy-
siologists that once the muscles have been
thoroughly developed by exercise, it needs only
a small amount of work to maintain them in the same
condition, and even if they do degenerate somewhat
through want of exercise, the subject is in no worse a
condition than the weakling whose muscles were never
developed at all. The muscles constitute about one-half
of the body weight, and if this half is not exercised to a
fair degree the other half must suffer. In addition, the
motor part of the brain, which controls the muscles, is
developed proportionately with the muscles, and as the
motor and other parts of the brain are mutually inter-
dependent, exercise leads to all-round development of the
brain. An instance of one-sided development is seen in
some school children whose brains are developed and
muscles neglected. It takes the form of a disease
called chorea (“ St. Vitus dance,”) in which the
portion of the brain controlling the muscles, so to
speak, runs wild. For the health of the body as a whole,
physical and mental culture should go hand-in-hand.
Muscular exercise not only developes the body but main-
tains a better standard of health. It promotes a feeling
of exhilaration and enjoyment of life. It improves
nutrition of the body. It increases the appetite and
promotes healthy action of the bowels. It compels the
lungs, which are not used to one-quarter of their limit
during sedentary occupations, to expand to their full
extent, and so tends to ward off consumption. It
increases the work the heart has to do, and so keeps this
organ in such a condition of energy that if extra work is
necessary it is able to do it. It makes a man sounder
physically, mentally and morally—when indulged in
within reasonable limits. Sandow is an exponent per-
haps of one extreme, your correspondent of the other.
The truth lies between.
Young G. A. Moir, the Australasian champion
amateur runner (son of Melbourne solicitor Moir), was
married last week to a grand-daughter of Graham Berry.
So the champion running career of young George is at an
end. He leaves his shoes to be filled by a bachelor.

Josition for old-age pensions in a letter to the

I trust the programme for the next parliamentary elec-

A curious letter, signed “PetiG. Park,”
appeared in a recent Sydney Telegraph. The
writer had returned to Sydney after 12 years’
absence, and on his second day in his old haunts
he wrote to say it was all a mistake about there
being any depression—the beautiful new buildings
that had gone up since his departure proved that.
The poor man never noticed that N.S.W. had
acquired £31,000,000 of new debt while he was
away, and with that money to spend it would
indeed be a cold day if there weren’t some new
buildings to show. When one considers all
the beautiful unpaid-for Government offices
that adorn Bridge-st. and its neighborhood,
and the unpaid-for post office, and the new, un-
paid-for tramways, and railway station, and land-
resumptions, and sewerage works, and the two
gorgeous blocks which belong to the two American
life assurance societies, and the splendid new
offices that are going up in Martin-place for an
English bank, there certainly is some progress.
The progress may not belong to the Australian
people, but it is there. And when N.S.W. has
borrowed in 12 years a sum equal to three-fourths
of the value of all the land and buildings of the
city of Sydney, it assuredly ought to be there.

“ Five-foot ” : “ L.M.D.” (B., 4/10/’02)
says he knows a permanent-way man when he
sees him. I doubt it, judging by his knowledge
of a railroad. Re the p.w.m.’s alleged three long
steps and a short one, there is no road in this part
of the world with but two sleepers between the
joints, or with rails only 10ft. long. Also, the
distance between joint-sleepers is lOin. —not 1ft.
or 18in. And, finally, p.w. men don’t walk on the
j sleepers except when obliged to do so.

Sydney D.T.’s representative in the
Federal Parliament writes in his summary of the
session :—
What Mr. Edmund Barton can do under the stimulus of
a great movement was shown in his gallant fights during
the Federal campaigns. It was when, with the work
accomplished, he came to administer its details, to place
the complete scheme in practical operation, that he dis-
appointed. It is almost impossible adequately to express
in language the awe, the reverence, in which Mr.
Barton was regarded in Victoria and some of the other
States. In Melbourne it was almost a sacrilege to con-
template, much less to create and control and lead, an
Opposition to him. The personality of Mr. Barton
was looked upon as so politically sacred that
opponents were regarded with mingled indignation and
derision. He was accepted, not as in Byron’s phrase,
half divinity, half dust, but as some superior and almost
supernatural being, whose name could not be uttered,
except in whispers. In Melbourne he was in reality Aus-
tralia’sNoblestSon. As the session wore on thedream van-
ished, the ideal was found to be very human indeed. The
unwearying devotion to public affairs that was expected
from Mr. Barton proved to be tempered by a keen appre-
ciation for his personal comfort and his personal idiosyn-
cracies. Instead of being constantly in the House,
managing public business, he was rarely present, save in
desperately fitful flashes. His control over the House
vanished. His influence over his own supporters slowly
passed away. Mr. Barton began not to lead, but
to sue for help. The Labor members, whom he at first
treated with some disdain, rose in his conception, and,
indeed, in reality, to be the power which supported his
throne. The political scales fell off the eyes of Victoria.
The idol was found to be as other men are. The cartoonist
who had sketched him as a Greek deity treated him with
even disrespect. What remained of Mr. Barton was
a high appreciation of his ability as a constitutionalist,
but a complete disillusionment as to his capacity for poli-
tical leadership.
A Decaying’ Profession.
Some burglars having broken into the house of
a poor man were diligently engaged ransacking
his shelves and drawers when the owner suddenly
awoke and sat upright in bed. “ Hunt away, my
men, hunt away,” he said. “I don’t know what
you expect to find here by this dim light. For my
part, I can find nothing of value in the day.” And
having made these remarks he resumed his
slumbers and the disgusted burglars withdrew.
The fact is, the profession of which Mr. Bill Sikes
was so conspicuous an ornament is a decaying one,
and the art of burglary is in a fair way to be lost.
There are more reasons for this than we have time
now to inquire into, but possibly one of them may
be the counter attraction of selling something
warranted to do that which it will not do. Even
as there are no friends like old friends, so there is
no remedy the equal of Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup with its thirty-three years of world-wide
renown. Just give two minutes’ attention to the
testimony of Mr. J. Armitage, the licensed
carrier, of Schrader-street, Adelaide, S.A., and
his wife. It deserves it. Mr. Armitage writes,
on the 25th of March, 1902 :—“As both myself
and my wife have derived great benefit from the
use of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, it is 6nly
just that I should thank you for it and give a few
particulars which may direct others, now suffering
as we formerly did, where to look for relief and
cure. My wife was afflicted for years with indiges-
tion. She tried a variety of mixtures, pills, &c.,
and at different times was treated by several
doctors, but without avail. Frequently she was
quite unable to attend to her household affairs.
About three years ago a neighbour recommended
her to try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, with
the result that she was cured within six weeks.
As for myself, I was subject to asthma and
rheumatism for a long period. Often have I lain
in bed for days suffering torture through the latter
complaint. The asthma, too, was very distressing,
especially in damp or cold weather. I spent
much money, and swallowed a lot of physic, in the
endeavour to free myself of these ailments; but
it was all to no purpose. At last it occurred to
me that possibly the medicine which had cured
my wife might benefit me; and, having resolved to
make the experiment, I discovered that it was
just what I required. It speedily enabled me to
breathe with ease and comfort, as well as subdu-
ing the rheumatism. About six bottles of the
Syrup cured me. My wife and I always have a
bottle of it in the house, and by taking a few doses
of it every week our health is never a source of
anxiety to us.”

The way of the hero- worshipper is some-
times hard. A small batch of returning swaddies
recently landed in Port Melbourne and in a
more or less muddled condition were taken by
train to the metropolis. With many narrow
escapes from falling in front of moving trains,
they prepared with difficulty to scramble up the
steps at Flinders-street. A well-meaning and
right-thinking person, seeing one man in imminent
danger of falling back and breaking his neck,
seized him kindly by-the arm and, preventing him
from dropping his swag, steered him carefully o
the upper landing. Once there, the returned
farm-incendiary turned a bleary eye on him, looked
thoughtful for a full second, and then dealt the
patriotic citizen a “woodener” on the jaw.
“You cow,” he said in explanation, trying
to steal a poor soldier’s swag!”

A year or so ago, when it was proposed to
hold a popularly-elected convention to draft a
reformed Victorian Constitution, about a dozen
MTs-L-C. were interviewed {vide Argus, 28/9/’00),
and this is the sort of verdict they returned
If, a Bill embodying reforms of both branches of the
Legislature be passed by the Assembly and sent on to the
Council it will receive careful and sympathetic treat-
ment at the hands of the Upper House —Bell, M.L.O.
It is absolutely necessary, however, that there should
b« reforms in both Houses . . . these reforms can be
dealt with in a Bill in the ordinary way.-EMBLiNa,
M ’rt ?S a mistake to suppose the Council is hostile to
reform. . . The Chamber is willing to have changes
made in its Constitution.— Winter Cooke, M -b.O.
There is no necessity for a convention. _ The Council
only wants to know what the people desire in matters of
legislation, and it will meet their wishes. Proposals for
reform should be brought forward in the ordinary way by
a Government Bill, and it will be found the Council is
fust as desirous of bringing the Constitution up to date
as the Assembly.— Dowling, M.L.C.
Now Premier Irvine promises a Reform Bill in
the ordinary way! And the spectacle of the
Council breaking its neck m its haste to accept
the people’s wishes will doubtless be visible at an
early date.

Read the Words
If You Please, Miss, Give Me Heaven.
“ Papa, I’m so sad and lonely,”
Sobbed a tearful little child ;
“ Since dear mamma’s gone to Heaven,
Papa, darling, you’ve not smiled.
I will speak to her and tell her
That we want her to come home;
Just you listen, and I’ll call her
Through the telephone:
Chorus—
“lf you please, Miss, give me Heaven—for my Mamma’s
You can find her with the angels on the golden stair.
She’ll be glad it’s me who’s speaking—call her, won’t you,
please,
For I want to surely tell her we’re so lonely here.
When the girl received this message,
Coming o’er the telephone,
How her heart thrilled in that moment,
And the wires seemed to moan ;
“ I will answer, just to please her :
‘ Yes, dear heart, I’ll soon come home.’ ”
“ Kiss me, Mamma, kiss your darling,
Through the telephone."
Chorus —“ If you please, Miss,” etc.

Strong-man Pagel, who has been challenging Sandow,
has been astonishing the Bendigoites with his
fiats, ineluding the lifting of a lion, said to weigh
3001 b., above his head. “ The act,” says a local paper,
“being rendered highly exciting by a display of resent-
ment on the part of the huge quadruped, which roared in
amrer ..As a final act, Herr Pagel carried a full-
sized horse, about 16 hands high, into mid-air by means
of two ladders, between wnich the horse was placed.
It was a tremendous lift, considering the resistance of
the horse, which was in fine condition, and inclined to be
restive. Herr Pagel is certainly a wonderfully strong
man, and the surprising thing is that he is not taken in
hand by some theatrical manager of an enterprising
turn.’’ But Pagel has no physical-culture “biz” to
boom, nor a pretty wife, nor any of the showman s arts.
He is just an unpretentious young giant of 24.

Asthma is one of those miseries of life
which sometimes make it seem impossible
to longer endure the afflict ion of existence 1
Yet the way to a cure is simple :

testifying to the great benefit iny m<>tl ec

January with bronchitis and congestion of

do her no good, but recommeuded her to try
MRS. BEDLOW.
(From a photograph.)
Soott’s Emulsion. At this time the sufferer
was at her lowest ebb, but after taking

disappeared, and persevering with the

mother, after being confined to her bed for

Mr. Bedlow writes that “ a small bottle of
Scott’s Emulsion caused the asthmatical
cough to disappear ” —a course of Scott’s
Emulsion completely eradicated the asthma.
It is ever the same old story, in troubles of
the throat and lungs when Scott’s Emulsion
is used: First relief, then cure.
Another point, too, which is daily made in
the correspondence which reaches us : “ the
doctor said the usual remedies would do her
no good, but recommended Scott’s Emul-
sion.” The doctor did not recommend
common cod-liver oil, the doctor did not
recommend substitutes for or imitations
of Soott’s Emulsion, they “ would do her
no good ” —what he did recommend was
Soott’s Emulsion, and only that.
This doctor’s experience is the same as
that of countless physicians ; they can rely
on Scott’s Emulsion; with any other thing
the result is still in doubt. There is, of
course, a solid scientific reason for this—the
scientific blending by our especial process of
pure cod-liver oil with hypophosphites of
lime and soda.
A distinguished physician who carried out
a series of comparative tests finds : “ Scott’s
Emulsion is three times as efficacious as
the same quantity of common cod-liver oil.”'
If you reflect a moment on these facts you

At Poverty Point.
“ P.N.” : Sandow would be far more impressive, not to
say pleasing, if he kept well within the limits of his own
strength (for even his horse-power has limits) and wasn’t
constantly trying to break his own records. Take any
feat-artist, acrobat or equilibrist, say Caicedo or Cin-
quevalli; they impress not merely because of what they
accomplish, but because of all the unattempted feats
subtly suggested to the mind that they could do if they
liked. The apparently effortless performances of Cin-
quevalli leave the beholder with the idea of great reserves
of power. Sandow is a magnificent animal, but the art
that conceals art is not his. The unpleasing physical
signs of over-exertion—the hurried, reckless way in
which he flings his furniturea way— are unconscious con-
fessions that he has got to the end of his tether, that he
has done all he dared attempt, and beyond would be
failure. There is no splendid bravado challenge to the
imagination suggesting worlds yet possible to conquer.
No wonder Mrs. Sandow stays away from her husband’s
performances “ for fear of something happening.”
“Boseo” : Strong-man-feats are often mere tricks. The
coin and chain-breaking' fakes are now pretty well sized
up ; and, as for the card-pack tearing feat, packs of cards
are often baked in an oven until they are extremely dry
and brittle, care being taken to avoid any tell-tale dis-
coloration. A pack so treated can easily be torn in
halves, and the halves paired and again torn. In the rare
event of a challenge as to the condition of the cards
it is perfectly easy to substitute unprepared cards.
Sandow’s rending of three conjoined packs may be
genuine, but anyone with fairly strong hands can, with a
little practice, duplicate it by trickery, thus : The cards
are held in the left hand, on the outside of the left knee,
the right side being turned to the spectators. As the
right hand grips the lower end of the cards the left
thumb slightly spreads them out (under cover of the
right hand). The tearing is then easy, as instead of
having a block of cards to deal with you" have only a fan.
The performer disguises the ease of the thing by pretend-
ing to violent effort, and there is a crash of music as the
hands are pulled apart. When the conjuror Hermann
was challenged by the late Czar of Russia to duplicate
ftisfeat of tearing a pack asunder, Hermann took one of
the halves and, by the trick described, smilingly tore
that in halves.
Melba, like all other visiting great singers, is being
rushed by importunate young persons who want to sub-
mit their voices to her. In most cases she refuses, of
course, but one or two favored nightingales, with the
influence of Mme. Wiedermann and other musical per-
sonages at their backs, have been given a chance of
showing off. The average singing aspirant sets out with
the idea that she has only to be heard by Melba or some-
body else to ensure being taken up with great enthusiasm
and exhibited to the world as a vocal marvel, whereas
the solemn truth is that there are not so many niches at
the top of the tower of fame that the people already there
should want to assist their fellows up. When the sweet-
voiced Dolores arrives in any Australian city she is imme-
diately besieged by so many would-be singing stars that
she has to set aside a day for hearing them all. Yet
Dolores has no use whatever for them, as she wants no
“support ” at her recitals, and the utmost she can do is
to whisper “ Very nice,” with perhaps a word or two of
encouragement, in the ears of ambitious girldom.
Two or three of the little weeklies claiming to have a
Melbourne circulation gave away the fact that they didn’t
get a ticket for the Melba concerts by not publishing any
notices of them. The deadly silence was expected to
blight the tail end of the Melba season, but it merely
served to advertise Musgrove’s managerial ideas re “ the
courtesies usually extended to the Press.” The
“ courtesy ” in this case was worth a solid guinea a time,
whereas the notices obtainable from small weekly papers
would have been worth practically nix. Therefore they
got no tickets.
Tickets for Melba’s concerts did not boom in Sydnej'
One syndicate bought 160 tickets at £1 Is. each, and only
managed to dispose of about 40 at a premium. The
balance were gladly sold at face value. Tickets “ along-
side the Governor’s box ” changed hands at a guinea the
day previous to the first concert, though as much as £3
3s. had been glibly asked for them two weeks previously.
Melba practically compels her flautist to play the
obligato for “ Sweet Bird” in perfect harmony with her
voice. When they come to the note-and-note passages,
where one mistake by either of them would spoil the
work, Melba draws closer to the music-stand and domi-
nates the whole art and soul of the man who is
blowing his anxiety into the “ reed ” instrument, which,
by the way, seems to be made of silver. It is a study in
magnetism. He must play the singer’s note at the exact
instant. If she were to strike the wrong one, that pallid
accompanist would commit the same error, in unison
with the ruling power.
Musgrove gave a most diplomatic denial to the state-
ment that Melba will make a few Australian appearances
in opera. Read between the lines G.M.’s letter, conclud-
ing with a reference to “ the difficulties attendant on
Madame’s appearance in grand opera” is practically a
hint that only a warm demand for seats is needed to
remove those “ difficulties.” ’Tis the voice of the manager
crying, “ Ask and ye shall receive.”
“ S.S.” : “ Melb.” in a recent P. Point par. laments.that
Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis did not re-enact her Hamlet, which
might “be revived with advantage.” Mrs. G. B. W. L.
has just celebrated her professional jubilee, and it is a
case of weight for age, the lady being decidedly massive.
To invite an actress into Hamlet’s garments under such
circs, is to encourage her to make a comic spectacle of
herself. I have seen Mrs. Lewis do much pleasant work
on the Melbourne stage. I have also seen her attempt at
Hamlet, and my flesh still aches with sympathy for the
misguided actress when I think of it. The man who can
find delight in the spectacle of a stout, middle-aged lady
playing Hamlet is a strange bird. Seriously now,
wouldn’t “ Melb.” like to see Wilson Barrett’s graceful
and girlish performance of Juliet ?
Sandow took hints from Paul Cinquevalli in the little
bits of “ frill-work” with which he embellishes his turn.
But he hasn’t the juggler’s natural grace of gesture and
nonchalant style, and whereas Cinque, is essentially
manly in his decorative arts the big fellow’s mincing
1 stage mannerisms are feminine. “ Call him a Samson /”
! said a ruffled Melbourne johnnie, whose girl was gazing
1 fondly at the show-man’s gleaming muscle, “ He’s putting
!on the airs ©f Delilah.” Sandow’s way of limping off
suggests that he is somewhat muscle-bound in one leg.
Dr. O’Hara’s son has accepted an engagement to
tour Australia in the Black Art line. The erstwhile
Melb. medical student appeared originally at one
of Rickards’ theatres and then went to London. He made
a few appearances at the ’Alls, but principally confined
himself to acting as assistant to other magicians with a
view to picking up fresh wrinkles.
Josephine Stanton, the nice, blonde Amurrican actress
n Musgrove’s company, makes the novel confession to
having had quite a placid, non-sensational stage career.
She was brought up in the city that means Brotherly
Love, and has a recollection of sticking a pin into her
elder sister when that relative disapproved of Josephine’s
craving for stage honors. When the relative still dis-
approved, Josephine confesses to having arisen and
smacked the objecting one. Josephine has travelled all
over America, but found the “ jumps ” rather long. By
jurnfis she means journeys. Honolulu is a pleasant
memory of a farewell-night, when, playing lead in
“ Olivette,” she had flowers pelted at her till, at the
' close, she counted 85 bouquets. Miss Stanton’s pet
animal is the dog, but she likes her husband too, because
when she wants to read bloodthirsty novels, and doesn’t
want anyone to know it, she borrows them on the plea
that her husband never cares for anything else. This
misrepresented individual in reality loathes the gory
variety of literature.
“T.D.”: Training it Melbournewards the day before
Melba’s first concert, I overheard two Toorakiennes of
great magnificence agree that if the cantatrice secured
the homage of Yarra city audiences “ she ought to pass
muster anywhere.” That is to say, a woman who has
topped the tree in critical London, Paris, St. Petersburg,
and New York, not to speak of Milan, is liable to bite the
dust in the Marshall Hall citj - . It would be quite correct,
of course, if Melbourne were competent to judge. Spite
of all its boasting on the score of its musical culture, and
; the fact that Collins-street swarms with nice-looking
girls carrying copies of Bach’s “Inventions” and the
Marchesi singing-book under their arms, there is probably
less real music to the square yard in Melb. than in any
other Australasian big city. I have listened to villainous
programmes, given by musical neophytes and derelicts to
so-called critical audiences, which same programmes
consist of one-half Tosti’s “ Good-bye ” and a dreadful
ballad called “ Doreen,” and the other half Highland
Fling and a lecture by somebody with Scotland in his
whiskers. That kind of concert is always crowded, but
Dolores and Gerardy and many another great musician
who didn’t hail from the Presbyterian Ladies’ College had
to leave with depleted pockets.
In wiping out the free pass system, Vic.
Minister Bent was scrupulously careful not to
tread on the corns of the rich newspaper proprie-
taries by doing away with the rule which permits
of reporters travelling at half-fare. There is no
earthly reason why pressmen travelling in the
interests of purely commercial concerns like the
Argus and Age should be able to do so at halt
the rates paid by other people, and it must be
apparent to Bent, if he is really solicitous for
public economy, that perhaps £3OOO or £4OOO
yearly can be saved by making the paper bosses
pay up like other people.

Hagar: A Problem Without
Solution.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
iTis in the backyardsof respectability that many of
the disreputable affairs of life occur. The nature of
what we call society is to put a grand front before
a back of very unpresentable pretensions. So
nobody could complain of Reginald Ascher’s social
propriety, and, being a vigorous self-deceiver, he
hardly realised the hypocrisy of his life. He was
happily married, his business was carried on in a
reputable way and returned profits, and his in-
clination for poetry was excusable.
But one day came discovery. His wife learnt
that there was another woman—a woman, too,
who had possibly held sway during a longer reign
than herself. Discovery did not come all in a day,
however; it had been approaching for many
weeks under the kind leadership of her dearest
friends, and it found her to some extent prepared.
After a fit of hysterical unreasonableness she made
her resolution and dismissed her friends, and
Ascher learnt that his denials could not cover the
mischief.
His wife, however, was what might be known
as a sensible woman. She never would have,
under any circumstances, placed herself in the
position of the other woman, and therefore blamed
the woman solely. She told herself, with a glow
of passionate feeling, that she would not alienate
her husband, but he must refrain, and join with
her in defying the scandal raised.
But she hardly realised the man’s position and
his unwillingness. The bareness of the respect-
able lack of respectability in his life was, for the
first time, before him ; and he knew that people
would class him with the casual free-lover, while,
in his heart, he felt he loved both women.
And the other woman, the first, had in reality
the greatest claim on his care. He recalled, with
a glow of remorse, how she, a girl at the time, had
attracted hini with the pale expressiveness of her
face, and the hungry, craving look of her eyes. She
was a girl-clerk, passionately revolting against the
drudgery of her life, friendless and alone, and he
had stolen into her life, been her only friend,
winning in exchange for his kindness the full rich-
ness of her love. He remembered she had cast
herself upon him, and he had debated strongly in
his heart about her, because of his wish to live
rightly ; but in his youthful repugnance for ties
he had chosen the easier way. She, in the confi-
dence of her passion, had never questioned or
doubted ; he was less abandoned, more cautious ;
but there had been days of delight when she
revelled in the things he gave her, when the
hungry, weary look began to give way to a glowing
passion of gratitude.
Remembering all this, the time when his heart
became divided was a time of pain. That recol-
lection was plain as the others, the striving to put
the new image away from him, the wish to be as
just as his nature allowed, struggling against incli-
nation. But the freedom from the bonds of
marriage was like a spirit on the side of the
newer face, and the newer face won.
Even when the new love had grown on him, and
was at its height, a tenderness that was not the
tenderness of regret, still tied him to the older
one, and his double life began. He had got into
the fashion of smoothing over the duplicity of his
conduct by assuring himself it was all for the sake
of one who had cast herself upon his chivalry.
Now, suddenly, he became aware that he loved
her—so well, that choosing between his wife and
her, his heart was divided as a child’s between its
mother and fathen He could not explain all the
circumstances to his wife—nor any. She had all
the prosaic virtues, and he loved her; she had not
the passionate abandon—which is not regarded
as a virtue except by its recipient—of her rival; and
she could not believe in anyone who disregarded
marriage in the fulfilment of love. Moreover, she
had been Wronged bitterly ; she had the right to
demand respectability of her husband. The fact
dawned upon him that the position he had been
run into was altogether unreasonable; and yet
to alter it meant the spoiling of someone’s life.
“ One way or the other, it must end,” insisted his
wife; “ either you must give up this woman, or I
cannot live with you any longer.”
“It would seem unreasonable to you,” he said,
“if I were to attempt to explain to you. lam
willing to give up the girl; but I must make her
some allowance. ”
“Make her what allowance you please,” she
responded, “as long as you never see her again.”
That was what she would feel. If she never saw
him again the allowance would be nothing to her;
she would be deserted, lonely and miserable, and
pine away. But his wife was indignantly inexor-
able; he must either yield or she would leave
him.
And he could not give up his wife! No, he
could not do that ! After all, if his wife left him,
if she sued for a divorce, what he was trying to
keep from the other woman would be revealed to
her with a harder face than ever, and the whole
misery of the position would be shown to the
world. There was only one way.
“Inez,” he pleaded ; “it may seem impossible
to you to believe that I have loved you truly all
this time; it is impossible to explain it. I will do
what you say. God forgive me for the wrong I
have done, but I can’t give you up.”
She was cold, but with a coldness that would
thaw ; in her heart there was the resolution regis-
tered not to lose him. He was in a fever to get
the whole affair cleared-up and its misery over.
Driving in a cab to the place he was to visit for
the last time, the misery grew upon him. He saw
a white face, blank and terrible with despair;
grief bowing down a heart that had never discip-
lined itself to bear anything. The picture was
horrible ; his wife, in her well-trained pride, could
bear anything with a certain grace ; to the other,
grief was utter abandonment.
There came an instant resolution to get the
interview over, and hurry away to drown in some
way the vision of her bitterness.
When he arrived at his destination she came
rushing to meet him. For a moment he held her
off, noticed her look of blank consternation and
yielded. She was a storm of passion it was
impassible to resist.
“You have come,” she said, passionately.
“ What has kept you ? Three days, and you have
not been near me ! How can I live without you
so long ! ”
She was crying, at the ominous whiteness of his
face, as much as at the memory of her loneliness.
How could he give the hard news he had to give
to her? Instantaneously he resolved a postpone-
ment.
Somehow the pleasure of seeing her happy
hardened his heart to the immediate peril of his
Position. What were the bonds and censures of
ociety to him ? She was a woman whose passion
was a thing ethereal, unique, apart from the com-
monness of the world; he was a man —when with
her, a poet whose heart was flooded with poetry.
His impulse asserted itself; his will grew bolder ;
he would not give her up for all the demands of
social propriety.
“ Dearest,” he whispered to her, “ when you
see me pale and doubtful as I have been, throw
your arms round me as you have done to-night.
I remember how I swore that you should never
suffer for your love for me—and you shall not.”
The position was the same as before; the
problem unsolved, insoluble ; and yet, as it stood,
impossible.
His wife had thought the black chapter of his
life was closed. In time she had resolved she
would forget; her love for him was too strong for
her to make a resolution to absolutely blot him
out. Passionately she rebelled at her own decrees
for leaving him. Even when the realisation came
that her threat had been ineffectual, it was im-
possible at once, with one act, to sever herself
from him. Yet, believing he loved her, she
resolved that showing coldness would drive him to
do what more decisive threats had failed to effect.
Ascher suddenly felt her affection leaving him,
and a new catastrophe yawned for him. He
would not notice it, but still it terrified him. In
those days there was no enjoyment in his life.
Sometimes in the agony of the impending crisis
he half resolved to do as she wished, but could
not.
“Inez,” he said, one day, passionately inter-
cepting her as she was leaving ; “ this life must
end ; you are brutally cold to me.”
“ Can you wonder ? ” she answered. “You ! ”
“What you want me to do,” he continued,
furiously, “is to break a woman’s heart ; because
I will not do it, you want to make my life miser-
able. Will you be satisfied if I swear to you,
though I will see her occasionally so as to keep
her from being miserable, I will be faithful to
you ? ”
“No,” she answered, curtly.
“ You do not trust me, I suppose ? ” pursued
Ascher.
“No,” answered his wife ; “you do not seem
to care about my being miserable,” she flashed,
iealously.
Then she turned and left him ; and he, going
out with a fixed determination, repeated, half-
mechanically, “ This life must end.”
For the coldness of his wife, and the warmth of
the other woman, had driven him to his resolu-
tion ; but there was a sense of dull loss at his
heart. He was a miser, who had jettisoned one-
half of his wealth for the keeping of the other,
and was troubled with doubts.
Inez woke to the realisation when, in two days,
he had not returned. In a moment all the
demands of her pride vanished; she could have
blinded herself to his doings, only to have him by
her. Now he was lost for ever.
But when that first feeling went, a strange
change seemed to creep into her heart, a sort of
carelessness about everything. Her nature had
not been openly passionate, but its reserve had
been strained to high tension; now reserve and
passion seemed both gone, and instead there was
a strange impersonal obliviousness to feeling, in
which only one thing mattered —the external
world.
He was at his office, as usual, when she came
upon him, and he was annoyed to see her.
“Reginald,” she said, quite calmly, “have you
resolved not to return home ? ”
“You forced me to it,” he stammered.
“I want you to return; I will act the part of
wife as well as I can, and we need not lose our
social standing.”
“You want me to return?” he said, eagerly.
“But there are no considerations, no conditions?”
“No,” she replied ; “do as you like ; all I want
you to do is to return home, to maintain the
semblance of respectability. Maintain our social
position—that’s what I want. I have nothing
but my social position to live for.”
“I will come,” he said, watching her with
apprehensive eyes. “ And when I have got you
to understand everything, you may perhaps
realise .”
“No,” she answered with a callous laugh.
“ There is no necessity for it; Ido not desire it.”
HAMER.
Pelorus Jack and Big Ben.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The visitor to Maoriland hasn’t done the Land of
the Kiwi thoroughly unless he has seen Pelorus
Jack.
Pelorus Jack is the pet of Maoriland. Going
from Picton to the West Coast, we passed the
gentleman’s home about three o’clock in the
morning and he wasn’t visible, but, returning, it
was a bright day, and the French Pass looked
nicer than a picture. This pass is about the width
of a fairly broad river; it connects Tasman Bay
with Pelorus Sound, and separates D’Urville
Island from the mainland. The tide boils through
like a flood, and as you swing along you can tell
the color of the lighthouse-keeper’s daughter’s
eyes, and the passengers chaff her. Further on a
fishing-boat fastens-on for stores, and when it is
thrown off Pelorus Jack flirts his tail above water.
Pelorus Jack is a fish, his tail shaped like a five-
days’-old moon, and his body the guise of an old-
fashioned soda-water bottle. The dorsal fin is
like that of a porpoise, but the head is bluff like
a killer’s, and he has a blow-hole. His color is
a grey-nurse shark’s, and he measures about 12ft.
He accompanies every steamer for about 15
minutes, playing about the nose of the vessel like
a porpoise ; the porpoises clear out as soon as he
appears, and return as soon as he leaves. They
evidently fear him. He has been greeting vessels
for the past 15 years, and is so esteemed that he is
protected by a special Act of Parliament.
There is something pathetic about this solitary
fish and his friendship for ships. Maybe his mate
was killed from a ship passing that way, and
he is waiting for her. return. Who knows ? And
the affection is not one-sided. Those sailor-men
show their weak side when you become interested
in their pet. One old salt told me of a fool-
traveller who came to sea with a gun. He was
like most men —ready to kill anything that can’t
talk ; so he took a shot at poor Pelorus Jack.
The captain put him ashore at Pioton.
This reminded me of another pet fish and
another fool-traveller with a gun, and I told him
about Big Ben of Kiama, but the sailorman’s eyes
looked stony, and he didn’t_ seem to like any
community but Maoriland having a fish for a pet,
But the people of Kiama never weary in telling
you about Big Ben. These people live on ghosts
and milk and blue-metal, and they have a Blow*
Hole where the sea climbs up as far as it ean and
then falls limply baok, like the spray from a soda*
water bottle. In the days of Big Ben their pas*
time was to go up to the Blow-Hole and feed their
pet shark with a dead horse,
Ben was the largest shark- along the coast, and,
knowing how partial the Kiamaites were to him
he lived mostly off the town. The fool-traveller
with the gun who tried to shoot Kiama’s Big Ben
was prosecuted for shooting within the precincts
of the town and heavily fined.
Nobody seems to know what became of Big Ben,
but Kiama folks have their theories, One man
told me that the fool-traveller hurt his feelings if
he didn’t hurt him somewhere else, and that the
shark abandoned Kiama for ever. Another
alleged that a sacrilegious quarryman went down
to the Blow-llole and gave the shark a hunk of
beef in which was a plug of dynamite with a slow
match attached. It seems that this quarryman
had a grudge against Kiama and had sworn to be re-
venged, and at certain times when lie had wanted
to hurt the feelings of Kiama people he had been
known to damn Big Ben.
You hear strange stories at Kiama; fearsome
monsters lurk in the deep there. One Saturday
afternoon, a boat capsized off the Blow-llole, and
two men were drowned JThab night, men went
out to recover the bodies, and they came back and
told how they found a corpse caught in the hal-
liards, and, as they came close, they saw a sea-
monster move off. It was bright moonlight, and
they could see it swimming about, looking at them
with enormous, baleful eyes, that expressed almost
human intelligence, and then it disappeared. A
fisherman told me that a similar monster to this
frightened him from deep-sea fishings He went
to work in the quarries.
They seem to have an intimate knowledge of
sharks in Kiama. One man said to me, “When
you hook a shark, haul in as fast as you can. A
shark is no fool. When he feels he is hooked, he
rushes forward to bite the line.” Another man
said “ The shark is the most passionate lover in
the world. When he makes love, he tear* his
sweetheart frightfully with his teeth.”
ROBERT BROTHERS.


THE KIDNEYS:
WHERE THEY ARE, AND WHAT THEY DO.
As the kidneys have more work to
do than any organ of the body, it is
important to know where they are,
what they are, and what they do.
The picture shows where the kid-
neys are—in the small of the back.
That is why the back aches when
there is kidney trouble.
What are the kidneys?—They are
two bean-shaped organs, made up of
thousands of small cells and fine mem-
branes. They are the most delicate
organs of the body. When the kid-
neys are weak, the least cold or chill
affects their action.
What the kidneys do.—They filter
the blood—every drop of our blood
goes to the kidneys to be filtered and
purified. When they are well the
kidneys take out nearly five hundred
grains of impure matter every twenty-
four hours.
When the kidneys are sick every
organ goes wrong, because sick kid-
neys cannot filter the blood properly.
Poisons and impurities are left in,
and these cause headaches, backaches,
limb aches, fluttering of the heart,
dropsy, and rheumatism. There will
be a too great or too little flow of the
urine, with gravel or sediment; you
will feel always tired, done up, ner-
vous, and restless.
Doan’s backache Kidney Pills are
the great kidney and bladder cure.
They go to the root of the whole
trouble, and greatly relieve, heal, and
regulate the kidneys. They freshen
you up, because they rid the body of
its accumulated impurities through the kidneys.
To one with kidney or bladder trouble, whose kidneys and whole system
are clogged with impurities, there can be no relief equal to that afforded
by this great kidney medicine.
